War of Hearts

by sapphix_ssana

Summary

“I did not enter this marriage under grounds of love or other frivolous feelings,” Rio said, voice steady. “It was an act of duty. Nothing more.”

Agatha tilted her head, folding her arms.
“You paid quite a high price for ‘nothing more’, Your Majesty.”

Rio’s jaw twitched. Her eyes sharpened into something dangerous. Controlled but cracking.

Agatha felt it. The shift, the restraint, the want.
She wanted to break it.
She wanted to own it.

OR

With the king dead and the kingdom on the brink, Princess Agatha is placed on the auction block — a pawn to be bartered for power, her hand sold to the highest bidder.

She expects a life of misery. Obedience. Silence.

She does not expect Rio Vidal. The cold, revered queen of a neighbouring realm. A warrior cloaked in shadows and secrets. A woman who seems determined to keep her at arm’s length.

Their marriage is a weapon. A wall. A vow.

But no walls are strong enough to stop what is destined to grow between them.

Chapter 1: The Price of a Throne

The temple air was cold. The kind of cold that seeped into one’s bones and coaxed hairs on the back of the neck to rise, despite the last of the summer sun still shining outside. 

Incense drifted through the chamber, thick and cloying. It failed to mask the rot beneath the roses.

Agatha tried to sit still. Truly she did. Even as her corset pinched and the fabric of her dress itched like nettles against her skin. The veil scratched against her cheek with every small movement, hot breath clouding the sheer fabric. A black shroud to match the mood. To mask a face that must not reveal too much.

Her mother had insisted. As if the veil might keep her from speaking out of turn. From revealing too much of herself — or perhaps, from revealing the half healed bruise on her left cheek.

That was what one was supposed to do at their father’s funeral, after all. At least someone of her status. So her mother had reminded her. 

There was no room for frivolous tears, not where her mother was concerned. Such displays were reserved for common folk. 

Would she have cried though, if that cold, judgmental gaze hadn’t been fixed so sharply on her? She wasn’t sure.

Perhaps, if she had truly been a common girl, and her father a common man, they might have shared a common bond. Then, perhaps, she could have wept freely at his death.

But she was not a common girl. And her father had been far from a common man. He had been a king and had possessed all the qualities of one. Stern, indifferent and conceited. 

She had a few memories, perhaps distorted by time or longing. Of her father being kind. Gentle, even. She mourned that time, at least. Before she came of age and he no longer looked at her the same. As though she was no longer his precious  little princess, but something else. Something that needed to be feared. 

“Would you quit that. Sit up straight.” 

A sharp hiss pulled her from her thoughts. She blinked and looked down, realising she had once again been picking at the skin around her nails. Thankfully, she hadn’t drawn blood this time. Her mother hated when she did that. It was quite “unbefitting” apparently. 

She folded her arms neatly on top of her lap and tried to straighten her posture further. And if her thumb dug into the flesh of her palm for the rest of the ceremony — well, that was her business. 

As the ceremony drew to a close, Agatha found herself growing restless. It was hard to remain expressionless and proper while listening to such a glowing description of her father that was so far removed from her own recollection.

 “A brave and honourable king, who served each and every one of his subjects with pride and wisdom.” 

She wanted to agree. She truly did. But all she could recall was as a man ruled by fear and mistrust. He had not been accepting and celebratory to all, as they claimed. More… tolerable. At best.

She nearly sighed aloud when the High Attendant finally concluded the rites, his voice fading as the body was carried down into the crypt. A soft chant rose behind the procession, but Agatha barely heard it. She slipped away before her mother could catch her eye.

The walk from the temple to the river was short, familiar. She followed the path in silence, descending the stone steps until she stood beneath the bare-limbed branches of an old tree, leaning lightly against its trunk. The bark scratched at her shoulder through the fabric of her dress — a small, grounding discomfort.

From here, she could still hear the distant murmur of voices—nobles, no doubt, offering their performative condolences to the Queen. Their sympathy, like their loyalty, lasted only as long as it was politically convenient.

She turned her gaze to the river.

Its surface gleamed with dying sunlight, darkening at the edges. The current was slow today, contemplative.

She had always found a strange kind of comfort in watching its waters.

The way the ripples stretched and folded, how the light broke against the surface, how the current seemed to shift its path for no one.

Sometimes, she wondered what it was the river knew.

Why it bent where it bent.

Why it had carried her here, of all places.

And when, if,  it would carry her somewhere else.

Somewhere farther.

Somewhere freer.

She wasn’t sure how long she stood there before the crunch of boots on gravel broke the silence behind her.

“Your Highness.”

She turned. A young guard stood a few paces away, bowing stiffly.

“Your mother—the Queen, requests your presence. She said it is urgent.”

Agatha arched an eyebrow. “Urgent? At this moment?”

“Aye,” the guard replied, eyes fixed somewhere over her shoulder. “She said it was a matter of great importance.”

Agatha tried to hold back an eye roll at that. “I see. And what, exactly, could be so important with her husband not yet cold?”

“I am only the messenger, my lady.”

“Of course you are.”

She didn’t move right away. Let the pause drag. Let her mother wait.

Then, finally, with a soft exhale, she pushed off the tree and began the slow walk back toward the castle.

“Agatha, there you are. What have I told you about slipping away?” Her mother ushered her into the council room with haste, annoyance hidden behind a carefully constructed smile.

“Sorry mother.” She responded plainly. Her response would not change her mother’s disproval. It never did. 

“Keep your apologies child. Sit. We have much to discuss.”

Unease unfurled low in her stomach. Something was afoot, and judging by the stares fixed upon her, it concerned her directly.

She hesitated, then obeyed, folding herself into the empty chair at her mother’s side. Her gaze swept the chamber, taking in the men of the council, grim faced and sharp eyed. She had never been summoned to sit with them before.

“Mother,” she asked carefully, “may I ask what this is about? Surely the council might have waited until Father’s body had grown comfortable in his crypt.”

“Hush, child. You speak so strangely sometimes.” Her mother’s hand settled on her shoulder. To onlookers it would appear to be an act of motherly comfort. Only Agatha could feel her nails digging into her flesh like talons. 

“Forgive her,” the Queen said smoothly, addressing the men. “It is the grief. She misses her dear father terribly.”

One of the advisors cleared his throat. His voice was formal, but his eyes lingered on Agatha with thinly veiled disdain.

“Your Highness, we regret the haste of this gathering. But with His Majesty’s passing, the kingdom stands… precarious. The borders are restless. Rivals circle like vultures. We cannot allow uncertainty to invite their strike.”

“Forgive me, but I am struggling to understand what my purpose here is today. I have never been invited to such councils.”

The man’s lips thinned. He did not answer her directly. Instead, he continued. “There is, of course, the matter of succession. You are the late King’s blood, but… the court cannot place the crown upon someone of your…abilities. It is far too… unpredictable. The people would not follow. Alone, you cannot rule.”

The words landed like stones. Agatha forced her face still, though her stomach roiled.

“Our solution,” he pressed on, “is a union. A strong alliance with another nation will provide protection, resources, and legitimacy. It is the swiftest way to secure our throne and quiet any dissent.”

Now the sickness rose higher, bile pressing sharp against her throat. There was no reason she need be present for a discussion of treaties. Unless—

Her mother’s voice cut the thought short. Honey sweet. Too sweet.

“What they mean, dearest, is that we shall conduct an auction. And the prize—” she smiled thinly, “shall be your hand in marriage.”

The words struck like a lash.

Her blood iced. Sweat pricked her skin. The chamber tilted, shrinking in around her.

She had in the space of mere minutes been reduced to a commodity. 

A pawn.

As though she was nothing more than a piece of meat to be bartered for a crown.

Her fingers curled tightly in her lap. She could feel the sting of her nails digging into her palm again, sharp enough to leave little half moons in the skin. She didn’t cry. She wouldn’t give them the satisfaction.

But her silence would not be surrender.

If asked, Rio would say that Lilia Caldera was her most trusted advisor. It had less to do with the woman’s once famed divination gifts and more to do with the fact that Rio trusted her—wholly, unquestionably. Perhaps more than any other living soul. 

So when Lilia stepped into her office speaking of visions, threads and shifting tides, Rio was only half listening . Her pen scratched absently across the margin of a requisition form, her thoughts elsewhere.

She trusted Lilia. Implicitly. If Lilia told her to move, she moved.

“I need you to do something,” Lilia said. “Something very important.”

“Of course,” Rio murmured, not looking up. “Name it.”

There was a pause. Then, with infuriating lightness. “I need you to enter a marriage auction.”

The pen stopped.

Rio lifted her head slowly.

“…Pardon?”

Lilia smiled — all serene wisdom, as if she hadn’t just attempted to shatter the known laws of Rio’s universe.

“For a young princess. The daughter of a fallen king. The courts of her kingdom have decided she cannot take the crown despite being the only heir. She’s an unseen , I hear. They deem her too unstable, too dangerous to lead. A marriage of political strength is the only thing that will allow her to keep even a sliver of power.”

Rio inhaled slowly, and carefully set her pen aside.

A girl. A kingdom. A crown that was hers by blood and denied by fear. She imagined the weight of a throne pressed into shoulders that weren’t ready, the grief of a father barely buried, the cold realisation that her life would never again be her own.

Rio knew that feeling far too well.

“How young?” she asked at last, eyes narrowing.

“She is of age,” Lilia replied. “Nineteen summers behind her.”

“That’s barely out of girlhood.” Rio frowned.

“If I remember correctly you were already ruling an entire queendom at that age.”

“Precisely,” Rio snapped. “And no child should have to grow up the way I did. And no young woman should be sold off before she’s even had a chance to live.”

Lilia didn’t flinch. “It is unfortunate. But consider what could happen if she falls into the hands of more… unfavourable bidders.”

That gave Rio pause.

The Brotherhood. Vassal lords with a taste for subjugation. Old men who saw wives as possessions.

She could imagine the kind of life this girl might be forced to endure under the wrong banner.

And though she told herself this wasn’t her problem, the thought of it curled something cold and sharp beneath her ribs.

“Lilia,” Rio said quietly, “I respect your judgment. And I do empathise with the girl. Truly. But I cannot be of help in this. I have no interest in marriage. Or consorts. And everything else that would come of such a public union. You know this.”

“This isn’t about affection,” Lilia said gently . “You do not need to love her. But we must consider the bigger picture. The princess’s kingdom holds trade routes we can’t afford to let fall into enemy hands. The Brotherhood has been pushing at your borders for seasons. With this marriage, you gain a diplomatic foothold in the East — and prevent a witch burning regime from claiming another throne. “

A pause.

“And you… could give her something none of them can. Safety. Dignity. Freedom.”

Rio clenched her jaw.

She wanted to say no. Gods, she wanted to. She wanted to laugh it off and leave Lilia with her riddles.

But she couldn’t.

Perhaps it was the strange pressure in the back of her mind, the tug in her marrow. Or perhaps it was something older — a memory she didn’t want to name.

A young girl. Alone. Grieving. Watching others barter for the power that should have been hers.

She knew what it meant to be used. To be underestimated.

And she knew what it cost to take that power back.

“…Fine,” she said at last, “I’ll enter this auction,” The word auction tasting like rot on her tongue “but I make no promises to win.”

Lilia bowed, satisfied. “As you wish your Majesty”

She left the room like a tide receding.

Rio remained seated, jaw tight, breath locked in her chest. The quill in her hand cracked with a soft, splintering sound. Ink spilling over her hand like black blood. 

She couldn’t recall when she had even picked it up again.

The word still sat in her mouth like ash.

Auction.

As if the girl were nothing but livestock, a contract written in blood and gold.

Rio didn’t want a bride. She wanted to break the table this offering was being served on.

 

 

Chapter 2: A Life For a Price

Chapter Summary

Agatha struggles to come to terms with shocking new information that will alter the course of her life.
Somewhere far away, Lady Death faces the weight of a choice she cannot take back.

Chapter Notes

Content Warning ‼️ This story will contain ongoing references to trauma, emotional abuse and mental health struggles (including passive suicidal ideation and self harm). These themes will appear throughout the story. Please read the tags carefully going forward and I will also add a CW before any chapter contains anything particularly triggering. If at any point you feel as though a tag hasn’t been covered properly please don’t hesitate to let me know.

Tw for this chapter includes emotional abuse, sexual objectification, passive suicidal ideation (non graphic), anxiety themes, references to self destructive behaviour that may be interpreted as self harm.

See the end of the chapter for more notes

A soft knock sounded on Agatha’s door, interrupting her relentless pacing. She knew immediately who it belonged to. 

Her mother never knocked and waited. She preferred to barge in, trailing disdain like perfume. Her father had never wandered near her chamber while he lived.  

No, this knock was familiar. She knew the rhythm like the lines on her palms.

Jen had been her closest friend since childhood — her only friend really. While the rest of the castle treated Agatha like a fragile secret to be contained, Jen treated her like a person. They’d spent countless nights in this very room whispering about books, gossip, and escape plans under candlelight. Jen was perhaps, the only person who truly knew her.

Agatha flung open the door and grabbed her wrist before pulling her inside, shutting the door firmly behind them.

“It took you long enough to get here.”

“I know, I’m sorry. I came as soon as I could, but my mother held me up — said she needed a hand in the infirmary.” Jen paused, studying her. “I’d ask if it’s true what I heard, but your face is answer enough.”

And what a sight she must have been. Her hair half undone, the silk hem of her nightgown creased and wrinkled, her eyes red from tears of anger and sleeplessness.

“They’re calling for an auction,” she muttered, resuming her pacing. “As though I’m livestock. Or a painting to be sold to the highest bidder.” 

Jen perched on the window seat, legs tucked under her dress, watching her friend circle the room like a storm of silk. “You’ll wear a hole in the floor if you keep pacing like that.”

“Good,” Agatha snapped. Maybe the castle will collapse and release me from this misery.”

“Hey,” Jen said softly, standing and placing her hands on Agatha’s shoulders. “It’s going to be alright.”

Agatha looked away, throat tight. “No it’s not. They wont even consider letting me rule. Not because I’m unfit. But because I was born with magic  they cannot control. They’re marrying me off to keep me contained. To keep the Unseen out of power.”

Jens tone gentled. “I know it’s wrong, but maybe it isn’t personal. It’s how things have always been—politics and all that.”

Agatha shook free of her grip, her voice rising. “That’s easy for you to say when you’re not the one they’re afraid of. You don’t know what it’s like to be looked down on, feared, hated for something you had no choice in. I never wanted any of this. All I ever wanted was to study, to learn, to travel… to live without shame. And now I’m to smile and curtsy for some stranger with a title and too many teeth.”

Jen’s expression softened. She hesitated, guilt flickering across her face before she stepped forward again, tentatively. “You’re right, it isn’t fair. It’s absurd that we’re still divided by something as meaningless as Seen and Unseen.” She placed a steadying hand on Agatha’s arm. “You’d have made an excellent queen.”

Agatha sniffed, wiping her eyes a little too forcefully.

“How do you know though, that they’ll have too many teeth?” Jen asked after a beat. 

“I can just feel it,” Agatha said with a wet laugh. “No man with perfect teeth would buy a wife at an auction.”

Jen tilted her head. “And how do you know it’ll be a man? Maybe the River might bless you and join you with someone you may actually like.”

Agatha scoffed. “As if my mother would ever let that happen. The River would have to freeze over before I’d be permitted to marry another woman. It will be a man. An old, smelly man.”

Her shoulders sagged. “I’m scared, Jen. What if it’s another cage? Another place where I’ll have to ask permission to breathe?”

Jen caught her hands, squeezing. “Then we’ll find a way out. If he’s awful, I’ll help you run. We’ll steal a horse, head east, live off honey and stolen apples. You can open a tiny school for rebellious girls and I’ll sell potions on the side.”

That earned a faint, shaky smile. “You’d last two days before falling for some beekeeper.”

“Only if they have an acceptable amount of teeth,” Jen said primly.

Agatha laughed for real this time, the sound startling in the quiet room. Then she sank onto the bed, gathering a fistful of her skirt. “It shouldn’t be funny. My life is being dismantled, and I can’t even stop it.”

Jen brushed a strand of hair from her face. “Then start planning how to build the next one. Maybe this marriage isn’t the end for you. Maybe it’s the start of something else.”

Agatha turned toward the window. Beyond the glass, the river cut a silver path through the valley—the same river she used to dream of following to the sea. “It doesn’t feel like a beginning.”

“Maybe not yet,” Jen murmured. “But it could be. You’re still Agatha Harkness. No amount of money can buy or tame that.”

For a long moment, neither spoke. The castle bells tolled somewhere below, counting down the hours till morning.

Agatha exhaled slowly, trembling. “Then let them bid,” she said at last. “But I swear, whoever wins will regret the price.”

The room was colder than the rest of the palace. 

Rio’s boots echoed softly across the obsidian floor as she entered, the sound swallowed by the vast, domed chamber. In the center sat a shallow pool — circular, rimmed with pale riverstone. Its surface shimmered faintly, water flowing in place, never still, as if breathing.

They called it The Mirror of the Source.

Anyone of importance kept one — monarchs, lords, and high council members alike. Each was carved from water drawn directly from the River of Life, bound with ancient spells that allowed the powerful to gather in body or in spirit across impossible distances.

Lilia stood at the edge of the pool, hands folded. “You don’t have to attend, you know. The council could send an emissary in your stead.”

Rio didn’t look at her. “If the council wishes to bind my house to another’s, I’d at least like to see the cost of the transaction.”

She approached the pool. A faint current reached toward her boots, as though sensing the magic in her veins. She ignored it and crouched, touching the water with her fingertips.

It was freezing. It always was.

She murmured the invocation, her voice low, controlled. Detached. The water rippled, glowing faintly green at her touch before clearing again. Around the chamber, runes etched into the stone flared to life.

When she opened her eyes again, she was elsewhere.

The river water beneath her feet became glass. Shadows of other monarchs and nobles flickered around the room — translucent figures standing over their own mirrors in distant lands. Voices overlapped — polite laughter, barbed courtesy, the thrum of politics disguised as civility  filling the space.

Rio folded her hands behind her back, expression unreadable. She didn’t want to be here. Curse Lilia for always managing to twist her arm. 

The conversations droned on — men congratulating each other on trade routes, trying to squeeze alliances out of one another. Unsurprisingly, the vast majority were much older. Men who could have fathered her, never mind the girl whose name was about to be auctioned.

The air shifted suddenly.

A projection flared to life in the center of the chamber. Stylised, gilded and insultingly romantic — depicting the girl whose hand was about to be auctioned. 

Agatha Harkness. 

Rio tilted her head slightly. So this is the girl. 

The Harkness line was old — much like her own. A much more…traditional kingdom though, by all accounts. The dévide between Seen and Unseen ran deep there. Something Rio herself had never truly understood. It was no surprise that they refused to let the girl inherit the throne. People tended to fear what they could not see. For what they could not see, they could not control. 

She examined the image . The artist had done the girl no favors. Too much rouge on the lips. Eyes downcast like a meek maiden. She looked… small. A convenient fiction for the men gathered here, who liked their prey compliant.

“Charming little thing,” one of the lords drawled. His voice echoed through the spectral chamber. “I hear she’s spirited. The kind that breaks nicely after a few months.”

Another voice laughed — low, sleazy. “Spirited or not, that’s a fine mouth she’s got.”

The muscles in Rio’s jaw twitched. For a moment, she almost felt the phantom weight of her sword at her hip, before she reminded herself this was only glass and water.

Besides, this was none of her concern. She was here only to maintain appearances, to make a modest bid, appease the council, and leave.

But the words grated.

The first bid came. Five thousand silvers. An insulting price for another life. 

Another followed. Then another.

The numbers grew — and with them, the sly comments.

“Surely a girl like that cannot be worth this much. “ 

“Do you think she’s even pure? women of her power are known to be…bewitching.”

Rio ground her teeth, the noise around her blurring into static. She lifted her chin and spoke for the first time, her voice even. 

“Five hundred thousand.”

Silence followed — a moment of stunned quiet before the chamber erupted in murmurs. 

Someone scoffed. “Lady Death must be bored, if she’s buying wives now.”

Rio said nothing. Her reflection in the river water stared back, eyes dark and steady.

“Eight hundred thousand,” another voice countered. 

Rio’s eyes flicked toward the sound. Lord Bohner. Of course. The man was a parasite, always crawling toward power he hadn’t earned. The thought of him marrying into that bloodline— of his slimy hands, meant to exploit on that girl, stirred something dark beneath her ribs.

“One million.” She countered. 

“Two,” Bohner snapped. His translucent figure turned toward her, bristling.

A few nervous laughs rippled around the circle.

Rio’s expression hadn’t changed. But her hand  — the one clasped behind her back — had curled into a fist.

“Three,” she said evenly.

“Four.”

“Five million,” Rio said, her voice like riverstone — smooth, unyielding. She paused, then with icy finality. “In crowns.”

The room stilled — not just at the number, but at the currency.

Rio let it hang in the air before adding. “And if anyone would like to challenge that…I can bring it up to ten.”

The chamber went silent. Even the river seemed to still.

No one countered. No one dared. 

A soft chime marked the close of the auction. 

For a long moment, Rio simply stood there, staring into the glassy water where the girl’s image had been. Her reflection rippled faintly — and for the briefest second, she thought she saw a flicker of something in her own eyes.

Not anger. Not duty.

Something older. Something alive.

The glass beneath her feet dissolved back into water.

Rio didn’t move. The air around her still hummed faintly with the echo of the ritual — the faint metallic tang of magic lingering in her throat. Then the vision broke entirely, and she was back in her own chamber, the riverlight dimming to a low, steady pulse in the pool.

Lilia was still there, waiting in silence. She didn’t need to ask who had won.

Rio straightened, brushing invisible dust from her sleeves. “Have the council draw up the necessary documentation. I’ve seen enough for one evening.”

Lilia hesitated. “You bid rather high.”

Rio’s gaze slid toward her. “Did I?”

“You did,” Lilia said gently. “Even for you.”

Rio’s mouth twitched — not quite a smile. “It’s not as if we can’t afford it. The treasury could fund ten such dowries and still have gold to pave the streets. Let them call it an act of generosity.”

Lilia’s brow furrowed. “And the girl? You do realize this means you actually have to marry her. She’ll be living here. With you. In close proximity.”

Rio’s jaw tightened slightly. “She’ll adapt.”

Lilia’s gaze softened. “She’s not the one I’m worried about.”

Rio’s eyes flicked up, cold and sharp. “Is this not what you wanted? Well, it’s done now. So please, let us carry on.”

She turned away, her tone final. “Dismiss the attendants. And tell the council not to disturb me until morning.”

Lilia bowed, the faintest sigh escaping her as she left. The door closed with a soft click, and silence reclaimed the room.

Rio lingered a moment longer by the pool. The surface had stilled completely now, clear as glass. She could almost see through it — to the source, to the current that bound them all.

A life for a price.

She had paid steeper costs for lesser causes.

This should’ve meant nothing. Another transaction. Another pawn on the board. And yet, her stomach turned.

She didn’t know what disgusted her more — the system that allowed it, or the fact that she’d played along.

It had been a week since her father’s funeral. A week since she was informed that her hand would be traded for the sake of the kingdom.

With each passing day, Agatha had secretly hoped they would call it off. That no one would bid. Who would want her, anyway? She was nothing special.

The knock came at dawn.

When the door opened, it wasn’t Jen or a maid — it was her mother.

That alone was enough to sour her stomach.

“Get up,” her mother said curtly, stepping inside without invitation. The morning light caught the edge of her jewels — far too many for a woman still pretending to mourn. “It’s done.”

Agatha sat up slowly, her heart already sinking. “What is?” Though a sick feeling had begun curling in her gut, she already knew.

“The auction,” her mother said, her tone brisk, satisfied. “The council received the final documentation late last night. You’ve been won.”

The words landed like a stone in her chest. 

“Won,” Agatha repeated, hollowly. “By whom?”

Her mother smiled — a smile that held no joy. “Lady Death herself.”

The name stole the air from the room. Agatha blinked, sure she’d misheard. “Who?”

“Lady Death,” her mother repeated, savoring the words like fine wine. “The infamous Queen of the Riverlands. Bid five million crowns for you — nearly offered ten by the sound of it. The council is ecstatic.”

Agatha stared. “Five million crowns? For me?”

Her mother’s tone turned almost gleeful. “I’ll admit I was… disappointed to hear it was a woman. But for that kind of coin?” She gave a dismissive shrug. “We’d be fools to protest. Her queendom is rich beyond reason. Mines, trade ports, farmland, river routes. And with her protection, no one will dare touch us. Our enemies will think twice before crossing House Harkness now.”

Agatha’s voice wavered. “You don’t sound particularly horrified by the idea of handing me over to a woman known for killing her enemies without blinking.”

Her mother shrugged, all feigned grace and real cruelty. “If she kills you, at least you’ll die useful. And if she doesn’t…” She gave a dry, pointed glance. “Perhaps you’ll finally learn to keep that mouth of yours shut.”

Agatha’s fingers dug into her palms. “You hate me that much?”

“I ought to have left you in the woods the moment you were born.” Her voice was steady, indifferent. “But look at you now. A good investment, in the end.”

The air seemed to crack around them.

It was suddenly all too much. Agatha could barely hear over the roar in her ears — her pulse, her breath, the sudden crash of everything crashing down at once.

Lady Death.

The most feared woman in the western realms. A monster of blood and stone. A name whispered like a curse.

And she was to marry her.

Her voice trembled. “Why would she even want me? She doesn’t know me. I’m no one. I have nothing.”

“Apparently, she disagrees,” her mother said, turning toward the door. “Be grateful. You’ll be well kept in her halls — assuming you manage to please her. I imagine her generosity only extends so far.”

Whispers she’d heard over the years returned in flashes. A queen who ruled alone, who had no court, no consort. Who could silence a room with a glance.

Who was said to have paid a price for her throne so steep, some said the River itself had turned from her. Or perhaps had claimed her altogether.

The idea of being married to someone like that, bound to her, trapped in her court, sent a wave of panic so sharp it made Agatha’s vision swim.

She pushed to her feet before her mother could say another word.

“Where are you going?”

Agatha didn’t answer. She was already halfway to the door.

“Agatha!”

Her mother’s voice followed her down the corridor, but Agatha was running — through the marble hallways, past startled guards, out through the open courtyard where frost still clung to the grass.

“Should we bring her back, my lady?” a guard asked as she vanished beyond the gate.

Her mother’s lip curled. “Let her go. She cannot outrun this. The deal is done.”

The cold air burned Agatha’s lungs by the time she reached the riverbank. Her feet were caked in mud, her nightgown clinging to her legs. She didn’t care.

The water gleamed silver beneath the rising sun — the same river that had always called to her when she felt trapped, whispering of escape, of freedom.

Now it only mocked her.

“Why would she do this?” she gasped, gripping the edge of the stones. “Why me? She doesn’t even know me. What does she want from me?”

Her voice cracked on the last word.

Agatha collapsed to her knees on the bank, her breath ragged. Her hands gripped the stones lining the edge. She leaned over the water, staring into her reflection. 

Pale lips. Blotchy cheeks. Wind tangled hair.

She clearly doesn’t know what she’s buying, Agatha thought bitterly.

She closed her eyes. Held her breath.

Not long. Just enough for the silence to settle in.

For the world to dim. For it to feel like something was in her control.

It was a habit she never spoke of. One she’d carried since childhood. When things got too loud inside her head, she held her breath until her body demanded to be heard again. Until it reminded her she was still here. Still herself. Even when everything else was not.

“Agatha!”

She gasped—half from the shout, half from the burn in her lungs. She turned sharply, blinking away dizziness.

Jen came into view, picking her way down the slope in boots far too nice for the mud. A thick wool cloak was clutched in her arms. Her eyes widened as she caught sight of Agatha kneeling in the frost dusted earth.

“I heard you ran out in nothing but your nightgown. Are you trying to freeze to death?”

Agatha didn’t answer. She just shook her head and looked away.

Jen crouched beside her and immediately began wrapping the cloak around her shoulders. “You’re freezing.”

Agatha’s voice was hoarse. “It doesn’t matter.”

“It matters to me,” Jen snapped, but gently. “You weren’t even wearing shoes.”

Agatha laughed weakly. “I didn’t plan on coming back.”

Jen paused. “Don’t say that.”

“I can’t—” Agatha’s chest hitched. “I can’t go there. You don’t understand what they say about her—she’s cruel, ruthless, I’ve heard she kills her own council members if they so much as defy her! What do you think she’ll do to me?”

Jen held her tighter. “You don’t know that. They say a lot of things about powerful women. Most of them aren’t true.”

Agatha’s voice was small. “And what if they are?”

Jen swallowed, her voice gentler now. “Then you’ll do what you’ve always done. You’ll survive.”

Agatha’s hands trembled in hers. “I don’t want to just survive.”

“I know.” Jen gave a faint, watery smile. “But maybe this isn’t a curse. Maybe it’s a door you didn’t see coming.”

She hesitated, then added with a teasing lilt, “Maybe you’ll even meet your soulmate while you’re over there. And run away together.”

Agatha snorted, the sound choked and half tearful. “Oh please. What are the chances of that ever happening? When’s the last time you actually met two soulmates, Jen?”

“You never know,” Jen continued, brushing a windblown strand of hair out of her face. “If anyone deserves one, it’s you, Agatha.”

Agatha exhaled shakily, letting her weight fall against Jen’s side. The warmth of the cloak, the quiet hush of the river, the steady rhythm of her best friend’s breathing — it was the first thing that had made her feel remotely safe all morning.

She didn’t believe it. Not really.

But for now, she let herself pretend.

Chapter 3: Fit for Display

Chapter Summary

Preparations are underway and both Agatha and Rio feel the pressure of expectations.

Chapter Notes

Cw‼️for chapter include

Emotional abuse and manipulation
Coercion and objectification
Depictions of humiliation and dehumanisation
Body autonomy violations / unwanted touching
Restricted eating
Psychological distress and dissociation

None of the above take place between the main pairing.

Safe reading and enjoy 💜💚

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Rio — The Road by the Rivers Bend 

The carriage rocked gently beneath her, each wheel turn muffled by the dense forest soil. Outside, the River of Life followed beside them. From where she sat it resembled a pale ribbon of silver light, threading its way through the trees. A silent companion. Familiar. Constant.

Unlike what lay ahead. 

Rio sat still, spine rigid against the cushioned backrest, gloved hands resting atop one another. She had not moved in over an hour. The silence inside the carriage suited her. No friends rode with her. No family. She was surrounded only by an entourage of her own choosing. Diplomats, scribes and silent eyed witnesses. Each travelling in separate compartments, with their own scrolls and opinions. None of them would speak to her unless summoned. 

Just as she preferred. 

She had left Lilia and Alice behind — officially so they could maintain order and oversee council affairs in her absence. But privately, it had been a calculated retreat. The fewer familiar faces she brought to this union, the less risk she ran of it feeling like anything more than it was. A deal. A duty.

She watched the river for a while longer, following the way it caught sunlight in flashes. At one bend, it narrowed — close enough that, had she been on horseback, she might have ridden beside it. Free. Quick. Breathing. Her fingers twitched toward the curtain’s edge. 

But the thought passed. 

She would not ride today.

She was not here to feel.

She was here to marry a stranger.

A diplomatic arrangement, nothing more.

She’d repeated it to herself again and again over the past month — while preparations had surged forward with rapt speed, while the weight of her choices settled heavier each night.

She was to marry a stranger. 

A woman she had never seen in person, whose voice she had never heard so much as a cadence of.

She knew nothing of the girl beyond what had been documented. Agatha Harkness, nineteen. Only daughter of House Harkness. Spirit witch. Unseen. Her soon to be wife.

The thought twisted something low in her gut. 

What was worse though, was that she couldn’t help but imagine how the girl felt. It was her after all that had been bartered like a prized mare. Her agency that had been signed away. Her birthright denied. 

However sick Rio felt about the arrangement, she knew in her marrow thaw it was worse for the woman on the other end of the bargain. 

And that knowledge — that guilt — had been slowly eating away at her.

Day after day.

Night after night.

Because Rio had once lived through something not so different. Not in name. Not in ceremony. But close enough that the shape of it still echoed in her bones.

She had clawed her way out of that fate.

And now, in carving peace for her people , she had helped drag someone else into it.

So now, as she sat here — en route to what would mark not only a new era in her life, but for two nations, she felt… hollow. As though the guilt had devoured everything inside and left only a quiet, efficient carcass behind.

She didn’t get to feel anything. Not when it was her hand that sealed the deal. 

The carriage tilted slightly as the road shifted upward, the forest beginning to thin. In the distance, sharp golden spires pierced the skyline like spears. The Harkness kingdom. Pale stone towers. Silk banners. Glittering illusions dressed in performative respectability.

It didn’t feel welcoming.

Not dangerous, exactly — but sharpened. All polish and spectacle. A kingdom that wore its refinement like armor. She could already feel the eyes that would greet her there. Reverent, watchful. Rapacious. 

She did not slow her breathing.

She did not look away.

A voice called from the front — the courier riding ahead.

“Your Majesty, the gates are within sight.”

Rio didn’t answer. She adjusted the cuffs of her dark riding jacket. It was formal enough for travel, her crest stamped in silver at the shoulder — a river stone bound by throned vines. She let her hand rest near the dagger strapped to her thigh, hidden beneath the folds of her coat. Ceremonial, of course. Everything about this was.

But still. She liked the weight of it.

Agatha — The Dressmaker’s Room

The pins kept catching her skin.

Not enough to bleed. Just enough to sting.

She stood on the low platform at the center of the room, arms half raised, gown half sewn, while four women fluttered around her like moths to a flame she never asked to be lit. Fabric rustled. Scissors clicked. Fingers tugged at laces and thread and pearls until her shoulders burned.

Agatha stared at the far wall, blinking slowly, letting the blur take over.

Someone adjusted the bodice again — too tight, always too tight. A voice tsked low behind her.

“She’s breathing wrong again.”

She wasn’t.

She was just breathing.

Too much, apparently.

Her mother sat in the corner, supervising with all the warmth of a frozen statue. She didn’t say much, just nodded sharply when a seamstress asked something, or made quiet suggestions like “we’ll need to cinch the waist further” and “the shoulders must be corrected — it’s looking rather hunched.”

Agatha’s legs ached. Her stomach growled softly, and one of the women made a face.

“What did she eat this morning?”

“Just fruit,” one replied. “And watered tea.”

“Make sure she avoids anything heavier until the ceremony,” her mother cut in. “The dress won’t forgive it.”

The dress. As if it were sacred. As if she were not.

Agatha didn’t respond. She hadn’t responded in nearly an hour.

Not since she had made one comment too many, and her mother had squeezed her cheeks together and hissed at her to shut her mouth. 

Hard enough for tears to spring up. 

Not hard enough to bruise. 

Never hard enough to bruise — at least not since the countdown to the wedding had started and she couldn’t risk “damaging the prize”.

Her fingertips flexed slightly, then curled into fists at her sides.

When she was small, she used to pretend her body was a shell. That she could step out of it if she needed to — leave it behind, weightless and invisible. She hadn’t done that in years.

She found herself doing it now.

They pulled at her again. Another adjustment. Another pin. Another murmur about her posture, her hips, her expression.

“Try to soften your mouth. No one likes a bitter bride.”

“Lift your chin. Not so much. You dont want to look as if you’re challenging anyone.”

“Try not to squint like that at the alter. You look like you’re glaring.”

“Don’t fidget.”

“Don’t slouch.”

Don’t exist, she thought, and that made her laugh — not aloud, just inside. Quiet and bitter. Like everything else about her.

She didn’t notice she was holding her breath until her vision narrowed.

She let the breath go slowly. Shallow. Silent.

No one noticed.

No one ever did.

A knock at the door pulled her out of her thoughts. A young maid entered a curtseyed low, eyes downcast. 

“What is it, girl? Speak.” Her mother snapped impatiently.

“I’m sorry to disturb, Your Grace, I was sent to inform you Her Majesty Queen Vidal and her entourage have arrived. They await your presence.” The girl rushed out.

“Why didn’t you say that earlier. Oh,I must go greet them. How do I look.” 

“Beautiful as always your Grace.”

“Of course,” Her mother said brushing her hair back vainly before tuning to face Agatha. “Make you she is kept far away from the Queen at all times until the wedding. I will not have anything spoiling this deal.”

“Of course, Your Grace,” came the chorus.

Perhaps she fears if she sees me up close before the wedding, she’ll call it off and high tail home, Agatha thought to herself. 

With her mother gone, the women got back to work with their poking and prodding. 

When they finally stepped away, admiring their work, she glanced at her reflection in the tall mirror across the room.

She didn’t look like a bride.

She looked like a gift.

All wrapped up and ready to be given away.

Rio —The Council Chamber

The chamber was colder than it needed to be.

Not cold in temperature but cold in design.

Walls too high, windows too narrow, air too still. Even the paintings on the walls were stiff with self importance. Past rulers with sharp eyes and thin mouths peered down at them from their gilded frames. 

Rio sat at one end of the long table, back straight, boots planted, gloved hands resting in her lap. Her officials stood behind her, silent and alert. 

They weren’t the ones being watched though. 

No— she was. Every inch of her. 

The gathered council members did little to hide their stares. From the old men in embroidered robes, to the younger scribes and few hawk eyed noblewomen. Their whispers had grown louder the longer she sat there. 

“Taller than I expected.” “Is that a sword at her hip?”  “A queen wearing trousers?” “She’d be a lot prettier if she smiled.”

She didn’t flinch.

Didn’t turn to look at them.

Didn’t let her mouth tighten, even though she wanted to.

Let them gawk. Let them weigh her like livestock at a market. She had been stared at by worse men, and she had killed most of them.

The doors opened with a theatrical sweep of air and perfume.

Evanora Harkness swept into the room like it belonged to her. With sweeping black silk, beaded shoulders, and practiced elegance. She smiled at everyone, nodded to no one, and took her seat with the grace of a woman who had spent her life expecting obedience.

Rio didn’t stand.

The room fell quiet.

“Queen Vidal,” Evanora purred, offering no bow, no greeting beyond the name. “What an honour to finally welcome you.”

“Likewise,” Rio replied coolly. “Though I notice we are not all present.”

Evanora blinked once.

“I’m sorry?”

“Your daughter,” Rio said, voice still polite but edged with steel. “Will she not be joining us for this meeting?”

There was a flicker of surprise around the table. A few exchanged glances. One of the councilmen coughed into his sleeve.

Evanora tilted her head.

“Oh, it’s simply customary, that the bride remain unseen by the groom — or should I say the…other bride, before the wedding. It’s quite sacred, you understand.”

She smiled sweetly — the kind of sweetness that reeked of rot.

Rio’s eyes narrowed, just slightly, at the evident dig. Same gender marriages were common among her people. But she knew this was a more…traditional Kingdom.

Still that was not what irked her most about the response.

“This meeting contains matters that concern her directly though, does it not?”

A few chuckled. In the uncomfortable, patronising way that always made her want to snap a blade in half.

“The princess has never taken much interest in political affairs,” one of them said.

“It’s all quite beyond her, really,” added another.

“Best not to burden the poor girl.”

Rio said nothing.

But her jaw tensed, just once.

The meeting began in full. Papers were produced. Terms laid out. Formalities exchanged.

Trade routes would be opened. Titles merged. Dowries restructured. Troop pathways revised.

All decided without the woman she was to marry in the room.

And yet they spoke about her constantly.

“The girl’s dowry lands have been reassessed, of course.”

“As her…spouse, you’ll assume full inheritance of her holdings.”

“The girl is Unseen of course but with how strong your magic is, I have no doubt any heirs produced will be of the Seen.” 

The words slide across the table like a knife on silk.

They stirred something inside her — not just a flash of anger. Something colder. Sharper. Older than rage.

She knew the tone well — that offhanded calculation dressed up in pleasantry. The kind used by generals when measuring casualties. By kings when selling daughters. By the same kind of men who once spoke of her bloodline with too much interest and not enough humanity.

They weren’t speaking of children. Not truly.

They were speaking of assets. Magical yield. Inheritance potential.

They spoke of the woman she was to marry as if her Unseen nature made her lesser, incomplete. A problem to be corrected by Rio’s blood.

And worse still, they assumed Rio would comply. That she would lay her body beside hers for the sake of lineage, power, legacy.

As if her womb were part of the treaty.

“I was not made aware that children were a condition of the treaty,” she said, voice even — a deadly kind of even.

There was a pause. A ripple. Just long enough to register that something had shifted in the room.

“Oh, nothing so crude, of course,” Evanora said with a smile that made Rio’s spine itch. “But it would be expected — eventually. Such a union should bear fruit, don’t you agree?”

The room chuckled again.

Rio didn’t.

She considered speaking — considered saying that the strength of magic was not measured by birthright, or power, or whether a child came from the Seen or the Unseen.

But she didn’t.

Not yet.

Let them believe her silence was diplomacy. Not disdain.

             ————————

After countless more negotiations and half veiled insults, at very last, the documents were drawn, the niceties were finished and the ink was drying.

“Thank you for your time, Your Majesty,” Evanora said, rising to her feet like a curtain call. “You’ll want to rest, I imagine. There will be a dinner this evening — a celebration of the alliance. You’ll be formally presented to the full court.”

A servant was summoned.

“Please escort Queen Vidal to her chambers. Her suite has been prepared.”

Rio stood, adjusting her gloves.

“See to it my staff are settled nearby,” she said. “And ensure I am not disturbed.”

The servant bowed low.

“Yes, Your Majesty.”

She walked out without another word, the air in the chamber clinging to her like dust.

Behind her, the council resumed murmuring.

Agatha — The Cleansing Ritual

The chamber smelled of rosewater and smoke.

Steam clung to the air like a second skin, thick and sticky, clouding the silver mirrors that lined the marble walls. She sat naked on a low stone stool as instructed, spine straight, legs tucked beneath her, as half a dozen women moved around her in silence, each one wrapped in white cloths that stayed dry while she remained drenched.

This wasn’t a bath.

It was a performance.

A purge.

One of her mother’s ladies — Dama Calis, the oldest and most severe — oversaw the entire thing in her mother’s absence, her bony arms folded, lips drawn thin as a blade. “Higher,” she snapped when Agatha’s chin dipped. “You are to be presented as royalty, not slouching like a peasant girl.”

Agatha obeyed, lifting her head. The steam made her dizzy. Sweat dripped into her eyes, but it was hard to tell what was sweat and what were tears.

They were careful never to speak to her directly unless correcting her posture or chastising a flinch. Though who could blame her? The cloths they used were harsh, and the scrubbing left her skin raw, stinging like a burn.

When she tried to shift away from one of them brushing too close to her chest, Calis narrowed her eyes.

“Sit still. You are not a child.”

The shame sank deeper than the heat.

They poured water over her again. Not warm. Not gentle. Not sacred. It felt more procedural. Almost punishing. 

Her hair was unbraided, then oiled, then combed out too roughly. Her scalp ached.

Her fingertips dug into the sides of the stool.

This was supposed to make her clean. Worthy. Holy.

But all she felt was scrubbed down to bone, like they were trying to erase something that refused to vanish.

She kept her eyes fixed on a crack in the wall, some barely visible line in the tile, and counted the seconds between each rinse. One… two… three… breathe.

The heat was too much. Her lungs felt swollen with it. She tried to take a deeper breath and failed.

Stillness. Obedience. Endurance.

That’s what they taught you.

That’s what makes you good.

One of the cloths scraped over her back again, harder than necessary. She gasped.

“Stop fidgeting,” Calis barked. “A queen does not wince.”

She said nothing. She was exhausted and had learned by now that silence was sometimes safer.

But as more water was poured over her — scalding this time — her throat tightened. She blinked against the steam. Her cheeks were already wet. She let them think it was just from the water.

They wouldn’t care either way.

Rio — The Call Home 

The guest chambers were too warm. The fire roared in the far end of the room, completely unnecessary for the time of the day. She put it out as soon as her escort had left. As she always did. 

The room itself was suffocating enough without the extra heat. The sickly sweet scent of perfume clung to the air like rot disguised as roses. Silk and lace adorned every surface. — the cushions, the walls, even the towering tapestry behind the bed — all in pale, muted colourings, whites and greys and soft creams. No contrast. No grounding feel. It was as though someone had tried to lull her into a docile state of femininity by way of decor.

She hated it.

She sat on the floor now, cross legged, spine straight, trying to steady her breathing.

Breathe in.

Breathe out.

Release the tension.

Do not kill anyone.

She tried to follow Lilia’s teachings. Tried to follow the rhythm of her breath to the pull of the River. But her chest was tight. Her jaw ached from clenching.

It didn’t help.

Eventually, she relented and rose. She crossed the room in slow deliberate steps and reached into her satchel. Her fingers curled around the smooth metal of her personal mirror — a travel sized disc, etched with runes along its bronze edge, the surface dark obsidian.

She knelt at the low table, set the mirror flat, and traced her fingertip over the main sigil. It flared briefly. 

She leaned in and murmured the activation spell.

The surface rippled like water. A heartbeat later, Lilia’s familiar face appeared with a gentle smile and soft eyes, silver hair twisted into her usual knot. She was seated in her office, lamplight casting soft shadows across her face.

“I expected you’d call around now,” Lilia said.

“Did the River whisper that?”

“No, I just know you well.”

The corners of Rio’s mouth twitched. Barely.

“And I know that face of yours,” Lilia added, tilting her head. “You’re concerned.”

“Im not…concerned.”

“You sound rather concerned.” 

A second face crowded the mirror — Alice, grinning like a fox with too much time and far too little sense of responsibility.

Rio exhaled through her nose. “This place is just..off.”

Lilia’s expression sharpened slightly.”Off how?”

“Cold,” she said. “Not literally but spiritually . It’s all… stiff tradition, and overly polite falseness. Everything smells like dust covered in honey. I don’t trust any of it.”

“That sounds about right,” Lilia said. “It’s an old court. Built on appearances and alliances. Nothing genuine survives here long.”

Rio’s fingers tapped once against the table. “I haven’t even seen her.”

“The girl?” Lilia asked.

She nodded. “Not a glimpse.”

“That’s to be expected,” Lilia said. “They’re most likely observing a pre-wedding rite. You know how it works.”

“Yes. But that doesn’t make it less eerie.” She hesitated. “I am to marry this woman tomorrow and I have only seen her reflection in a projection once. It’s strange.”

Lilia tilted her head. “Do you wish to see her?”

“I didn’t say that.”

“You didn’t deny it either.” Alice smile widened in the mirror. 

Rio ignored her. “I just think it’s wrong that she is being excluded from conversations that directly regard her future.”

Lilia didn’t speak for a while she simply watered at her, long and contemplative. “That’s not all that’s bothering you though. Is it?”

Rio hesitated. Then quietly she responded. “They’ve already begun speaking of heirs.”

There was a beat of silence. 

Alice’s face vanished from the mirror like she’d been pulled away. 

Lilia didn’t speak. Her eyes simply studied Rio’s face assessing.

“You didn’t mention that would be part of the treaty,” Rio added. Her voice was low, quiet, controlled.

“I didn’t think it would be brought up this soon,” Lilia said honestly. “But I won’t insult your intelligence, Your Majesty. I believe you knew it would come eventually.”

Rio looked away, jaw clenched.

Lilia continued. “You are not a woman who has ever been forced into something she didn’t choose. This will be no exception. It is a choice — yours to make.”

Rio’s voice cut through the air, sharper than intended. “It’s not only my choice.”

Lilia paused. Then she smiled — not mockingly, but with something softer. Deeply proud.

“Of course.”

The silence that followed stretched almost  uncomfortably for a moment.

Then Lilia added gently, “You have survived harder things than a royal wedding.”

Rio nodded once, but her hands were tight in her lap.

She wasn’t afraid of surviving.

She was afraid of what surviving this would cost.

She turned to look away, and that’s when she caught it.

Just for a breath. 

Rose water. Steam.

She froze. The scent clung to the air like a memory, soft and sudden, before vanishing without a trace. 

Strange.

The windows were closed. The guest chambers were tucked deep in the west wing, nowhere near any baths. 

It made no sense. 

And yet…it stirred something in her. Something she couldn’t name.

A flicker. A pull. Gone as quickly as it came. 

Agatha — The Veil Fitting 

They still hadn’t fed her. 

Not properly anyways. A few pieces of dried fruit, a thin slice of dry bread. Some lukewarm broth. It did nothing to dull the cache in her stomach. That same ache now twisted inside her ribs, dull and constant, as if her body had begun consuming itself in protest.

She stood in the center of the room, unmoving as three women circled her, adjusting and re-fastening the veil.

It wasn’t cloth. Not this time.

This veil clinked faintly with every breath. A fine lattice of metal — silver and gold, cool against her skin. It was delicate enough to glisten like thread, heavy enough to bruise. It draped from the circlet above her brow to the curve of her shoulders, a waterfall of tempered light and iron restraint. With each adjustment, the chainmail tightened, the links shifting, pulling, catching at the roots of her hair. It bit at her collarbone.

Woven with charms and twisted sigils meant to “protect” her. That’s what they said. That it was sacred. That it was tradition. That it was an honour, worn only by brides of noble blood on their wedding day. A symbol of virtue, meant to persevere her from sight until the union was sealed.

But she wasn’t naïve.

The weight of it wasn’t protection. It was ownership. Obscurity. A reminder of what she was meant to be.  Seen but not fully known.

She couldn’t help but think it looked very much like a cage. 

She couldn’t even feel the cool metal anymore. Her skin had gone numb. Her scalp ached from the constant repositioning. One of the women tugged the veil again, muttering under her breath about symmetry.

Agatha didn’t react.

Not when they tightened the drape around her temples. Not when the clasps dug into the base of her neck. Not even when they asked her to lift her chin again for the fifth time.

She had stopped flinching. Stopped arguing. Stopped asking why.

Somewhere between the bath and the silence that followed, she’d learned that obedience made things faster. Less painful, if not in body then at least in spirit.

So she stood still. A good daughter. A quiet bride.

She stared at her reflection, hollow eyed and pale under the silver mesh, and wondered if anyone would even recognise her beneath it. If she even recognised herself.

The veil shifted slightly as the women murmured among themselves, stepping back to assess their work.

“Perfect,” one of them said.

Agatha said nothing.

She was tired. So tired her bones felt waterlogged, her head too heavy for her spine. She didn’t remember when she’d last felt warmth that wasn’t steam or shame or hands she hadn’t invited.

She didn’t cry. She’d done enough of that earlier. Now there was only quiet, and the dull ringing of something inside her being smothered.

The mirror showed a figure draped in elegance. In reverence.

But underneath the veil, she was gone.

Rio — The Banquet 

She arrived exactly on time. Not a minute early. Not a second late.

She’d never been late to anything in her life — not battle, not council, not ceremony. That was the kind of discipline bred into her bones.

As the great doors creaked open, her name was formally announced. 

“Now enters Her Royal Majesty Queen Rio Vidal, ruler of the Riverlands, Commander of the River Guard.”

The room fell into a hush so absolute it could’ve been a funeral. 

Rio stood tall at the threshold, letting them look.

Her ensemble was calculated. Tailored obsidian robes interwoven with deep green silk, the stitching lined like veins across her shoulders and down her chest. Structured like armor but fluid like a priest’s vestments — neither dress nor uniform, neither feminine nor masculine . Deliberate.

The embroidery shimmered faintly when she moved, catching the firelight, but the sharpness of her silhouette left no room for softness.

She watched their faces closely. Most were blank with politeness. A few weren’t fast enough to hide their distaste.

Good, she thought. Let them choke on it.

She moved forward slowly, each step measured. The murmurs resumed the moment she passed. Forks stilled. Goblets were lowered. She could feel them staring — as though she were a wild animal who was about to bear its teeth or snarl.

She did neither.

At the long central table, she was guided to her seat. And seated to her left, of course, was Lady Harkness.

Her perfume was sharp, floral, something synthetic trying to mimic lilies. It clung to the air like frost.

“Your Majesty,” the woman greeted her, with the kind of smile that had razors hidden behind it.

Rio inclined her head in return. “Lady Harkness.”

The food was brought out then — heavy platters of roasted meats, limp vegetables, sugared breads. A feast on the surface, but the flavors were dead. She chewed slowly, methodically, barely tasting it.

To refuse would be rude. To complain would be political.

So she swallowed it down, like she swallowed most things. Quietly.

Conversation buzzed around her like flies. Tedious stories. Bad jokes. Thinly veiled questions about her lineage, her policies, her queendom. At one point, a baron asked if she fished her own rivers. Another inquired if women were permitted to duel where she came from. She gave cool, clipped answers — never impolite, but never what they wanted.

Still, she felt it — the itch of eyes on her. The small pauses in conversation when she lifted her goblet. The sideways glances. The whispers tucked behind sips of wine.

And yet…

Her gaze kept drifting, as if against her will. Of course she knew the girl would not be here. Part of the ridiculous, outdated tradition of this court. 

It still landed stubbornly in her chest. The faintest sense of disappointment. 

“Will your daughter be dining alone tonight?” Rio asked quietly, without turning her head.

Lady Harkness dabbed delicately at the corner of her mouth with a linen cloth. “Oh I’m sure she’s eaten something light.”

Another sip of wine. “She’ll be watching her figure though, no doubt.”

Rio’s hand tightened subtly around her goblet.

“I understand the sentiment,” She spoke coolly. “I’d simply hope she was being cared for.”

A flash of teeth “Oh, she’s being prepared for you. Don’t worry.”

Prepared.

Rio said nothing.

But her stomach turned.

Agatha — The Aftermath 

The door clicked shut behind the attendants. 

Silence fell like a cloud of ash.

Agatha fell into her bed and laid there for a long time, motionless. Her skin was pink and stinging, scrubbed raw beneath layers of perfumed oil and rose water. Her hair had been combed until it shone, plaited and pinned until they finally deemed her “presentable.” She’d been dressed, undressed, draped, tightened, veiled, unveiled, redressed.

Now she was alone. Stripped bare of ceremony, but still wearing the day like residue.

Her body ached. Her stomach churned

Her temple still felt tender from whee the chainmail veil had pressed against her skin. Her scalp throbbed. Her ribs felt too tight.

She felt—

Filthy. 

In a way water couldn’t cleanse.

When the knock came, it was tentative. Then again. Softer.

“…Agatha?”

“Enter,” she called weakly from the bed. She had no edgy to rise.

The door creaked open. Jen slipped through like a shadow. She didn’t speak at first. She just looked at her. Her face folded when she saw the state of her — red rimmed eyes and colourless lips.

Jen moved quickly.

“I brought you something.” She shut the door behind her and held up a cloth bundle. “Don’t ask what it is. I stole it from the kitchens when they weren’t looking.”

Agatha didn’t answer. She didn’t trust herself to not cry.

Jen crossed the room, set the bundle down on the low table, and crouched beside her. She unwrapped a still warm meat pie and half an apple, sliced cleanly with a dull knife.

Agatha sat down finally, slowly, like someone afraid their bones might break if they bent too far.

She reached for the food. Took a bite.

Chewed once. Twice. Swallowed.

Then shook her head.

“I can’t,” she whispered.

Jen didn’t press. Just wrapped the food again and sat back on her heels.

“They wouldn’t let me see you all day.”

“They had me quite…occupied.”

Silence stretched between them.

“I hate this place.” Agatha said at last.

“I know.” 

“They’ve dressed me up like a thing. A… display. All this talk of sacred duty, and chastity, and veils, and I’m just—” Agatha trailed off, chest rising unevenly. “I’m not even a person to them anymore. I don’t think I ever was.”

Jen reached for her hand.

“I know,” she said again. “But it’s almost over.”

Agatha looked at her, eyes hollow. 

“I mean it,” Jen said, squeezing her hand. “You’ll be a part of her court after tomorrow. And then you’ll be away from all this. They can’t hurt you anymore.” 

“What if it’s just worse there?” Agatha whispered, fingers curling in the sheets. 

“Then you give them hell,” Jen said firmly, her voice breaking through the hush. “And remind them who you are.”

Agatha let out a weak laugh — small, cracked, but real. “I don’t think I remember her anymore.”

“Yes, you do.” Jen squeezed her hand tighter. “You’re Agatha Harkness. My best friend. A fighter. A survivor. And you don’t give up.”

For a moment, Agatha almost believed her.

She closed her eyes. Breathed in the faint scent of rose water and metal that still clung to her skin.

“I’ll try,” she whispered.

Jen brushed a strand of damp hair from her face. “That’s all you ever need to do.”

When she eventually left, the room felt even quieter.

Agatha lay back on the bed, staring at the canopy above her until her vision blurred.

Somewhere, beyond the walls, the River whispered.

Tomorrow, she would become someone’s wife.

Tonight, she was still herself—barely.

And that, for now, would have to be enough.

 

Chapter End Notes

Hope you all enjoyed this update. I’m in the middle of reading week and while I should be catching up on assignments I’m instead catching on writing.

In case anyone might be confused

Seen= The fractions of magic users that have visible, tangible and easily classified magic. Includes healing, earth magic, elemental magic, protection magic, etc They are praised, displayed and celebrated. You can see the physical results of their magic. It is deemed as safer and easier to control.

Unseen= The fractions of magic users whose magic do not manifest in spectacle or are deemed as dangerous and to be hidden away. These include spirit witches, dream walkers, shadow magic and also divination though that is more complicated and is often regarded higher as long as it is controlled. These magic users are feared, mistrusted, misunderstood, often mistaken as powerless and dangerous.

Chapter 4: A Royal Wedding

Chapter Notes

CW for:
emotional abuse
dehumanisation
Self inflicted injury
Panic/anxiety themes
Mild dissociation

See the end of the chapter for more notes

The morning light was too clean.

 

It sliced through the high windows in pale ribbons, silvering the steam that curled from a basin beside the bed. The palace felt hollow in the early hours, its marble halls still and watchful, as though the building itself knew what day it was.

 

Rio had been awake for hours.

 

The bells had started at dawn, their low toll rolling through the palace like the slow beat of a war drum. Every note reminded her what today was — what she had agreed to.

 

She sat at the edge of the bed, still in her undershirt, working her thumbs over the seam of one leather glove. The material creaked softly every time she flexed. A quiet, steady rhythm. Almost enough to drown out the pulse stubbornly beating too fast beneath her collarbone.

 

It wasn’t nerves, she told herself. Nerves were for young cadets at their first duel, for soldiers on parade, for fools who mistook desire for destiny. This was politics. Strategy. Paperwork sealed with silk and vows instead of ink.

 

Still, her pulse refused to obey. It thudded traitorously under her ribs, steady and fast, as though her body hadn’t received the memo that her heart had no business being involved in today’s events.

 

She rose and crossed to the window. Below, the courtyard was already a blur of motion — attendants carrying gilded trays, horses being brushed to mirror sheen, nobles weaving between banners. The whole kingdom seemed to hold its breath in anticipation.

 

Rio adjusted her cuffs, then reached for the uniform hung beside the door.

 

The garment waiting for her was not the soft ceremonial robes her advisors had suggested — pale silks meant to flatter, to make her look gentle and inviting. No. She had set those aside last night without hesitation.

 

Instead she chose the other ensemble:

A dark navy coat, cut sharply at the waist.

Gold threaded embroidery climbing the collar like stylised roots.

A heavy ceremonial cloak clasped at her shoulders with two wrought gold leaf pins.

Structured, martial, unmistakable.

 

The cut of a general’s uniform. A soldier’s silhouette. It carried the right message.

 

Love had no place in this treaty.

 

She dressed in silence, every button closed with mechanical precision. When she was done, she reached for the small mirror on the table. The woman staring back looked composed, carved from calm. No one would know she hadn’t slept. No one would see that the hollowness behind her eyes was not from boredom, but from the faint, inexplicable tremor that kept catching her breath.

 

Her hand drifted, almost unconsciously, to the space above her heart.

 

The place where, in stories, a soulmark would burn if fate ever laid claim to her.

 

Children loved those tales — the spark, the instant recognition, the bond deeper than blood. She had loved them once too, before she learned what they meant for someone in her position. Before she was old enough to understand that a soulmate for a queen was not a blessing but a disaster waiting to happen.

 

A monarch bound by magic was a monarch compromised.

 

A queen in love was a queen controllable.

 

The court whispered it often enough. Better for the throne if she never burned at all. Better that her chest stay cool and untouched, her heart loyal only to the crown. Better that she never gave the people — or her enemies — someone they could pry from her.

 

She had outgrown the fantasies long ago.

Soulmates were for dreamers and poets.

Not for women who built queendoms from ash.

 

Still, her palm lingered there a moment longer than it should have. The space beneath her hand felt too alive, too aware. She pulled her hand away as though burned.

 

She frowned.

 

A knock at the door broke the quiet. One of her aides entered — a young diplomat assigned to oversee the formalities. He bowed deeply, eyes flicking nervously over her attire.

 

“Your Majesty, they’re readying the hall.”

 

Rio inclined her head, voice steady. “I’ll be there shortly.”

 

When he left, she exhaled. It was not quite a sigh, more a slow release of pressure. The silence folded around her again, thick and waiting.

 

She slipped on her gloves at last. The leather molded perfectly to her hands.

 

Outside, another bell tolled — lower, heavier. The sound echoed the her sternum.

 

Rio straightened, squared her shoulders, and forced her heartbeat to slow.

 

She had faced sieges with steadier hands than this.

 

Yet as she stood there it felt as though she were preparing to walk straight into fire.

 

———-

 

The corset was too tight.

 

Agatha’s first thought when they finished pulling on the laces that morning was that it felt as though her chest had been bound with iron. The second was that she was going to be sick.

 

By the time Jen reached her, she was already doubled over the basin, trembling. Nothing came up—there was nothing in her to give—but her body kept trying, convulsing with dry, shuddering heaves that tore at her throat. The room spun, candlelight bleeding against white walls.

 

“Breathe,” Jen murmured, pressing a cool hand between her shoulder blades. “It’s all right, it’s just nerves—”

 

Agatha shook her head, gripping the rim of the basin until her knuckles blanched. The air refused to come. It scraped at her throat in small, useless gulps.

 

Jen had actually managed to sneak in that morning to Agatha’s surprise. Her mother was too busy running in and out of rooms, barking orders and demanding perfection from everyone within earshot to notice the intrusion.

 

And as though the mere thought of her mother was enough to summon her, the woman appeared in the doorway.

 

“I was told she would be dressed already,” came her mother’s grating voice. “Why isn’t she standing?”

 

Agatha froze.

 

Her mother swept into the room like a gust of cold wind, silk skirts whispering against the floor. Her gaze cut through everything—through Jen, through Agatha’s trembling hands, through the quiet plea caught in her daughter’s chest.

 

“Get up.”

 

“Mother, I—”

 

“Enough.”

 

Jen tried to protest—something about the corset being too tight—but was silenced with a glance. The older woman crossed the room in two measured steps and caught Agatha’s chin in her hand. Her perfume smelled of crushed roses and iron.

 

“You will not shame this family with theatrics,” she said. “You will walk. You will smile. You will thank her for saving you from spinsterhood, and you will not make a spectacle of yourself. Do you understand?”

 

Agatha nodded because it was easier.

 

Her mother released her with a small sound of disgust and left as swiftly as she had come. The door clicked shut, leaving only the ringing in Agatha’s ears.

 

Jen hovered beside her, wringing her hands. “Try slow breaths. In—out—”

 

But Agatha had already slipped into the rhythm she knew best.

She closed her eyes and held her breath. One, two, three, four—

Her chest burned. The world blurred.

Five, six—

Her mind quieted. Control reclaimed.

 

She let the air out carefully, steadying her shaking hands.

 

When she opened her eyes again, the maids had returned — three of them, faces expressionless as they entered carrying boxes, brushes, and a small tray of jewels. The air filled with the scent of starch and metal polish.

 

They descended on her with mechanical precision.

 

First came the underskirts, layer after layer until she could barely move. The weight gathered around her hips, pulling her backward as though the dress itself were trying to swallow her. Then the gown — silk, embroidered with gold thread that caught the candlelight in cruel flashes. It was beautiful, objectively. A work of art, the kind meant to be admired, not inhabited.

 

The maids tugged and pinned and adjusted. Her arms were raised, lowered, turned, spun. Someone brushed powder over her collarbones, another painted her lips the colour of wilted roses. The smallest one knelt to fasten pearl buttons up her sleeves, murmuring apologies each time the fabric caught her skin.

 

Agatha stood motionless through it all, letting them dress her like a doll. Each tug, each fastening stole another inch of breath. By the time they were done, her body no longer felt like her own—it belonged to the gown, to the performance.

 

Her hair came next. Jen took over there, working  in silence, combing it through trembling fingers, weaving braids into soft coils. The pins bit into her scalp until she stopped flinching.

 

“There,” Jen whispered eventually, stepping back. “Perfect.”

 

Perfect. The word rang hollow in her ears.

 

The room had grown too bright. Candlelight bounced off every mirrored surface, splintering her reflection into half a dozen versions of herself. Pale, still, obedient. The only movement was the slow rise and fall of her chest as she fought for shallow breaths beneath the corset’s vice.

 

The veil waited folded on the dressing table — chainmail so fine it glittered like frost. When placed over her face, she would be unseen, unheard, owned.

 

Jen’s hands were gentle as she adjusted the gown’s neckline, trying to smile. “It’ll be over soon. Just remember to keep your chin up, all right?”

 

Agatha nodded, eyes fixed on her reflection. Her mouth curved into something that might have been a smile if not for the tremor running through it.

 

“Good girl,” Jen murmured. “You look beautiful.”

 

Agatha could almost laugh at the absurdity of it.

 

Agatha’s gaze flicked to the veil again. To the gloves lying beside it. To the spot at her wrist where the cords would soon tie. She thought of the hall beyond these walls, of the stranger waiting there — her future carved into treaty parchment.

 

The air pressed in.

 

One of the maids knocked lightly on the doorframe. “It’s time, my lady.”

 

Jen squeezed her hand once, as if she could anchor her. “You can do this,” she said softly.

 

Agatha nodded,  though she wasn’t sure who she was agreeing with.

 

For a heartbeat, she almost tore the dress apart just to breathe.

To ruin it. To ruin everything.

 

Instead, she straightened her spine the way her mother had taught her. Counted to six. Smoothed her skirt.

 

The veil came down last. The world dimmed.

 

Sound narrowed to the whisper of fabric and the steady thud of her heart.

 

Each step toward the great doors felt heavier than the last.

 

When they opened before her—revealing the endless marble aisle, the sea of strangers, the waiting queen at the altar—Agatha finally understood what her mother had meant by spectacle.

 

The entire kingdom had gathered to watch her disappear.

 

And for a moment, she almost did.

 

——

 

The hall was too quiet.

 

Not silent. No, silence would have been mercy. This was the kind of quiet that hummed, filled with the low murmur of anticipation and the brittle weight of expectation. Every cough, every shuffle of feet echoed like a drumbeat through the vaulted ceiling.

 

Rio stood at the altar, hands clasped loosely behind her back, the faint scent of candle smoke and river incense coiling in the air.

 

The officiator — a tall, gaunt figure draped in the silver vestments of the River Order — stood at her right. His voice flowed in the old cadence of the binding rites, each line steady and low, crafted to soothe the River as much as to instruct the living. Verses about devotion, surrender, continuity — words Rio knew by heart long before she ever wore a crown.

 

The sigil on his robes caught the candlelight.

The River’s sigil.

 

It glowed faintly, alive with that familiar pulse she had been taught to welcome — a presence she had once believed watched over her. Guided her. Carried her family. Took them, too.

 

A presence she had spent years pretending she no longer feared.

 

Her throat tightened.

 

The River had made her queen. The River had guided her bloodline for generations. Whatever vows she took today, the River would take them too.

 

The thought was supposed to carry comfort to her.

 

But as the officiator continued, Rio realized she was barely hearing him.

 

Her gaze was fixed on the double doors at the far end of the aisle. Sealed shut. Still. Waiting.

 

They were carved with the crest of the Harkness kingdom—a serpent wound around a burning branch. Beautiful. Merciless.

 

She could feel the eyes of the hall pressing against her shoulders. Nobles studying her posture, advisors measuring her expression, guards waiting for her slightest movement, ready to emulate it. Everyone wanting her to be something. Softer, harder, warmer, colder. A symbol, not a woman.

 

And above them all, the gaze she felt the most — the River’s. Its envoys lined the outer aisles, silver robed, watchful.

 

Every breath she took here would be noted.

Every hesitation carried into the archives.
