Every vow judged not just by her people but by the sacred current itself.

 

Her gloves felt too tight suddenly. She flexed her fingers once, twice. The polished metal of her insignia glinted beneath the candlelight.

 

Just a ceremony. That was what she told herself.

She had attended a dozen of these political unions in her lifetime — some for peace, others for power. It was nothing more than ritual theatre.

 

And yet her pulse had been steady only until the moment the first note of the ceremonial organ struck.

 

The doors creaked open.

 

The murmurs died, swallowed by silence.

 

Rio drew a breath and looked up.

 

The bride entered.

 

Everything seemed to slow.

 

At first glance, she looked like a statue — something carved of snow and moonlight, swaying slightly under the weight of its own beauty. The gown was enormous, heavy silk embroidered with so much gold it gleamed like armor. Chains of jewelry hung from her wrists, her neck, her hair.

 

Each step brought her closer to the fate they were about to share — the one Rio had agreed to, and in doing so, sealed for both of them.

 

To most, it might have seemed she was gliding forward effortlessly. But Rio’s trained eye caught the truth in every movement. The subtle hesitation in her gait, the way her back held too straight, her breath too shallow.

 

Every step was measured. Forced. Pained.

 

Rio’s gaze dropped to her hands.

 

Ribbons of silk wound tightly down her arms, binding her wrists together as if she were a prisoner being offered, not a bride being received.

 

Her breath caught when she looked higher.

 

The bride’s face was obscured — not by lace or sheer silk.

 

Chainmail.

 

Not the heavy kind worn for battle, but a delicate weave of silver, fine as frost, veiling her features in shimmer and silence.

 

The sound it made — a faint, metallic whisper — cut sharper than anything the officiator had said.

 

They caged her, Rio thought.

 

A strange twist of feeling clawed its way up from her gut — not disgust, not pity. Anger.

 

This was meant to be a union. A peace. Yet everything about this girl’s attire screamed possession. Her bowed head. Her bound wrists. The weight of her veil.

 

Rio’s jaw clenched. Her fingers twitched at her side, as though itching to undo the knots themselves.

 

But she didn’t move.

 

Not yet.

 

The hall’s eyes were still on her, waiting. She had to remain composed, poised, untouchable.

 

The bride reached the steps.

 

And then, she knelt.

 

Not in a sweeping, elegant gesture. Not for show.

 

She dropped slowly, like her body was made of stone. The veil shimmered as it fell forward, hiding her face completely once more. The cords around her wrists clinked faintly, pulling her shoulders into a hunch.

 

She didn’t lift her head.

 

But she raised her hands.

 

Delicate, gloved fingers extended forward, wrists bound together by silk, held in the quiet gesture of a girl trained not to speak — only to offer.

 

The movement was precise. Practiced. As if she’d rehearsed it in a hundred nightmares.

 

Her chin stayed low. Her eyes remained hidden. She didn’t look at Rio.

 

She was waiting.

 

The officiator paused. The hall was watching.

 

Rio stared down at the figure kneeling before her — still, silent, veiled in silver.

 

And something in her chest cracked.

 

She stepped forward slowly, the echo of her boots against marble the only sound in the room. Her hands itched at her sides. The gloves felt too tight again. Her lungs struggled to expand.

 

When she reached the girl, she stopped. The small hands remained lifted, wrists lifted in silent submission.

 

As if she were an offering.

 

As if she belonged to her now.

 

A flicker of heat sparked in Rio’s throat. She didn’t like being watched. She didn’t like being pushed. But more than anything, she didn’t like what this moment was asking her to become.

 

Her boots echoed as she stepped forward.

 

Each sound rang louder than it should have — and yet the room said nothing.

 

The officiator’s voice paused, waiting for her to complete the next motion. To take the bride’s hands. To lift the veil. To claim what was apparently hers.

 

Rio reached the kneeling figure and stopped.

 

The girl didn’t look up. She was perfectly still.

 

The candlelight danced across the curve of her shoulders. The cords at her wrists shimmered like silk shackles. Her head was bowed beneath a veil that glinted like frost.

 

Rio exhaled — once — and then slowly bent her knees.

 

She knelt in front of her.

 

Gasps rippled through the room. A murmur rose — low, startled, sharp. Somewhere at the edge of her awareness, someone whispered her title. Someone else cursed.

 

But she didn’t hear them.

 

She was watching the girl.

 

She had flinched when Rio had knelt down. Her shoulders tensed and her head lifted just slightly as if afraid of what was happening.

 

They were level now. Two women, face to face.

 

Separated only by gold mesh.

 

Rio looked through the fine lattice of chainmail, and for a single breathless moment, she wished she could see her eyes.

 

Her hand rose slowly. No flourish. No ceremony. Just movement.

 

The veil caught slightly on the girl’s ear. Rio adjusted it carefully, lifting it back over her crown and letting it fall like water behind her shoulders.

And there she was.

 

Pale, composed, eyes still downcast. But her mouth was set, her jaw firm, breath held like a thread in her throat. Rio recognised that look.

 

She was not empty.

 

She was enduring.

 

The candlelight caught on the jewelry around her neck, turning her into something both regal and ruined.

 

Rio looked at her for one second too long.

 

Then she reached for the cords.

 

The silk was soft beneath her gloves, smooth as river reeds. She worked through the knots slowly, gently — not the way she’d been taught, not the way the ritual called for.

 

The first knot loosened with a soft pull.

 

The girl’s hands twitched in surprise.

 

The second knot gave way with a breath.

 

The final tie fell apart between her fingers, and the cords slid free.

 

Her wrists were bare.

 

But she didn’t lower her hands yet. She stayed still, gaze still lowered.

 

Rio hovered for a moment, gloved fingers lingering just above hers. The air between them felt warmer now. Heavier.

 

There was something there.

 

A quiet pressure, warm and unfamiliar, rising in the breath of space between their hands.

 

She looked up, briefly meeting the officiator’s eye.

 

He inclined his head.

 

“You may rise.”

 

Rio offered her hand.

 

The girl glanced at it — then at her. Their eyes met for the first time.

 

Ocean blue. Too many stories written behind them.

 

She hesitated, then placed her hand in Rio’s.

 

And together, they stood.

 

——-

 

Agatha lowered herself to her knees as she had been taught.

 

She had practiced the motion dozens of times under her mother’s watchful eye — the art of lowering without tripping on her skirts, of keeping her spine straight even as her pride bent.

 

She kept her head bowed — hands offered, posture still.

 

Not out of devotion.

Not even submission.

 

Shame.

 

The shame of being reduced to this.

Of being expected to make herself small.

 

But even here, even like this, she refused to let her spine bend too far. She could kneel. She would kneel. But she would not break.

 

She waited for the expected motions—the claiming hand, the lifting of the veil, the sharp tug of silk cords undone by someone standing over her.

 

But none of it came. The silence in the hall stretched.

 

And then—

 

A shadow lowered beside her.

 

Agatha felt the presence before she understood it: solid, deliberate, grounding the space rather than dominating it.

 

The queen had knelt.

 

Her breath caught, sharp behind her ribs.

 

Through the chainmail veil, the shape beside her blurred and shifted, kneeling to her level. Not claiming height. Not claiming ownership.

 

Agatha didn’t know what to do with that.

Kindness, in her experience, was never given freely. It always hid a hook. A price. A lesson.

 

She braced herself for the catch.

 

Instead, gloved hands lifted her veil with slow, careful precision. As though afraid to disturb her.

 

The chainmail shifted. Light rippled across her skin.

 

Light rippled across her face as the world came back into focus.

 

Agatha still didn’t look up.

She didn’t trust herself to.

Better to expect contempt than be caught off guard by something unfamiliar.

 

Her gaze stayed fixed on the queen’s hands as they reached for the silk cords binding her wrists. Each knot was untied with measured patience, the movement steady enough that Agatha felt a strange flutter of unease.

 

Gentleness always unsettled her more than cruelty.

She knew how to defend herself against harshness — she had practice.

Softness, though… softness unmade her.

 

The gloves brushed her skin only lightly, dulled by fabric, but warmth seeped through despite the barrier. A faint tingle crawled up her arms. Not magic. Not anything she could name.

 

Something else.

 

By the time the cords fell away and her wrists were freed, Agatha felt both lighter and heavier — loosened physically, tightened somewhere deeper.

 

She hadn’t expected this.

 

She hadn’t expected her captor queen to kneel.

She hadn’t expected gentleness.

She hadn’t expected to feel anything at all.

 

The queen rose first, then extended a hand down toward her.

 

Agatha froze.

 

Not in fear.

Not in defiance.

 

In calculation.

 

Everything in her wanted to refuse the hand – wanted to stand on her own, to show she wasn’t as breakable as the world kept insisting she was. That she still had dignity left.

 

But refusing would draw her mother’s ire. Would insult the queen. Would risk everything before it even began.

 

So she lifted her chin—just barely—and looked at the offered hand.

A compromise with herself.

 

When she finally raised her gaze higher, cautious and searching, her eyes met the queen’s for the first time.

 

Dark. Steady.

Not cruel.

Not cold.

 

But not soft either.

Something more complicated lived there — something unreadable.

 

Agatha’s throat tightened.

She ignored it.

She took the hand.

 

The contact was fleeting, dulled by glove and courtesy, but it sparked something low and unwelcome in her chest — a quiet pressure, a whisper without words.

 

She didn’t trust it.

 

She didn’t trust her reaction to it.

 

And she didn’t have time to make sense of it anyway. The officiator turned, voice rising, calling them forward for the next phase.

 

Agatha stood beside the queen, hands folded neatly now, wrists bare and suddenly cold. With the veil gone, nothing blurred her vision — and yet somehow she saw less clearly than before.

 

She glanced up once more, quick, assessing, guarded.

 

This wasn’t what she’d been told to expect.

This wasn’t a tyrant.

This wasn’t a monster.

 

But Agatha had learned young: even gentle hands could turn cruel.

 

So she said nothing.

 

She listened, she watched, and she waited.

 

Not broken.

Not trusting.

Not yet.

 

——-

 

The officiator’s voice echoed across the stone hall.

 

“Join hands before the River. Let your union be bound, and your fate entwined.”

 

Rio stepped forward first.

 

She stood before the ceremonial basin — silver and still, filled with water drawn from the northern tributary of the River of Life. A single flower floated at its center, undisturbed. Tradition. Symbol. Ritual.

 

She heard the girl move behind her.

Not timid, not faltering — just quiet.

 

When she reached Rio’s side, the officiator extended the silk cords — the same ones that had bound the girl’s wrists only moments ago.

 

Now, they would be used to join them.

 

The symbolism of it wasn’t lost on Rio.

She just didn’t have the time or clarity to think about what it meant.

 

“Bare hands,” the officiator said.

 

She hesitated.

 

Her right hand hovered at her wrist.

 

Then, without ceremony, she slipped the glove off.

 

The leather fell away with a soft hiss. The air kissed her skin — cool, clean. She hadn’t touched another person barehanded in weeks. Maybe longer.

 

She extended her hand toward the girl —

 

Agatha — the name burned itself into her mind without being spoken.

 

And she raised hers in turn.

 

Their palms met.

 

Skin to skin.

 

The world split.

 

Rio’s legs almost buckled. She gripped tighter — reflex, instinct — as a surge of something massive shot through her chest.

 

Not heat. Not pain. Not magic.

Something deeper. Older.

 

This could not be what she feared it was.

 

Agatha gasped softly, eyes flickering to her like she’d felt it too.

 

Rio’s grip tightened reflexively, steadying her. She didn’t speak, didn’t move, didn’t dare breathe.

 

Her ears filled with the rush of riverwater — but it wasn’t sound. It was sensation. Movement inside her. Pressure behind her ribs. Light behind her eyes.

 

For one disorienting moment, she wasn’t standing in a ceremonial hall.

 

She was standing in a current.

Dragged under by something inevitable.

 

Their eyes met again.

 

The pull between them burned like truth.

 

Like fate.

 

The officiator was still speaking — reciting the vows, invoking the River — but Rio barely heard him.

 

Agatha’s hand was small, but steady.

The cords were being wrapped now — looped around their joined wrists, one, then the other.

 

And still, the sensation didn’t fade.

 

The mark had bloomed beneath her skin — she could feel it. Not visible to the crowd, but present. Seared into her chest like a second heartbeat.

 

This wasn’t supposed to happen.

This could not happen.

Not here.

Not now.

 

She fought to keep her expression still.

To keep her breathing even.

 

She tried to maintain a facade of indifference.

But something inside her had already shifted beyond repair.

 

——-

 

The water shimmered beneath their joined hands.

 

At first, Agatha thought it was just the light. The reflection of the silver bowl, perhaps, or the flicker of candles against metal. But then it moved — not across the surface, but through her.

 

A sound — not sound at all, more like a breath drawn through the soul — passed between them. Her fingers tightened instinctively around the other woman’s, though she didn’t know why.

 

The world narrowed.

 

Her heart stuttered once, twice, and then found a rhythm that wasn’t hers.

 

As though it now beat with someone else’s.

 

She inhaled sharply. The scent of river salt and candle smoke filled her lungs, dizzying. It was as though the air itself had changed — thicker, sweeter, alive.

 

Then came the heat.

 

It started beneath her sternum — a sudden, sharp pulse that spread outward in a single sweep, radiating through every nerve. She barely swallowed a gasp. Her knees weakened.

 

No, she thought. No, no, no.

 

This couldn’t be what she thought it was.

 

And yet… it was.

 

The mark.

 

The stories always spoke of it — the way your soul would know. That when you touched the one the River had chosen, you’d feel the current carve its name into your heart.

 

She felt it now, burning invisible and undeniable beneath her ribs.

 

Her gaze lifted, dazed, to the woman standing before her.

 

Rio Vidal.

 

The foreign queen. The stranger she was meant to now call wife.

 

Agatha’s breath caught on a quiet, disbelieving laugh that never reached her lips. For years she had convinced herself she didn’t believe in this—didn’t need it. And still, in the dark hours, some fragile part of her had imagined what it might feel like. What it might mean.

 

She’d never thought it would feel like this.

 

Like drowning.

 

Like being handed a miracle and a curse in the same breath.

 

She searched the other woman’s face for any sign of recognition — the faintest flicker that she felt it too.

 

Nothing.

 

The queen’s expression didn’t shift. Her jaw was set, her eyes unreadable, her shoulders squared in calm defiance. If she felt the same lightning that was burning through Agatha’s veins, she hid it behind a wall of stone.

 

The officiator’s voice thundered the final words of the vow. The crowd applauded.

 

Agatha barely heard it.

 

Her hand slipped from her now wife’s as the applause swelled. The absence of contact was like stepping out of sunlight into cold air. A hollow space where something had been forming only moments ago.

 

She curled her fingers into a fist, letting her nails bite half-moons into her palm. It grounded her. Brought back the clarity she needed.

 

So that was her answer.

 

Not rejection — not quite — but distance. Deliberate. Controlled.

A queen refusing to acknowledge whatever had flickered between them.

 

And Agatha understood why.

Of course she did.

 

A soulmate bond between queens was dangerous enough.

A soulmate bond to her — an unseen Spirit witch from a kingdom the court already distrusted — would look like corruption. Influence. A threat to the throne.

 

She knew exactly how their story would be spun.

 

The foreign girl bewitched her.

The Spirit witch ensnared our queen.

Her magic tainted the River’s chosen heir.

 

Agatha would be the villain before she ever had the chance to speak.

She always was.

 

So the coolness in the queen’s eyes wasn’t cruelty.

It was strategy.

Survival.

And Agatha couldn’t fault her for that.

 

But the faint ache beneath her ribs remained — a small, sharp thing she refused to name.

 

It didn’t matter. It couldn’t.

 

Whatever spark had flared the moment their hands touched would have to stay buried. Hidden so deeply no Tidekeeper, no advisor, no rival kingdom could detect it.

 

The bond, if that’s what it was, wasn’t a gift.

It was a liability.

A threat.

A chain with two ends — and one of those ends wrapped around her neck far more tightly.

 

Besides, she’d spent her whole life being something to endure, to hide, to barter. She didn’t need to be chained to someone else by law and by soul.

 

Her throat ached with the effort of holding herself upright beneath the weight of the dress, the jewelry, the crowd’s gaze.

 

The cords were gone, but her wrists still burned where  the queen’s fingers had touched them.

 

And though she told herself she didn’t care, the mark beneath her heart throbbed once more, like it was laughing at her.

 

Before them stood the basin — the same basin they had joined their hands over while the fate of their lives were forever changed.

 

The officiator turned toward them, his voice lower now, as though even ceremony bowed before the water.

 

“By the current that carves all fates,” he intoned, “do you enter into this union willingly?”

 

The question hung in the air like smoke.

 

Agatha’s mouth went dry.

 

Willingly.

 

The word struck harder than she expected.

 

Her gaze wavered. Instinct told her not to look anywhere but forward, but the silence stretched too long. Against her better judgment, she glanced toward the crowd.

 

Rows of faces stared back — unreadable, gilded, waiting.

 

And there, near the front, was her mother.

 

Her expression was a blade. Eyes sharp. Jaw tight. A single small movement of her chin — a warning.

 

Don’t you dare.

 

Don’t embarrass us.

 

Don’t make this harder than it already is.

 

Agatha’s chest tightened. Her throat ached. The room was too warm, the veil of incense too thick. The officiator’s expectant pause pressed against her ribs like a hand.

 

Her eyes drifted back — unwillingly — to the woman in front of her.

 

The queen was still watching her.

 

Their eyes met briefly. Just a flicker.

There was no command in it. No impatience.

 

Her gaze was still unreadable but something flickered through it. Something that almost resembled guilt.

 

Then the gaze dropped, and the moment passed.

 

Agatha swallowed hard, the word scraping its way out of her throat.

 

“I do.”

 

It came out soft — almost fragile — but it was enough.

 

The officiator turned toward the queen.

 

“And do you, Rio Vidal, enter into this union willingly?”

 

There was a beat of silence.

 

Then, clear but Agatha caught almost a hint of hesitation.

 

“I do.”

 

The officiator nodded once. “Then let the River be your witness.”

 

He gestured to the basin.

 

Agatha hesitated only a heartbeat before extending her hand. The queen did the same. Their palms met once more — bare this time. No gloves. No cords. Only skin and skin.

 

The officiator guided their hands together, then lowered them both into the basin.

 

The water was cold. Not sharp, but chilling enough to steal her breath. Her fingers curled slightly against the queen’s, instinctively, reflexively.

 

The officiator began the vows.

 

He spoke slowly, with weight. “Repeat after me.”

 

Agatha held her breath.

 

“I, Agatha of House Harkness vow to serve this union,” he said.

 

Agatha echoed, softly, “I, Agatha of House Harkness vow to serve this union.”

 

“I vow to obey the laws that bind us.”

 

The words scraped like glass on her tongue. “I vow to obey the laws that bind us.”

 

“I vow to honour my duty, even when the current turns.”

 

She forced the words out. “I vow to honour my duty, even when the current turns.”

 

“Until the River runs dry.”

 

She hesitated — just barely — then whispered, “Until the River runs dry.”

 

Silence.

 

Then the officiator turned to the queen.

 

Her voice, when it came, was steady.

Stronger. Sharper. But no warmer.

 

“I, Queen Rio Vidal, vow to lead this union.”

 

“I vow to uphold the laws that bind us.”

 

“I vow to protect my duty, even when the current turns.”

 

“Until the River runs dry.”

 

Each line was delivered with perfect clarity, but something shifted in her voice on that last phrase. A tightness, barely there. As if it cost her something.

 

Their hands remained in the basin.

 

Agatha thought the water would do something — shimmer, hiss, swirl. But it only trembled once, like a held breath, and stilled.

 

No thunder. No blessing. No voice of the gods.

 

Only the cold.

 

And the way her hand began to ache the longer she held it in the queen’s.

 

The officiator nodded. “The River has heard you.”

 

He stepped forward with the rings — simple bands of river forged metal. Silver for Agatha. Iron dark for the queen.

 

“Do you accept this bond?” he asked.

 

Neither of them spoke.

 

But their hands released, and they each took the other’s ring.

 

The metal was warm — warmer than it should have been. It slid onto her finger like it already knew where to go.

 

The queen’s touch was brief.

 

A brush. A weight.

 

Then it was done.

 

There was a beat of silence.

 

The applause swelled, formal and hollow. Somewhere behind it, the officiator’s voice rang out one final time, clear and ceremonial.

 

“By the will of the River and the bond that now binds you—

 

You may seal your union with a kiss.”

 

Agatha went still.

 

Her breath hitched. She didn’t look up.

 

She didn’t know what would be worse. To be kissed like a stranger… or not kissed at all.

 

The silence that followed felt too long.

 

Then came a shift beside her.

 

She felt the queen turn, not toward the crowd, but toward her. Agatha looked up, just in time to meet her gaze.

 

There was no cruelty in it. No coldness.

 

But there was something else — something measured and unreadable.

 

She braced herself.

 

But the queen didn’t lean in.

Didn’t reach for her waist.

Didn’t touch her face.

 

Instead, she extended a hand.

 

Agatha, unsure, let her take it.

 

The queen lifted it gently and pressed her lips to the back of it.

 

Soft. Measured. Brief.

 

A gesture not of possession. Not of passion.

 

But of something stranger.

 

Something that almost felt like respect.

 

A flicker of heat curled up Agatha’s spine.

 

Not from the touch.

 

From the unfamiliar ache that followed it.

 

The queen straightened again.

 

Agatha said nothing.

 

But something in her had shifted.

 

She had braced for coldness. For cruelty. For indifference wearing a crown.

 

And instead, she got that.

 

A type of kiss that was not expected by duty but felt more powerful all the same.

 

It meant nothing.

 

And yet it felt like it meant everything.

 

She didn’t know what to make of it.

 

Didn’t know what to make of her.

 

No cheers followed. Only silence — brittle and uncertain.

 

Somewhere near the front, a whisper cut through the stillness like a knife.

 

Agatha didn’t look toward it. She kept her eyes forward, her face composed.

 

But her pulse thundered in her ears.

 

Because she didn’t know if what had just happened was an act of mercy—

 

Or a rejection.

 

——-

 

The question had been simple.

 

Do you enter into this union willingly?

 

The words still rang in her skull.

 

She’d known the answer before Agatha spoke it. She’d felt it in the silence that followed, in the way the girl’s shoulders had locked beneath the weight of her mother’s stare. That was not the pause of a bride gathering her breath. It was the pause of someone cornered.

 

And when the answer finally came—soft, obedient, wrong—it scraped against something raw inside Rio.

 

Her own “I do” had tasted like iron.

 

She’d said it anyway. For duty. For the River. For peace.

 

But as the officiator spoke the vows, the words curdled in her mouth. To lead. To command. To take what is offered and make it mine. They were relics from a time when victory was measured in possession. When unions were spoils.

 

She repeated them because her position demanded it. Because hesitation would be read as weakness.

And still, she felt sick.

 

The basin’s water had been cold, but when their hands touched, heat had pulsed up her arm—alive, startling, wrong. She’d kept her expression blank while the current twisted through her chest.

 

It didn’t stop.

 

Even now, standing before the crowd, her pulse thrummed unevenly. Something foreign had lodged itself beneath her ribs, moving with each beat.

 

She didn’t want to name it.

 

Couldn’t.

 

When the officiator offered the rings, she slid the silver one onto Agatha’s finger carefully, avoiding the tremor in her own hand. The gesture should have been meaningless, mechanical. It wasn’t. The sight of metal against pale skin looked final, binding in a way that no treaty had ever been.

 

Then the officiator uttered the last words—You may seal your union with a kiss—and she felt the entire hall tilt toward her.

 

Expectation.

 

She could feel it pressing against her spine, the crowd hungry for spectacle, for proof of dominance.

 

Agatha didn’t move.

 

For a heartbeat, neither did Rio.

 

She could have ended it cleanly— a formal kiss, brief and cold, the sort that satisfied ceremony. But her stomach turned at the thought of forcing it.

 

So she’d done the only thing that felt bearable. She’d taken Agatha’s hand, kissed the back of it, and hoped the crowd would see restraint as strength.

 

They hadn’t. The air curdled with whispers the instant she straightened.

 

Let them talk.

 

She’d endured worse.

 

But when she looked at Agatha—really looked—something twisted in her again. The girl wasn’t trembling anymore. She stood tall beside her, face unreadable, eyes bright with something Rio couldn’t name. Not defiance. Not fear.

 

Something closer to understanding.

 

Or danger.

 

The pulse beneath her ribs surged once more, hard enough to steal her breath.

 

It couldn’t be.

 

She’d read enough of the old texts to know what it meant—the pull, the warmth, the flicker that had burned through the water. A bond. A mark. A claiming of souls.

 

Impossible.

 

Unthinkable.

 

Unwelcome.

 

This was supposed to be political. Contained. Manageable.

 

Now it was none of those things.

 

Her heart knew it, even if she refused to. The River had tied her to someone she could not afford to want.

 

She imagined what would happen if anyone discovered it. The Council. The courts. The Order. A queen bound not only by law but by soul to another witch — an Unseen, no less.

 

They would not call it sacred. They would call it corruption.

 

They would say her magic had been compromised, her will entangled, her throne no longer her own.

 

The River’s favor would be questioned. The balance between Seen and Unseen — already fragile — would fracture.

 

The council would circle like vultures, her enemies whispering that Lady Death had been claimed.

 

And once the court began to believe she could be claimed, they would believe she could be replaced.

 

It would end her.

It would end them.

 

She forced her jaw to still.

 

The hall had begun to empty, courtiers whispering, attendants following in a ripple of fabric and perfume. Agatha moved beside her, the rustle of her gown soft against the stone.

 

Rio didn’t look at her again. She couldn’t.

 

She stood until the last echo of footsteps faded, the cold basin water drying on her skin, the mark beneath it burning like a brand.

 

She straightened her shoulders, the mask of composure sliding back into place.

 

This was a treaty, she told herself. A union. A necessity.

 

Nothing more.

 

And yet, as she turned to leave the altar, the phantom warmth of Agatha’s hand lingered against her palm, refusing to fade.

 

——-

 

The celebrations had been moved to the grand dining hall. Where rows of tables lined the floor piled high with food and wines. A great waste if you asked Agatha.

 

The silence and vows were traded for food and laughter — or something that resembled laughter. The sound of silverware and strained pleasantries filled the great dining chamber, its vaulted ceilings glittering with banners from both kingdoms.

 

Agatha sat beside her new wife at the head of the table.

 

A golden crown weighed against her temples. The scent of roasted meats and river herbs clung thick in the air, yet she found herself unable to eat. Her stomach hadn’t quite forgiven her for the morning.

 

To her left, the queen sat in perfect silence.

 

Her posture was immaculate — straight-backed, composed, as though carved from the same stone as the throne itself. The candlelight slid across her armour trimmed coat and the curve of her jaw, catching on the dark braid that fell against one shoulder.

 

It was long — longer than Agatha had seen anyone in her kingdom keep their hair before. It would probably reach her hip bone if unbraided. The braid itself was intricate, almost ceremonial in pattern, threaded through with fine silver cords that caught the light each time she moved.

 

In Agathas kingdom braids were a private thing. Or something only common ladies wore openly. A woman unbraided her hair only in solitude — before bathing, before sleep, or before surrender. To wear it so openly was considered intimate. Improper, even.

 

And yet on her, it didn’t look improper at all.

 

It looked deliberate. Regal. Powerful.

 

The contrast unsettled Agatha more than she wanted to admit. There was something striking about the way the braid framed her face — too soft for a warlady, too human for the legends that followed her.

 

Agatha tore her gaze away before the queen could notice.

 

They hadn’t spoken a word to each other.

 

That that it bothered Agatha. It didn’t.

 

She traced the edge of her goblet with her thumb, watching the ripples shiver across the wine. Around them, courtiers from both realms conversed politely, glancing toward the head table now and then to gauge how well peace had taken root.

 

It hadn’t.

 

Not yet, anyway.

 

When Agatha finally glanced toward her mother, she found her watching — eyes sharp, lips pressed in that familiar warning curve. A pointed flick of her gaze toward the queen. Then back to Agatha.

 

Speak.

 

Agatha inhaled silently, schooling her face. Forced conversation in front of half a court — the very thing she detested. But disobedience would bring questions she couldn’t afford.

 

She angled slightly toward the queen, catching the profile carved in candlelight — calm, composed, unknowable.

 

“So,” she began, steadying her voice, “is it true that the rivers in your kingdom glow blue at night?”

 

The queen blinked, as though pulled back from somewhere deep. “Only near the northern cliffs.”

 

Her voice held a low, smooth certainty. A sound that made Agatha’s pulse flicker against her will.

 

“Minerals?” Agatha asked.

 

A brief pause. “No. The spirits,” the queen corrected softly.

 

Agatha nodded — but not blindly. “Do the Order say that,” she murmured, “or do you believe it?”

 

The queen’s gaze slid toward her, a flicker of surprise breaking through the mask. “Both,” she said slowly. “The cliffs are a sacred burial ground. The stories may exaggerate, but they have roots.”

 

Agatha hummed, turning her goblet. “Stories always do.”

 

The queen’s expression changed — tiny, but there. Not quite approval. More like recognition.

 

Encouraged, Agatha added, “And the myth about your soldiers? The blindfold training?”

 

That earned a sharper reaction — the queen’s eyes cutting toward her, startled, amused for half a heartbeat before she quelled it.

 

“No,” she said. “Pure invention.”

 

Agatha allowed a faint smile. “Pity. My mother speaks of it like scripture.”

 

The queen exhaled — almost a laugh, almost disbelief. “It would be inefficient. And deadly.”

 

“So the truth is less… dramatic?” Agatha asked lightly.

 

The queen considered that. “The truth is quieter,” she answered. “Which is why it’s easier to miss.”

 

Agatha met her gaze — brief, assessing. “Or easier to twist,” she said, echoing the subtle edge in the queen’s voice.

 

That surprised the queen again. A small tilt of her head. A softening at the edges, quickly concealed.

 

Her mother’s approving nod from across the table made her want to throw a knife at her.

 

After that, the conversation dissolved.

 

Agatha’s food went cold. She caught herself glancing at the queen more than once — not because she meant to, but because she couldn’t seem to help it. There was something unreadable about her.

 

She’d expected cruelty. Arrogance. Control.

 

Instead, she found quiet.

 

Too quiet.

 

Each time she thought of something to say, the words dried up on her tongue. The queen’s expression gave nothing back, and yet… Agatha couldn’t shake the feeling that beneath all that stillness, there was movement.

 

Something restless.

 

Something alive.

 

Eventually, she gave up trying. She sat back in her chair, lifting her wine in mock celebration as the courtiers’ laughter swelled around them.

 

Let her be silent, she told herself. It’s not as if I care.

 

And yet, when the music began to rise from the far end of the hall — soft, distant, carrying the first notes of what would soon become their dance — Agatha found herself stealing one last glance.

 

The queen hadn’t moved.

 

Her hand hovered over her goblet, untouched. Her eyes were on the far wall, but her mind was somewhere far beyond it.

 

For someone who ruled with such control, she looked utterly lost.

 

And Agatha couldn’t decide if that made her feel sorry for her. Or just more intrigued.

 

———

 

From the outside, she knew she looked composed.

The perfect picture of control — head high, hands still, posture unyielding.

 

But inside, everything was unraveling.

 

Every breath, every heartbeat, every accidental brush of Agatha’s sleeve was a reminder of the bond — of that quiet, burning pulse beneath her skin that refused to fade. She could feel it humming between them, faint but constant, like an invisible thread tugging whenever Agatha shifted in her chair.

 

It was unbearable.

 

She kept her eyes fixed ahead, because if she looked at her wife — at the woman she was not supposed to want, not supposed to feel anything for — she was certain the entire hall would see it written across her face.

 

The council. The nobles. The River Order.

They couldn’t find out.

 

Not now. Not ever.

 

She was already calculating contingencies — how to contain this, how to make it manageable. Layers of routine. Distance. Control. If she kept everything tightly ordered, perhaps the bond would fade into the background, forgotten beneath the rhythm of governance and duty.

 

And she’d have to protect Agatha — not because she wanted to, but because it was the right thing to do. The girl hadn’t chosen any of this. She shouldn’t have to pay for Rio’s mistakes or for the River’s cruelty.

 

The court would already be watching her for weakness. If they sensed anything unusual — if they even suspected something sacred had passed between them — the rumors would spread like wildfire.

 

A queen bound to another witch was not a symbol of unity.

 

It was a threat. A weapon.

 

And in the end, that weapon would be turned on the one least able to survive it.

 

So she sat still.

 

She didn’t trust herself to speak — not without giving something away. Her silence wasn’t disinterest, it was survival. Every word felt like stepping into open flame.

 

Still, when Agatha spoke — hesitant, yes, but curious, perceptive, reaching toward her in a way Rio hadn’t expected — Rio had to fight the absurd urge to actually answer her. To let the stiffness drop for a moment. To smile. To try.

 

It was ridiculous, truly.

The great Queen Vidal, breaker of sieges, terror of half the realm, undone by a girl asking questions at a dinner table.

 

But Agatha’s questions weren’t foolish.

They weren’t vapid court pleasantries.

They were clever, probing in the gentlest way, hinting at a mind far sharper than her gilded dress suggested.

 

Rio felt something shift inside her.

Interest she could not afford.

 

She kept her expression composed, cool, distant. She forced herself to sit as the queen they expected — the one who revealed nothing, who felt nothing, who could not be read.

 

But as the musicians lifted their instruments at the far end of the hall, their chords swelling into the air like a tide, Rio realized something unsettling.

 

Her pulse was still tangled with Agatha’s.

 

Steady.

Relentless.

And entirely impossible to ignore

 

——-

 

They had moved to the ballroom — though to Rio, it felt less like a celebration and more like an inspection.

 

Light poured through the crystal sconces, scattering against the mirrored walls until the room shimmered like water. A full orchestra waited near the dais, bows poised, the first notes trembling in the air but not yet released.

 

The courtiers had already begun to circle, eager for spectacle.

 

Rio stood near the head of the room, spine straight, jaw set. The weight of her crown felt heavier than it had during the ceremony.

 

She knew what came next.

Every royal wedding ended the same way — with a first dance to mark unity before the court.

 

She could already hear Lilia’s voice in her head. You cannot refuse. To do so would insult the treaty, and worse — her.

 

Still, she found herself inching toward the nearest exit as attendants bustled past with trays of wine. If she moved carefully, perhaps no one would notice—

 

“Your Majesty.”

 

She froze. It was Lord Cyril, one of her senior advisors — sharp-eyed, the kind who noticed everything. He gave a slight bow, then murmured low enough for only her to hear.

 

“The court expects the dance. And a reminder Your Majesty, that tonight particularly the envoys of the River Order are watching. We would hate to stir any problems with the High Mother.”

 

Rio’s gaze flicked toward the far side of the room.

 

Sure enough, the silver robed envoys stood in quiet formation near the marble pillars, their expressions unreadable, their attention fixed squarely on her. Their presence coiled around her like a net.

 

Of course they were here.

Of course they would report everything — her poise, her restraint, the way she looked at her bride.

 

She swallowed hard. Refusal wasn’t an option.

 

The master of ceremonies stepped forward and raised his voice, echoing through the hall.

 

“Honored guests, it is tradition that the union of the Vidal and the Harkness line be sealed not only by vow, but by grace.

Her Majesty, Queen Rio Vidal of the Riverlands, and Her Grace, Lady Agatha Vidal, will now open the first dance.”

 

Lady Agatha Vidal.

 

The title hit her harder than she expected — strange, final, tethered to a future she hadn’t meant to claim.

 

She drew a breath that barely filled her lungs, then stepped forward.

 

The crowd parted like a tide.

 

Across the polished floor, Agatha stood waiting — still and uncertain, hands clasped lightly before her.

 

Rio bowed slightly — a gesture of formality, nothing more — and extended her gloved hand.

 

For a moment, Agatha didn’t move. Then she placed her fingers lightly in Rio’s palm.

 

The orchestra struck its first note.

 

——-

 

The music rose, slow and swelling, like a tide filling the room.

 

For a heartbeat, neither of them moved.

 

Agatha could feel the weight of every gaze in the hall pressing against her — nobles, courtiers, envoys — all waiting to see what kind of union this would be. But her attention caught instead on the woman standing across from her.

 

The queen. Her now wife.

 

The dark eyed woman extended a hand — smooth, controlled, palm upturned beneath those black leather gloves. A barrier, intentional or not. A choice. Agatha’s mouth twitched, a smile she didn’t let fully form.

 

So she didn’t wish to touch her skin.

Well. The feeling was mutual.

 

Perhaps the queen should have reconsidered before paying a hundred times a traditional dowry for her.

 

The hesitation before she had extended her hand hadn’t escaped Agatha’s notice. Nor had the taut line of her jaw when their names were spoken together. For one raw, absurd instant, Agatha had thought she might actually turn and leave.

 

The pang that shot through her chest was small, sharp, humiliatingly quick. She smoothed her expression before it had a chance to surface.

 

It didn’t matter.

She was used to distance.

Used to people recoiling before they even knew her.

 

She placed her hand in the queen’s.

 

The touch was brief, gloved, formal — and yet something under the surface warmed unpleasantly, as if answering some unseen shift in the air.

 

They moved.

 

The first sweep of the dance was measured, textbook, palms joined as they circled each other beneath the blaze of chandeliers. The polished floor gleamed like water catching moonlight. Her body moved on instinct — posture perfect, steps exact, the result of years pressed into her bones by tutors who demanded flawlessness.

 

Control.

That she could do.

That she could survive.

 

Her chest tightened, breath thinning despite her composure. She disguised it well — timing each inhale with the music — but the edges of her vision wavered, just slightly.

 

Focus.

Left foot. Turn. Step.

 

When she risked a glance upward, she found the queen’s eyes on her.

 

Not distracted.

Not polite.

Focused. Keen.

 

Noticing.

 

No one had ever noticed before.

 

Surely she hadn’t. Surely it was coincidence, just the queen maintaining her focus—

 

But those eyes didn’t waver.

 

Agatha’s breath stuttered and then she released the breath all at once, too quickly.

 

To cover the moment, she forced a smile — small, crooked, dangerous — and leaned in slightly, her voice just audible over the music.

 

“You’re awfully tense, Your Majesty,” she murmured. “Surely I’m not that frightening.”

 

The queen’s grip faltered — a barely-there slip of control, but Agatha felt it.

 

Encouraged, Agatha’s smirk deepened. “Don’t worry,” she added softly, “I promise not to step on your toes. Much.”

 

A flicker — something that almost looked like surprise — crossed the queen’s face. Then, unbelievably, a glint of amusement.

 

“See?” Agatha went on, stepping into the next turn with unexpected grace. “Not so bad, is it? All that fuss for nothing.”

 

The queen’s expression shifted — the briefest tug at her mouth, gone almost before Agatha could be sure she’d seen it.

 

And yet, for that single breath between beats, the tension in the room changed.

It wasn’t the bond exactly — though she could feel it, thrumming beneath her skin, answering the rhythm of the music. It was something simpler. Stranger.

 

The sense that, despite the formality, despite the distance, they were suddenly seeing each other.

 

——

 

It should have been easy.

A series of practiced steps. A public performance.

 

Instead, Rio couldn’t remember when her last clean breath had been.

 

The room blurred around the edges, gilded faces blending with candlelight. All she could focus on was the woman before her — the deliberate way she moved, the precision of her form, the quiet defiance tucked between every graceful motion.

 

She’d expected fragility. What she found was force.

 

When their hands met, the warmth that sparked through her gloves startled her. The bond again — that low, insistent pulse that refused to fade. She could almost feel it beneath the music, as if the River itself were keeping time between their steps.

 

She’d nearly refused the dance. She still wanted to. Every nerve in her body screamed retreat. But the envoys of the River Order were watching, their silver robes gleaming in her periphery, and she couldn’t give them more reason to whisper.

 

So she held her posture, moved as the court expected — and tried not to think about how close they were.

 

Then Agatha looked up.

 

Their eyes met.

 

For an instant, Rio’s rhythm broke — just slightly — because she saw something she hadn’t expected. Not fear. Not obedience. Something sharper.

 

Then she noticed the pattern — the faint tremor at Agatha’s throat, the way her shoulders tensed before each inhale. Holding her breath.

 

And then it stopped.

 

The sudden release was small but enough to draw Rio’s attention. She hadn’t meant to stare, hadn’t meant to notice at all, but something about it struck her.

 

Before she could decide what to do, Agatha spoke.

 

“You seem tense, Your Majesty.”

 

The words were light. Teasing. Irreverent.

For a second, Rio forgot to breathe altogether.

 

She glanced down, startled, certain she must have misheard — but the look in Agatha’s eyes left no room for confusion.

 

Then came the smirk. The whisper. The promise not to step on her toes.

 

It was absurd. In the middle of ceremony, with half the realm watching, Agatha Harkness — Lady Vidal — was making jokes.

 

And somehow, Rio felt the laugh rise before she could stop it. It caught in her throat instead, softened into a breath that almost became a smile.

 

Almost.

 

She reined it in, her composure snapping back into place as the music swelled.

 

She couldn’t afford softness. Not here. Not now.

 

But as they turned again — her hand steady on Agatha’s waist, the bond burning faintly beneath her glove — she couldn’t shake the feeling that something had shifted.

 

That she’d just glimpsed the woman behind the veil, and she wasn’t at all what Rio had been prepared for.

 

The music swelled, and the air itself seemed to move with it.

 

Rio adjusted her grip, one hand at Agatha’s waist, the other guiding hers, and for the first time that day the world stopped watching. Or maybe she simply stopped caring that it was.

 

The orchestra’s rhythm deepened — a waltz spun faster than decorum should have allowed, the kind meant to dizzy its dancers.

 

Agatha followed every turn effortlessly. The silk of her skirts flared like water catching light, brushing against Rio’s legs as they moved. Their steps fell into perfect time — cautious at first, then surer, sharper.

 

A pivot. A sweep.

The floor gleamed beneath them like the surface of a river, their reflections chasing one another across polished marble.

 

Rio led, and Agatha moved with her — not resisting, not yielding, but matching. Each motion mirrored the other’s will.

 

The orchestra rose higher. She turned Agatha beneath her arm, silver chains caught the light like rain. A dip followed — Rio’s palm steady at her back, the scent of river salt and candle smoke between them.

 

Her heartbeat matched the tempo — fast, pulsing, alive.

 

Every nerve seemed to hum with the current that ran through the hall, through her veins, through the woman in her arms.

 

The bond thrummed beneath her ribs, undeniable. It was there in the way their steps synced perfectly, in the way Agatha’s breath brushed her throat as she spun back into her hold.

 

She didn’t have to see it to know. The River was moving through them.

 

They were the Source and the Confluence, circling, colliding, becoming one current for a fleeting, impossible moment.

 

The music reached its crest — a rush of strings, a heartbeat of silence, and then the final note fell.

 

Rio’s hand was still at Agatha’s waist.

Agatha’s fingers still rested against her shoulder.

 

The hall erupted in polite applause.

 

Neither of them moved.

 

For that suspended breath between ending and separation, Rio swore she could still feel the River in her pulse — and Agatha’s matching it beat for beat.

 

——-

The applause still thundered in Agatha’s ears long after the last step of the dance. The queen had slipped from her grasp; the music had stopped; nobles were bowing, clapping, whispering. And Agatha just… stood there.

 

Heart pounding. Breath shallow. Skin too warm.

 

That had been—

 

No.

She wasn’t going to think about it.

Not here.

Not with half the world watching.

Not ever, if she could help it.

 

She forced her hands to still at her sides, willing her pulse to quiet. But her body had other plans — everything felt too alive, too aware. And before she could process any of it, a sharp hand latched onto her elbow.

 

Her mother.

 

“Come,” she hissed, voice pleasant enough for the public, talons underneath. “You are needed.”

 

Needed.

Of course she was.

 

Agatha let herself be steered through a side door, down a corridor thick with candlelight. The second the door clicked shut behind them, attendants descended like a flock — swift, silent, invasive.

 

Her crown was lifted away.

Her veil unfastened.

Her jewels stripped.

Her heavy embroidered gown peeled off layer by layer until she stood in nothing but her shift, naked beneath her mother’s scrutiny.

 

Her mother took her chin in a hard grip.

 

“Stop looking like a startled doe,” she muttered. “You will behave. You will complete the rite. You will not embarrass this family again.”

 

Again.

 

The word lodged like a splinter.

 

Agatha said nothing.

There was nothing safe to say.

 

Her attendants painted her again — not in golds and ochres this time, but in something softer. A glow to her cheeks, a touch of color to her lips. A temptation dressed up as duty. Then they slid her into an almost sheer ceremonial nightgown — elegant, expensive, humiliatingly fragile. The kind meant to display rather than protect.

 

Her mother stepped back, satisfied.

 

“Good,” she said. “Remember what is at stake. When you leave that chamber, there must be no doubt.”

 

No doubt about what, exactly, she didn’t bother to clarify. She never had to. Agatha had grown up on those unsaid conditions, on the things implied rather than spoken aloud.

 

Agatha swallowed back the tightness in her throat. Not fear. Something deeper — anger, maybe. Or resignation. Or the old, familiar sense of standing at the edge of a role someone else had written for her.

 

Her mother led her through another corridor. The air shifted — cooler, heavier, thick with incense. She recognized the place before the doors even opened.

 

When they did, Agatha found her wife already inside.

 

The other woman stood rigid, flanked by a cluster of advisors and silver robed witnesses — a Tidekeeper among them, clutching ritual scrolls. Agatha’s mother moved near the back, examining her daughter’s appearance with clinical precision, as if assessing a negotiation rather than a person.

 

Agatha’s breath caught, but only for a moment.

 

This was expected.

This was duty.

This was the part she had steeled herself for — the part she had quietly made peace with long before tonight. Blood, sheets, proof. There had never really been another path.

 

The Tidekeeper stepped forward, voice carrying the heavy cadence of rehearsed scripture.

 

“To complete the union before the eyes of the River, the rites must now be sealed.”

 

Murmurs flickered through the room like sparks. The queen’s advisors took their positions; attendants lowered their gazes; Agatha’s mother’s chin lifted in satisfaction.

 

This was happening.

 

Agatha swallowed once. She hated it. But she had prepared herself. She would endure. She always endured.

 

Then the queen spoke.

 

“No.”

 

The entire room snapped still.

 

Agatha blinked, stunned. Advisors gasped softly. The Tidekeeper froze mid breath. One of the queen’s councilors leaned forward as if to correct her.

 

“My Queen,” he whispered urgently, “the rite must—”

 

The queen didn’t raise her voice.

She didn’t need to.

 

“I do not believe this step is necessary tonight.”

 

Agatha stared at her.

 

What are you doing?

Why now? Why here? Why like this?

 

Humiliation rose hot and sharp in her chest. She did not want this ritual — she dreaded it — but she had prepared herself. She was ready to do her duty. She had braced for it, swallowed it, folded it into the shape of inevitability.

 

Why couldn’t this woman just do the same and be done with it?

 

Where did she get off embarrassing her?

 

The room was painfully silent. This was a disaster waiting to happen. Not completing the rites could mean the entire union might be seen as illegitimate. That this would all have been for nothing.

 

Agatha did not go through all of this for nothing.

 

Agatha stepped forward.

 

“If I may speak—”

 

All eyes bar one pair in the room glared at her.

 

The queen’s voice cut through the room like a blade.

“You may.”

 

Silence swallowed everything.

 

Agatha lifted her chin — slowly, deliberately — and when she turned toward the queen, her sweet expression was a masterpiece of courtly poison.

 

“With all due respect, Your Majesty,” she said, tone dripping honey, “while I appreciate your… concern… I also appreciate that the rites must be completed. I would hate to insult the Divine Mother, or the River’s councils, on our wedding night.”

 

Her words seemed polite.

But her eyes leveled the queen like a knife.

 

She walked toward her wife — unhurried, graceful, commanding her own body despite the humiliation coiling in her gut — and extended her hand.

 

“I am prepared to fulfil my duty,” she said softly. “Whenever you are.”

 

Dark eyes looked at her as though she were something entirely unexpected.

 

For a moment, the queen almost refused again.

 

Agatha’s fingers remained outstretched.

Her gaze sharpened.

 

The other woman inhaled slowly — one quiet surrender of breath — and took her hand.

 

“Of course,” she said, voice tight. “If my consort sees it fit… we may commence.”

 

A collective exhale washed across the room. Relief. Tension ebbing. Advisors straightening. The Tidekeeper’s shoulders lowering as she muttered a prayer of thanks under her breath.

 

Together, Agatha and her wife crossed into the inner chamber.

 

The door shut behind them with a heavy finality.

 

And the moment they stepped inside the candlelit room — away from the witnesses, away from the eyes — they dropped each other’s hands like the touch had burned.

 

The queen turned on her first.

 

“Why would you do that?”

 

Her voice was quiet — almost calm — but Agatha felt the blade under every syllable. Precision like that could cut deeper than shouting.

 

Agatha crossed her arms, nails digging into her sleeves to mask the tremor in her hands.

 

Why would she do that?

Why would she save them both from being the laughingstock of two kingdoms?

Why would she protect a woman she had met only hours ago?

 

The fury rose before she could cage it.

 

“Why would I save us from certain humiliation?” she shot back, her tone sweet as honey and sharp as cracked glass. “Such a mystery.”

 

The queen stared at her.

Not angry.

Not cold.

 

Just startled.

Like she’d never been spoken to like this in her life.

 

Agatha hated that she noticed.

 

The queen stepped forward, jaw tight. “I wasn’t going to let them dictate what we—”

 

“What we?” Agatha echoed, stepping toward her despite every instinct screaming Don’t you dare challenge her. “We, who met not half a day ago? We who just swore eternal loyalty in front of two realms and the River’s envoys?”

 

She didn’t wait for an answer — couldn’t.

If she stopped talking, she might stop breathing.

 

“You nearly jeopardised the entire union,” she said, low, steady. “The council would never recognise the marriage as sealed without the rites. Not without the blood of my—”

 

She cut herself off too late.

Her voice lodged in her throat.

 

The queen flinched.

 

Agatha wasn’t prepared for the satisfaction that flickered under her ribs at that — tiny, shameful, burning bright.

 

She pressed on before she lost her nerve.

 

“Is that not what you wanted?” she demanded. “When you bought me?”

 

The queen’s jaw clenched.

A muscle twitched in her cheek — the first crack in her mask.

 

“Well,” Agatha went on, mock formal, as if announcing herself to a court, “here I am. On your arm. In your chamber. Ready to complete the union you paid a kingdom’s ransom for. So what changed, Your Majesty?”

 

The title hit the cold air like venom.

 

The queen didn’t answer.

Her silence was heavy enough to break ribs.

 

Agatha stepped closer — though her heart hammered so hard it felt like it might bruise itself against her ribs. She couldn’t stop. She had held her tongue her whole life, and now here was a woman powerful enough to ruin her who still hadn’t struck her down for speaking.

 

Something in that was intoxicating.

 

The queen’s jaw tightened. When she finally spoke, her voice was low, almost hoarse. “This was not what this union was supposed to be about.”

 

“Then what is this about?” Agatha pressed. “Because I have no wish to be paraded like breeding stock either, but I came prepared to do my duty. You—” she jabbed a finger toward the queen’s chest before she lost her courage, “—are the one acting above it all.”

 

The queen’s voice came out low, controlled. “This custom—”

 

“—is tradition,” Agatha snapped. “To you it’s archaic. To my people, it’s sacred.”

 

Her voice wavered, barely, but she forced steel into it.

 

“You humiliated me,” she said. “Before them. Before my mother. Before the River. Do you know what they will whisper?”

 

The queen swallowed — actually swallowed — like the words hit her lungs instead of her ears.

 

Agatha didn’t let up.

 

“They’ll say I was too impure to touch. That a queen refused her bride on sight. That I was defective, or worse — dangerous.”

 

She scoffed, folding her arms tighter to hide the way her fingers were shaking.

 

“Say the word, and they’ll write songs about your noble restraint and my unlovable defects.”

 

The queen didn’t move.

 

She stood in the center of the chamber, rigid, breathing with that careful precision that looked like she was trying not to break something — or someone.

 

“I wasn’t trying to shame you.”

 

Agatha let out a hollow, humourless laugh. “Of course you weren’t.”

 

“I truly wasn’t.” The queen’s voice was quiet, strained. “Where I’m from, a delayed rite is an inconvenience. A scandal, at worst. People gasp, then move on.”

 

Agatha scoffed softly. “That’s lovely for your people. Here it’s a stain. A curse. A confirmation that I’m exactly what they think I am.”

 

Her voice tightened.

 

“They would blame me. Not you.”

 

The queen flinched — just a fraction, but Agatha saw it.

 

“I didn’t realise the weight of it,” the queen said. “In your kingdom. For you. I didn’t…”

Her jaw tensed, as if forcing the truth out cost her something.

“I should have. I am deeply sorry.”

 

Agatha’s breath hitched — barely noticeable, but real. Her anger loosened by inches, like fingers slowly unclenching around a blade.

 

No one ever apologised to her.

Aside from perhaps Jen.

 

Her mother certainly never had.

Advisors never had.

Suitors never had.

People didn’t apologise to girls like her.

 

She didn’t know where to put the feeling that rose in her chest — too soft, too unfamiliar, too dangerous.

 

The fight inside her — hot, sharp, desperate — wavered.

Sputtered.

Softened.

 

“Whatever,” she muttered, though the heat had drained from her voice. She rubbed at her wrist, suddenly aware of how tired she felt. “If you’re so set on not doing this, then…”

 

A shaky breath escaped her before she could mask it.

 

“Then I will not force you.”

 

The queen’s shoulders eased — not fully, but enough to betray relief. And guilt. And something quieter that Agatha couldn’t yet name.

 

Silence settled over them again, but this one felt less like a blade and more like a bruise — tender, aching.

 

Agatha looked down at her hands, fingers curling, uncurling, trying to find steadiness.

 

“But the rite needs to be sealed,” she said at last, voice low but resolved. “One way or another.”

 

The words hung between them like a drop of blood about to fall.

 

——

 

Agatha didn’t wait for her to respond.

 

She turned with a kind of eerie composure, the storm inside her gone still in that frightening way a sea goes silent before a wave breaks. Her steps toward the ceremonial bed were measured, almost calm.

Rio exhaled — not fully, but enough that her shoulders eased for a heartbeat.

 

Until she saw the glint of silver between Agatha’s fingers.

 

Her entire body went taut.

 

“What are you doing?”

The words tore out before she realized she’d moved.

 

Agatha didn’t flinch. Didn’t hesitate. Didn’t give herself the mercy of fear.

 

She dragged the jagged hairpin across her palm in one clean, practiced motion.

 

Rio’s breath stopped.

 

“Making it believable,” Agatha said quietly, letting her blood fall in slow, crimson drops onto the pristine white sheet.

 

Rio halted a few paces away.

 

Not because of the blood.

Because of the conviction in her voice.

Because of the calm in her movements.

Because this girl — this stranger — was willing to wound herself rather than invite shame onto either of them.

 

Who was she?

 

“That’s—” Rio began, but her throat tightened, the word catching. Smart? Quick thinking? Making Rio feel things she should not be feeling?

 

She stepped closer, voice low, steadier than she felt.

“Give me your hand.”

 

Agatha lifted a brow, breath thin. “Why?”

 

“It’ll look suspicious if the wound is clean,” Rio murmured, already reaching before she could talk herself out of it. “Let me. Please.”

 

The please startled her.

And startled Agatha even more.

 

Agatha hesitated — just a small hitch of breath, just the faintest flicker of… something. As if no one had ever asked her for anything gently.

 

Then, slowly, she offered her hand.

 

Rio took it with a care she hadn’t planned on giving.

The cut was bright, pooling in Agatha’s palm.

Rio pressed her fingertips to the wound, and her magic rose instinctively — a warm current under her skin, soft, steady.

 

The wound knitted closed.

 

But something else moved too.

 

A flicker beneath the skin.

A pulse.

Soft, deep, ancient.

 

The bond.

 

It surged like a buried ember catching flame.

 

Rio froze.

Agatha’s breath caught.

For a moment too long, neither of them moved.

 

Their eyes met.

 

And the world seemed to inhale.

 

Then Agatha tore her hand back.

 

“Thanks,” she said—stiff, clipped, as though politeness might smother the spark burning under her skin. As though Rio hadn’t felt it too.

 

Rio said nothing. She couldn’t.

 

Agatha turned away and folded down the blood-marked sheet with mechanical precision. Then she climbed into the ceremonial bed, spine straight, shoulders rigid with exhaustion and something quieter.

 

“I’ll take the bed,” she said coolly, “since you’re so determined to be a gentleman.”

 

Rio stood motionless.

 

Then she nodded once — a silent surrender to a battle she didn’t understand.

 

“Of course.”

 

She retreated into the adjoining chamber, cloak whispering across stone as she forced each step to remain measured, regal, steady. Her mind was anything but.

 

The door closed quietly between them.

 

On one side of the wall, Agatha lay stiff beneath the thin blanket, her hand pressed to her chest as if she could calm what burned there.

 

On the other side, Rio stood in the dark, palm pressed flat to her sternum, fingers resting over the exact same spot.

 

Neither saw the other.

Neither spoke.

 

But both reached for the same mark beneath their ribs.

And both felt it warm.
