Chapter 5: In The Morning Light

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Agatha heard her leave in the early hours of the morning.

 

The queen moved like someone trained to vanish before being noticed — steps light, breath held, the faint whisper of fabric passing through a room without disturbing a single thing. Another woman might have slept through it. Agatha did not. Her eyes had been open long before the queen rose from the sofa in the adjoining chamber.

 

She had barely slept at all.

Neither had the queen.

 

She knew this not because she had made a sound — far from it. The woman was so unnervingly quiet Agatha would have doubted she was truly there, would have assumed she’d imagined the presence entirely, if not for the mark.

 

That damn, cursed, restless mark.

 

It had throbbed under her ribs through every hour of the night, a soft, insistent tug that refused to let her forget she was not alone. It was as if the River itself disapproved of the wall between them — reminding her, with every pulse, that someone lay just beyond it. Someone she did not understand. Someone she had shouted at. Someone she now feared she had offended beyond repair.

 

Every time she drifted toward sleep, the bond stirred warm and sharp, tugging her awake again.

Too close.

Not close enough.

It hummed like a warning. Or a reprimand. Or simply a presence refusing to be ignored.

 

Agatha lay on her back staring at the ceiling, hand pressed flat over the blanket where she knew the mark lay beneath. It still felt warm. Tender. Almost bruised.

 

So by the time she heard the queen slip out, Agatha’s eyes were heavy, her pulse thin, her mind already picking apart every word she’d said the night before.

 

Had she gone too far?

Had she been foolish?

Had she spoken to a reigning queen with a sharpness unforgivable in any court — let alone a court such as hers?

 

Her stomach twisted.

 

Of course she had.

Of course she’d ruined something before it began.

 

The queen had every right to be furious. To avoid her. To plan whatever consequences awaited a bride who didn’t know when to hold her tongue.

 

And yet…she would have had the chance to punish Agatha last night. She could have done whatever she had wanted with her and no one would have so much as blinked.

But the queen hadn’t yelled.

She hadn’t struck her.

Hadn’t even looked angry.

 

She had apologised.

 

Agatha still didn’t know what to do with that — with the softness under the armor, the gentleness she kept stumbling into.

 

She flinched at the memory, at the way the queen’s fingers had touched her wounded hand — cautious, reverent — and how the mark had leapt like a live thing between them.

 

Agatha squeezed her eyes shut.

 

She didn’t want to think about that.

She didn’t want to think about her wife.

She didn’t want to think about the bond.

She especially didn’t want to think about how empty the chambers felt now that the queen was gone.

 

 

Agatha rose slowly, limbs heavy, as though sleep had finally desired to catch up with her the moment the queen slipped away. She wrapped a robe around her shoulders and crossed the floor barefoot, the stone cool beneath her feet. Dawn filtered through the high windows in a pale, uncertain light — the kind that made everything look soft and half imagined.

 

The washroom mirror fogged slightly with the morning chill. Agatha wiped a clear arc across its surface with trembling fingers.

 

Her own reflection stared back at her — pale, wide-eyed, hair  mussed from a night of restless turning. She looked too young. Too tired. Too bruised somewhere deep beneath the skin.

 

She would have to amend that.

 

But for that moment, she simply exhaled slowly.

 

Then, with hesitant fingers, she loosened the collar of her nightrobe and pulled the fabric aside.

 

There it was.

 

The mark.

 

It wasn’t the bright, searing brand spoken of in old tales — no violent flare, no regular carved letters spelling destiny. It was subtler, like moonlight caught beneath ice. A soft warmth radiated from it, small but insistent, pulsing with a rhythm in sync with another’s heartbeat.

Not quite script, not sigil, but something in between.

 

Old stories whispered that true soulmate marks carried the name of the other, but only in the language the River first spoke. A language scholars argued over, priests pretended to understand, but only the River itself truly remembered.

 

She couldn’t decipher it.

 

But she could feel it.

 

Could feel the weight of its importance. Pulling her in without choice.

 

She pressed her fingertips lightly to it.

Heat bloomed there.

Alive and aware.

 

Agatha snatched her hand back as if she’d touched a flame.

 

This wasn’t supposed to be her story.

Not her fate.

Certainly not with her — a queen she had only just met, a woman who confused her greatly. Someone who could kill her in a heartbeat and no one would dare say a word.

 

She gripped the edge of the basin to steady herself.

 

The mark pulsed again, it was a gentle pressure, like a hand reaching without touching.

 

It made something in her chest twist painfully.

 

“What do you want from me?” she whispered at her reflection, though she wasn’t sure whether she meant the mark… or the woman tied to it.

 

Her throat tightened.

Her eyes burned.

 

She hated this helpless feeling of not knowing, and even worse the unwanted hope threading itself through her fear.

 

Everything from the day before swirled in her mind:

 

The queen kneeling in front of her.

The queen untying her wrists with unexpected care.

The queen apologising to her.

The queen stopping the rite.

The queen’s expression… unreadable, restrained, impossible to name.

 

Agatha didn’t know what any of it meant.

 

Perhaps the mark did.

 

Judging by the way it kept pulsing — quiet, steady and impossible to ignore.

 

She closed her robe hurriedly, as if hiding it would smother whatever it was trying to say. It didn’t. The warmth seeped through the fabric, soft against her ribs.

 

Agatha swallowed hard and straightened her spine.

 

She would not let herself be undone by this.

By a mark she didn’t ask for.

By a bond she didn’t understand.

By a queen left her confused with every interaction.

By a marriage she was not ready for.

 

She forced her breathing into even counts — slow, practiced, the way she’d learned to survive her entire life. Composure layered itself over her like armor.

 

But when she looked into the mirror again, the truth still stared back at her:

 

She was afraid.

She was confused.

And despite everything, a part of her did care what her new wife thought of her.

 

A part she knew she desperately needed to silence.

 

 

Rio closed the door behind her with a careful hand, though she barely remembered walking there. Sleep had not touched her the entire night. She had lain still throughout, rigid on the sofa as the girl had demanded while she slept on the bed with only  a wall dividing them. The bond had thrummed beneath her ribs from the moment they separated. A restless, watchful thing.

 

Now, in the thin grey of dawn, it pulsed with a steadiness that made her throat tighten.

 

She stripped off her cloak and set it aside on the chair, then loosened the fastenings at her collar with mechanical precision. Her body moved by habit, her mind was elsewhere — pulled inevitably to the faint heat burning under her sternum.

 

It had grown stronger since she woke.

 

Of course it had.

 

She crossed the floor to the small mirror near the washbasin and forced herself to look.

 

The mark shimmered faintly beneath her skin, a soft ripple of light that wasn’t quite visible yet wasn’t entirely hidden.

 

As she looked closer, the glow resolved into lines — curved strokes, rising arcs, shaped exactly the way the River wrote its chosen truths.

 

She knew the language instantly.

 

The old tongue.

The first tongue.

The one spoken in every prophecy of binding.

 

Her stomach dropped.

 

Because the shape the light was forming…

Was a name.

Agatha Harkness.

 

Clear. Unmistakable.

Written in the River’s script across her ribs, as if carved there by fate itself.

 

Rio’s breath caught.

Her pulse stuttered.

She felt the world tilt beneath her feet.

 

This was real. It wasn’t a cruel nightmare. There was no escaping it.

 

She was in deeper trouble than she’d perhaps ever been in her life.

 

Her council would be in uproar if they knew.

The River Order would question her standing.

They would all look at Agatha as a distraction, a corruption, a threat to the throne.

 

She swallowed hard, jaw tightening until it ached.

 

This bond endangered them both.

 

But she could not tear her gaze from it.

 

Because beneath all the fear, something else gnawed at her — something she shoved quickly back into the dark space it belonged.

 

Her thoughts slid back, unwanted, to Agatha’s face the night before. Not the veiled bride.

Not the trembling girl forced beneath ceremony.

But the woman who had stood in front of her and spoke — openly, brazenly, sharply — as if Rio were not one of the most feared sovereign both sides of the River, but simply another person who needed to be told when she was wrong.

 

Rio ran a hand over her face.

 

She had expected meekness. Fear. Submission.

 

Instead she’d been met with wit, intelligence, and a political instinct sharp enough to cut.

 

She had understood the ramifications of refusing the rites  better than Rio herself had. And the way she had said it — not begging, not pleading, but stating facts with a sweetness that barely hid the blade beneath…

 

It had startled her. More than that, it had… impressed her.

 

She didn’t want to think of that though.

Didn’t want to taste the truth of it.

 

Rio shut her eyes as though that could make all her problems disappear. But in doing so another set of eyes seared into her mind. Blue, shockingly so.

 

A colour that caught light like riverwater at dusk.

 

But it wasn’t the colour that unsettled Rio.

 

It was the stories clearly stored in them.

 

Pain she recognized.

Fear she understood too well.

A defiance she had not yet seen in any noble bride.

A loneliness that mirrored something in herself she refused to touch.

 

She should have looked away quicker.

She shouldn’t have looked at all.

 

Rio tore her eyes open and stepped away from the mirror now, heart hitting a painful rhythm beneath the mark.

 

This could not continue.

Interest was a luxury she could not afford.

Attachment would only lead to ruin— for both of them.

 

She had to control this.

Had to distance herself.

Had to pretend none of this existed.

 

The girl could not know the effect she had.

The bond could not be given room to grow.

 

Rio steadied herself against the basin, breathing through her nose until the trembling under her ribs dulled to a muted throb.

 

Only then did she draw the washbasin nearer.

 

Breakfast.

She would be expected there.

Appearances mattered, especially here, especially now.

 

She could not appear looking like she’d spent the night wrestling ghosts, even if that was the truth.

 

Layer by layer, she rebuilt herself.

 

And only when every line sat perfectly, when her reflection resembled composure rather than chaos, did she allow herself one final breath.

 

She would see Agatha again soon.

 

She only hoped her pulse would not betray her.

 

 

By the time Agatha returned to her chambers, the early morning light had fully settled over the castle. Pale sun washed across the stone floors, cold and unforgiving, reflecting off of every polished surface.

 

The attendants had not lingered long in the ceremony room.

Three knocks.

A politely lowered gaze.

Hands moving with ritual precision as they collected the stained sheet.

 

They had said nothing.

Nothing but the formal phrase:

 

“Her Highness may return to her chambers.”

 

But silence had never shielded Agatha.

Silence could wound as deeply as words.

 

She had seen the pity in their eyes — soft, fleeting, but unmistakable. The kind people wore when they thought they were witnessing a tragedy from a safe distance. The queen had gone. The chamber had been empty save for Agatha herself. And though no one dared comment, every attendant had drawn the same conclusion:

 

The new queen consort had been left alone.  Unwanted.

 

Embarrassing.

 

Agatha swallowed hard, closing the door behind her.

 

The room felt too large, too quiet. Yesterday it had been buzzing with handmaidens and orders barked from her mother and the suffocating pressure of ceremony. Today it felt… hollow.

 

She crossed to the vanity and sat carefully, as though something might break if she moved too quickly.

 

Her reflection stared back — tired eyes, paler cheeks, faint smudges beneath her lashes from a night spent awake and afraid. The mark still warmed beneath her robe, but she refused to touch it again. She could not bear to look at it now, not while the understanding that every piece of her life teetered on the edge of someone else’s decisions pressed sharply on her mind.

 

The queen had not punished her last night.

 

But that didn’t mean she wouldn’t this morning.

 

Agatha picked up her comb, forcing her hand to steady. She tugged it through her hair slowly, careful not to snag or rush. She needed control. Needed order. Needed to appear once more like a woman who belonged by her side rather than someone the queen might regret choosing.

 

If the queen regretted this union…

 

If the queen decided Agatha had overstepped…

 

If the queen chose to withdraw her protection…

 

Agatha’s stomach twisted.

 

She could already imagine her mother’s expression.

The venom in her voice.

The humiliation.

The certainty that Agatha had failed at even this — the one chance she had to escape this miserable castle and the life laid out for her like a noose.

 

She could not let that happen.

She would not.

 

She set the comb down and let her fingers move on their own. A simple half up twist, nothing elaborate, nothing that resembled ceremony. Her curls fell naturally over her shoulders, the rest drawn back and fastened with a small silver clasp she had acquired  weeks ago and never once been allowed to wear.

 

The style framed her face just enough.

Not too soft.

Not too sharp.

A line between them.

 

Plain compared to yesterday’s lacquered, ornate  creation — but plain suited her. Plain felt like breath. Like reclaiming something small and hers after a day that had taken too much.

 

When she lifted her head again, the mirror returned a woman who did not beg for attention, yet was impossible to ignore entirely. A woman who could stand beside a queen without shrinking, without shining too brightly, without offering herself as a target.

 

A careful choice.

A precise one.

Exactly as she needed to be.

 

Next came the gown — midnight blue, the colour of deep water and quiet resolve. It fit her snugly, outlining her waist, grounding her in the shape of her own choosing. She smoothed the fabric as though it were a shield.

 

She added a pin at her collar.

Something subtle.

Something elegant.

Something that might catch the queen’s eye before a frown did.

 

Not to charm.

Not to entice.

Simply to avoid provoking.

 

Perfect.

Untouchable.

Harmless.

 

A woman a queen would not immediately regret binding herself to.

 

Agatha looked at her reflection once more. She saw the difference.

Yesterday she had been a dressed up relic of her mother’s design, a doll for ceremony.

Today she looked like herself — sharper, older, calmer, but no less frightened.

 

Frightened enough to move carefully.

Frightened enough to protect the fragile future laid before her.

Frightened enough to try.

 

She set her hands on the vanity’s edge and inhaled, long and slow.

 

When she crossed paths with the queen this morning, she would smile.

She would speak sweetly.

She would not provoke.

She would not remind her of last night.

 

She would survive this day.

She had to.

 

Agatha rose, straightened her skirt, and stepped toward the door.

 

Breakfast awaited.

 

And so did whatever judgment the queen had decided upon.

 

 

The morning hall was quiet when Rio arrived, quieter than she expected. A long table stood untouched beneath the high windows, sunlight catching on the silverware, the steam rising from the small assortment of dishes. Only two places had been set.

 

Rio paused.

 

She had assumed incorrectly that advisors or ceremonial witnesses would be present. That someone would mediate the strained aftermath of the Rite. Instead, the emptiness seemed to press in around her, too intimate for comfort.

 

She took her seat regardless, posture straight, hands folded neatly while she waited. The calm in her expression felt practiced, brittle at the edges.

 

Footsteps approached soon after.

 

Rio looked up and her breath caught.

 

Her wife stood there with a steadiness that far outdid how Rio herself was feeling inside.

 

Her hair was in loose curls that morning, unlike the elaborate coils of the day before. These were softer, more natural—half pulled back with a delicate twist and secured by a pretty clasp that caught the light as though it was crafted with that sole purpose in mind.

The gown she wore was plain compared to yesterday’s spectacle, but the simplicity of it suited her far better. Rio tried not to let her eyes linger in the way it hugged her hips in all the right spots. 

 

All the performance had been stripped away, yet Rio couldn’t but think she looked far more beautiful like this than before.

 

She dropped her gaze before the thought could linger, but the impression remained—too clear, too vivid.

 

Agatha took her seat.

 

The silence stretched at the table, weighing down until Rio heard herself speak.

 

“I…like your hair.”

 

As soon as the words slipped out, her pulse stumbled. She straightened her back, as if posture could disguise a lapse she had not meant to reveal.

 

***

 

Agatha lifted her eyes, startled. Compliments were rarely gifts in her world, they were usually tools.

 

“My hair?” she asked, the words careful, searching for what she might have missed.

 

“Yes.” Her Majesty’s tone was light, quickly masked. “It suits you.”

 

Agatha studied her—the queen’s stillness, the faint tension at the corner of her mouth. Nothing in the expression told her whether this was kindness, mockery, or some courtly subtlety she had not been trained to decipher.

 

“…Thank you?” she managed at last.

 

Something flickered across the queen’s face—quick, unreadable. Then she spoke again.

 

“It appears tidy.”

 

Agatha blinked, then nodded once. “Well I’m pleased it’s to Her Majesty’s standards.” She replied with a tight smile.

 

The queen said nothing more, only lifted her cup with a measured calm that made Agatha wonder whether she had somehow missed something entirely.

 

***

 

Gods why had she said that? She had definitely offended the younger woman.  Rio tried to steer the conversation into safer grounds.

 

She set her cup down, fingers steadying themselves against the porcelain.

 

“I admit,” she began, keeping her tone formal, “I thought we would be joined by others this morning.”

 

An expression passed through Agatha’s face before it was hastily smoothened away. “In my culture the first Mel is reserved for the couple alone after the Rite. To encourage unity I believe.”

 

Unity.

A harmless word on her tongue.

A far more perilous one in Rio’s mind.

 

She inclined her head in polite acknowledgment, though internally the thought was a quiet, sinking one:

 

A private meal. With her. And my mind that refuses to co-operate. Perfect.

 

The hall seemed smaller suddenly. Or perhaps it was only her breath.

 

Breakfast lay untouched between them, steam curling upward as if unsure whether it was welcome.

 

The strain between silence and speech stretched thin.

Not hostile.

Not warm.

Something uncertain, something new.

 

Rio reached for her utensils—anything to anchor herself—only to realize her hand wasn’t as steady as she’d hoped.

 

***

 

They ate in a quiet that felt almost ceremonial. Agatha took a sip of tea, gathering her thoughts. If they were expected to build unity, she might as well try. And she needed to learn more about the kingdom she was meant to call home before long.

 

She looked up, voice gentle but steady.

“Your Majesty… may I ask about your lands across the River?”

 

The queen stilled, her gaze lowered briefly to her plate before she answered. “Ask away.”

 

Not cold.

Not dismissive.

Just an opening — controlled, but still offered.

 

Agatha straightened subtly, encouraged by the permission though she wouldn’t show it.

“Are the forests truly as vast as the stories say? And the cities… I’ve heard they are unlike those in the East.”

 

She asked gently, but precisely.

Not idle curiosity — strategic curiosity.

The queen’s eyes lifted, a flicker of something like surprise crossing them.

 

She hadn’t expected Agatha to begin there — with land, with governance, with questions that revealed she was already imagining what life might look like beyond this castle.

 

Most new brides asked about gowns, duties, court etiquette.

Agatha asked about terrain and infrastructure.

 

“It is… temperate,” Rio answered.

A pause.

“Dense forests. Fertile land. The River binds most of it.”

Another beat.

“Our cities are… practical, but I think most would agree they’re beautiful too.”

 

Agatha’s gaze didn’t waver. She waited, patient, quietly urging more.

 

“It is peaceful,” Rio added at last, “when left to be.”

 

The admission felt too close to honesty. She looked away.

 

She watched the queen’s restraint with interest.

Tight answers, clipped but thoughtful. Agatha began to get the impression though that it was not out of lack of care, but perhaps caring too much.

Choosing words as if each one mattered.

 

Agatha tilted her head.

“I’ve always heard your forests are beautiful,” she offered softly.

“And your cities—innovative. Far beyond what is found here.”

 

The queen nodded carefully. “Yes, I would agree.”

 

She didn’t offer anymore than that and Agatha tried not to let the disappointment bother her.

 

The quiet stretched again, thinner now, charged in a different way.

 

Agatha set down her cup. Then because she was apparently unable to help herself she spoke again.

 

“Is Your Majesty always this quiet in the mornings?”

A beat passed. “Or is my company particularly uninspiring?”

 

The moment the words left her mouth, she regretted them.

Stop. Stop doing that.

 

***

 

The question hit her like a hand closing around her throat — not harsh, not painful, but sudden, startling, intimate.

 

It wasn’t the tease that undone her. It was the fact that Agatha was watching closely enough to tease at all. The fact that she had noticed her silence, interpreted it, reached into it.

 

It was being seen, plainly and without fear.

 

Rio blinked once, slowly. Her pulse quickened in a way she hadn’t felt in years. The edges of the room sharpened, every sound too crisp, the quiet too loud. Something inside her perked its head. She pushed it aside with haste.

 

Agatha waited for an answer — not mockingly, but what appeared to be earnest curiosity Rio was wholly unprepared for.

 

Rio felt the bond hum under her ribs, low and persistent, responding not to proximity but to attention.

 

She wished Agatha hadn’t asked.

She wished she could answer.

She wished she didn’t want to.

 

Because a part of her —a part she had been trying to bury — did want to speak with her. Did want to follow the thread of Agatha’s mind, see where her thoughts curved, what she noticed, what she feared, what she wanted.

 

She wanted to understand her.

 

And that was dangerous.

 

Her jaw tightened. Her breath came uneven for half a second before she forced it still.

 

“I am…”

The words caught.

She looked down at her hands as if they were betraying her.

 

She felt too exposed.

Too readable.

Too close to forgetting herself.

 

“I am finished,” she said quietly.

It was the safest truth she could offer.

 

She pushed her plate aside with a composure she did not feel.

 

***

 

Agatha blinked. Finished?

Her stomach dropped, her heart tightening with the familiar sting of humiliation.

 

You did it again. Always too sharp, always too much.

 

But the queen’s expression wasn’t cold. It was… strained. As if something had pressed too close.

 

Agatha wasn’t sure what to make of that.

 

Her Majesty rose.

 

“I should contact my advisors,” she said. “There are matters requiring attention.”

 

Polite. Formal. Too quick.

 

Agatha lowered her gaze, trying to salvage dignity.

“I doubt your people are so helpless they cannot manage without you for the span of a meal.”

 

She winced inwardly. Stop speaking. Stop speaking.

 

Her fingers tightened around her napkin, breath catching, but she refused to let it show. Fear would only make the moment crack wider, fear would make her small again, and she could not afford smallness now.

 

So she straightened.

 

Lifted her chin.

 

Let her spine lengthen by instinct alone.

 

Her heart hammered hard enough to rattle in her chest, but her face held steady. She raised her eyes to meet the queen’s, prepared for whatever expression waited there, because retreat would feel like surrender.

 

And that, she could not bear.

 

***

 

The words halted her footsteps.

 

She turned.

 

Slowly.

 

As if giving herself time to reassemble the lines of her face.

 

Agatha sat poised at the table, chin lifted, eyes steady. No fear. No shrinking. Not even a polite flinch. Just a calm challenge tucked beneath courtesy.

 

Rio’s gaze narrowed—not in anger, but in a stunned, startled focus.

 

This girl.

 

Bold enough to prod a queen mid exit.

Soft spoken enough to make it sound like nothing at all.

And wholly unaware of how close she stood to the edges of things she did not understand.

 

Something flickered warm and unwelcome beneath Rio’s sternum, the bond tightening like a pulled thread.

 

Agatha wasn’t afraid of her.

 

Not properly.

Not like everyone else.

Not the way she should be.

 

And that—the absence of fear—terrified Rio far more than any defiance ever could.

 

Because fear kept people alive around her.

Fear kept them distant.

Fear protected them from the gravity of her, from the curse of being tied to someone the whole world named a blade.

 

But Agatha looked at her as if she were merely a woman.

No monstrous shadow clinging to her crown.

No stain of legend.

 

Just a person.

 

It unsettled Rio so completely she almost forgot to breathe.

 

Intrigue curled at the edge of her thoughts, sharp as a thorn.

Amusement ghosted through her confusion—who teased the one the one the world referred to as Lady Death so lightly?

And beneath it all, a quiet dread.

 

She should be afraid.

For her own sake.

For mine.

 

Rio held her gaze one heartbeat longer than she meant to—long enough for the air between them to tighten.

 

Then she inclined her head, the only gesture she could trust not to betray her.

 

And turned away before those blue eyes could drag her deeper.

 

***

 

Agatha sat very still, watching the doorway long after the queen had gone.

 

Her cheeks burned.

Her pulse thudded at her throat.

 

She had no idea what that look had meant.

 

But it frightened her less than how much she suddenly wanted to understand it.

 

 

By the time Rio reached her chambers her mind was running faster than a race horse.

 

The door closed behind her with a muted finality, the sound softer than it should have been for how sharply it landed in her chest. For a moment she simply stood there, one hand still resting against the wood, breathing as though she had climbed too many stairs too quickly.

 

Breakfast lingered in her nerves like an echo.

Agatha’ voice.

Her eyes.

That look — sharp, curious, unafraid in a way that unsettled her more than fear ever could.

 

Rio began to get the creeping suspicion she was wholly unequipped to deal with the anomaly that was her new wife.

 

She had crossed the room to where her mirror still lay.

 

This was truly all Lilia’s fault. Was it not her who had pulled Rio’s hand towards this marriage? And now it was Rio having to deal with the ramifications of that decision.

 

If anyone deserved to be dragged into this mess it was her.

 

She raised her hand.

 

The glass clouded under her touch.

 

“Lilia”

 

The mirror darkened, then brightened again. Lilia’s face emerged from the surface, lines of concern already etched deeper than they had been the last time they spoke.

 

Her eyes sharpened the moment she saw Rio.

 

“…You look awake,” Lilia said carefully. Not in pleasantry. A question.

 

“You could say that,” Rio replied.

 

A pause.

 

“How have things been? We have not spoken since before the wedding.”

 

Rio hesitated—only a second but she saw the crease of Lilia’s brow.

 

“Things have been…eventful.”

 

Silence stretched between them, taut as a drawn thread. Lilia did not rush her. She never did. She simply waited, watching the way Rio stood too still, the way her gaze refused to settle.

 

“What is this Rio,” Lilia said at last. “You did not summon me for nerves.”

 

Rio said nothing.

 

Lilia’s expression shifted. The warmth faded, replaced by something colder, sharper — the look she wore when matters of state turned dangerous.

 

“If something occurred,” she said quietly, “something that affects the balance of power, you must tell me.”

 

Rio’s jaw tightened.

 

“I know.”

 

“Then tell me.”

 

Rio looked away. The words suddenly stuck in her throat.

 

Lilia leaned closer to the glass, voice lowering. “Is it—”

 

“No.”

 

The word cut clean and fast.

 

Lilia stilled.

 

They held each other’s gaze through the mirror, something old and unspoken moving between them. Lilia searched Rio’s face once more, then inclined her head slightly, accepting the denial — but not dismissing the fear that had prompted the question.

 

“…Then what?” she asked.

 

Rio swallowed.

 

“Something happened during the ceremony…when we touched hands,” she said finally.

 

Lilia’s breath caught.

 

“I felt… something,” Rio continued, choosing each word with care. “And I know she did too.”

 

Lilia’s face had gone pale now. “Rio.”

 

“I didn’t imagine it.”

 

“Speak plainly.”

 

Rio closed her eyes for a brief moment. When she opened them again, the truth was already there.

 

“A soulbond,” she said. “It has awakened.”

 

Lilia did not speak.

 

The mirror reflected only her stillness, her attention narrowing inward, calculating, measuring fallout before it had even begun.

 

“You think,” Lilia said carefully.

 

“I know.”

 

Another silence — this one longer, more dangerous.

 

When Lilia finally nodded, it was slow. Grave.

 

“This cannot be allowed to surface,” she said. “You know that.”

 

“Yes.”

 

“Especially not there.”

 

“I know.”

 

“Not now.”

 

“I know.”

 

Lilia’s voice softened only slightly. “You must not let anyone suspect. Not your council. Not her family. Not the court you are standing in.”

 

Rio’s hands curled at her sides.

 

“And the girl?” Lilia asked. “How is she taking it?”

 

Rio hesitated.

 

“She doesn’t understand it,” she said. “Not yet.”

 

“But?”

 

Rio exhaled. “She’s… perceptive.”

 

Lilia’s eyes flicked sharply upward. “That is not what I asked.”

 

Rio’s silence answered anyway.

 

“She challenges you,” Lilia said. Not accusing. Observing.

 

“She speaks her mind.”

 

“And you allow it.”

 

Rio’s mouth tightened. “She isn’t afraid.”

 

Lilia’s gaze sharpened further. “That is dangerous.”

 

“I know.”

 

“Do not confuse fascination with safety, Rio.”

 

“I’m not—”

 

“Then why does your voice change when you speak of her?”

 

Rio stiffened.

 

“I am not attached.”

 

Lilia studied her for a long moment. “Then do not become so.”

 

The words landed hard.

 

“For her sake as much as yours,” Lilia continued. “Distance will feel cruel. But it is mercy. At least until you are home.”

 

Rio’s chest ached at that — an ache she refused to examine too closely.

 

“She has already been displaced,” Rio said quietly. “I don’t wish to make her feel cast aside.”

 

Lilia’s expression softened — just for a heartbeat.

 

“That impulse,” she said, “is exactly what you cannot afford.”

 

Rio said nothing.

 

“Appearances must be preserved,” Lilia continued. “And bonds — awakened or not — grow fastest when given room.”

 

The implication settled heavily between them.

 

Rio nodded once. Then, after a moment—

 

“We should leave,” she said.

 

Lilia blinked. “Leave?”

 

“Sooner than planned.”

 

Understanding flickered across Lilia’s face.

 

“…That may be wise,” she said slowly. “If you are certain.”

 

Rio’s answer came without hesitation. “I am.”

 

The mirror dimmed.

 

“Be careful,” Lilia said, her voice softer again. “With yourself. And with her.”

 

Rio inclined her head.

 

The glass went dark.

 

She stood alone once more, the echo of the bond stirring beneath her ribs — restless, watchful, far too alive for her liking.

 

Rio tightened her grip on the edge of the table until the feeling dulled.

 

Distance, she told herself.

 

Just until they were home.

Chapter End Notes

Well hello there. It’s been a minute but my exams are finally over and i promise I will try my hardest to catch up on writing during this break. This capter has been sitting Hal finished in my drafts for weeks so im so happy to finally just get it out. This is a bit of a lighter chapter so happy reading.

Chapter 6: At First Light

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Agatha had sat there until she could no longer hear the queen’s footsteps retreating. Until even the last whisper of her presence had thinned into distance.

 

Until the bond began its usual cruelty.

 

A quiet, insistent tug beneath her ribs, mapping a soulmate just out of reach, as if the River itself wanted to drag her after her by the wrist. As if it wished her to follow.

 

She would do no such thing.

 

No, Agatha sat there, fingered clenched tightly around the edge of the table until her knuckles ached, until the queens presence was far enough away that she felt as though she could breathe once again without her breath catching on something sharp.

 

And then she rose.

 

She abandoned her own unfinished dish with the same grace—though far less urgency—as the queen. The guards did not stop her when she exited the room, nor when she walked through the side gate exiting the castle and entering the gardens. They did not stop her as she snuck through the garden gates either, heading down the familiar path towards the one place she aways sought out for refuge, or enlightenment.

 

Perhaps it was pity.

 

Perhaps it was simple indifference.

 

Either way, it worked in her favour. Not a soul spoke to her the entire walk, though she could feel eyes in the quiet behind her—soft as dust, heavy as judgment.

 

Now she sat perched on a smooth river rock, the chill seeping through her skirts, her mind a hooded thing full of teeth.

 

Idiotic.

Fool.

You have done it again.

 

The thought came in like a lash from one corner of her skull, the voice of survival dressed up as righteousness.

 

You can never just keep your mouth shut, and one of these days you will pay for it with your life.

 

Agatha groaned, lowering her head to her hands, her hands slid back in frustration pressing the palms of them to her temple while fingers curled into the roots of her hair and tugged.

 

You were meant to soften her. Distract her from last night. Not lay fresh kindling at her feet.

 

Her fingers found the little hair brooch at the back of her head, pretty and useless, and tore it free. The neat twists she’d forced into order unravelled at once, curls loosening and spilling down her shoulders like her body had been waiting to undo the lie.

 

She stared at the clasp in her palm.

 

What was the point?

 

Nothing would distract from her disrespect now—not the careful gown, not the pinned hair, not the practiced poise. The queen was likely already weighing the shape of her punishment, or worse—speaking to the council, to Agatha’s mother, saying the words that would end this before it properly began.

 

This marriage cannot work.

 

Because Agatha had no manners. Because Agatha could not be managed. Because Agatha was not worth the trouble.

 

She could almost hear her mother’s voice wrapping around it like silk over a blade.

 

And then there was the money—five million crowns, tossed at an auction like it meant nothing at all. Agatha’s stomach turned at the thought, at the absurdity of it, at how little sense it still made. Who spent that much on a stranger? On a bride they did not need to win?

 

Why had the queen done it?

 

What did she want from Agatha?

 

Agatha had hoped that by now she would have an answer, something solid she could hold up to the light and understand. Instead each interaction only left her more off balance, more painfully aware that she was walking a corridor she could not see the end of.

 

There were moments where Agatha almost thought she saw something gentle behind those eyes, something that strayed so far from all the stories and legends it almost made her head hurt. Then it would vanish, locked behind an impenetrable wall. Agatha could not tell what game they were playing but it felt as if she were being blindfolded half the time and walked around blind.

 

Agatha did not like walking blind.

 

She liked to know what she was up against. Liked to know how small she had to make herself, or how sharp her bite had to be, depending on who stood in front of her. With her mother, the rules were carved into stone. With courtiers, the games were predictable. With fear, at least, she understood the language.

 

But with the queen—

 

Agatha could not find the balance. Not the right shape of compliance that kept her alive, nor the right measure of defiance that let her keep a scrap of pride.

 

And worse than all of it—worse than the fear, worse than the confusion—was the quiet, humiliating truth pressing at her ribs like a bruise:

 

That the bond still pulled.

 

Even now.

 

Even as she sat here and told herself she would not follow.

 

She couldn’t be certain how long she sat there, before she was pulled from her thoughts. Her name was called from behind, tugging at her attention with far more gentleness than the bond she was still trying to strangle into compliance.

 

“Agatha?”

 

She recognised the cadence without even turning.

 

Agatha squeezed her eyes shut for a heartbeat, as if that might turn the voice into one more thought she could ignore. It did not. When she looked up, Jen was standing at the edge of the bank, skirts gathered in one hand, curls pulled half loose by the wind.

 

“I knew I would find you here,” Jen said, picking her way over the stones. “You always run to the River when the walls start to press in.”

 

Agatha huffed something that might have been a laugh on a kinder day. “I did not run.”

 

“No, I bet you didn’t” Jen said mildly, coming to stand beside the rock. “You fled with great dignity.”

 

Despite herself, the corner of Agatha’s mouth twitched. It vanished as quickly as it came. “How’d you slip past your mother?”

 

Jen let out a small huff of a laugh at that. “I could ask you the same question.”

 

Agatha didn’t bother restraining an eye roll. “Haven’t seen that woman since last night, thank the River.” She dipped the toe of her slippers into the river water lapping at the rocks. “I suppose the deed is done, she has gotten what she desires, along with the council and the queen. She has little need to converse with me now, other than to remind me not to make a mess of this union.”

 

Jen did not speak straight away, simply watched Agatha with one of those looks that made Agatha’s skin itch. Not because it was full of pity, Jen rarely looked at her with pity. No, it was the knowingness in her eyes. A look that felt as if she could see past all the carefully constructed walls and barriers Agatha had build around herself to fence off the outside world. Jen was aways too good at breaking into places she shouldn’t.

 

“How was last night?” She asked gently, as though speaking to a skittish animal who was known to flee at the slightest sign of danger—Agatha wouldn’t comment on the accuracy of that comparison.

 

“Eventful,” she provided, through clenched teeth. “We performed our duties and went to sleep.”

 

“Hmm. Sounds riveting.” Jen narrowed her eyes. “You’re sure nothing else occurred? You can tell me, you know.”

 

“Nothing else occurred last night Jen, this isn’t one of your sordid books you sneak from your mother’s chambers.”

 

“I know that Agatha. What I want to know is why you look so pained right now.”

 

“I always look pained,” Agatha snapped “This isn’t a particularly enjoyable time in my life.”

 

“Yes, you often look like someone who’s sucked on a sour lemon, and yes, things have been even harder as of late, but I know you, Agatha. Since we were little girls. And the way you looked on that alter yesterday? I saw the moment something shifted in your face, this is more than just unease or frustration about a marriage I know you did not want. You might be able to fool the rest of those mutton heads but you cannot fool me.”

 

Agatha’s heart pounded in her chest so hard it almost muted out the still-buzzing bond. What did Jen see exactly? Did anyone else notice? Were they that obvious?

 

“I don’t know what you’re talking about, I am not hiding anything.”

 

“I didn’t say you were hiding anything,” Jen replied. “At least not outright.”

 

“I do not know what you would like me to say Jen.”

 

“The truth” Jen said softly, holding Agatha’s gaze. “Has she…hurt you?”

 

“No.” It came out more forcefully than it should have, for someone who’d just been imagining all the way in which their wife was going to punish them. “I mean, not yet. Surprisingly.”

 

Jen watched her again, eyes searching as if hunting for any trace of a lie.

 

“Okay,” she said slowly. “Then what is it? I know it’s something, I just can’t put my finger on it.”

 

Agatha looked down, focusing her attention on the gentle currents of the river. She weighed it out. Yes, this was a secret that could be the end of her if it were ever to come out. The Order would be on her the moment they caught a whiff of proof that she was bound not just to any woman in power, but to a queen. They’d consider her too big a threat. A corruption at the throne.

 

But this was Jen. Jen, who had been her closest and only friend for as long as she could remember. Besides, Agatha wasn’t sure she could hold this secret between just her and the queen. Maybe sharing it with Jen would make it feel less suffocating. Less…intimate.

 

She took in a shuddering breath. “Something did happen yesterday,” she admitted, chancing a glance in Jen’s direction. “Not during the rites, but during the ceremony.”

 

Jen said nothing, just continued watching her, a small scrunch forming between her brows.

 

“You’re right. Something…shifted. When we touched hands during the handfasting I—we felt something. Something here.” She touched the spot where the mark lay beneath her dress.

 

Jen’s eyes widened. Agatha saw the moment it clicked. “Surely you don’t mean… Agatha, are you saying that the queen—the Lady Death—is your—”

 

Agatha shot out a hand and clapped it over Jen’s mouth before the word could form.

 

“Do not say it,” Agatha hissed out, voice low and hard. “Not here. Not anywhere. Not to anyone.”

 

Slowly, she let her hand fall.

 

Jen’s voice, when it came, was barely a whisper. “So it’s true?”

 

Agatha looked away, out over the slow, indifferent current. For a long moment she said nothing. Her thumb worried at the little clasp in her palm until the metal bit into her skin.

 

“It does not matter,” she said at last.

 

“Of course it matters.”

 

“It does not.” She cut across her, forcing the words to lie flat. “Soul ties are for stories told to foolish girls who have not yet learned better. I am not a child, Jen. I am the Queen’s Consort now. This union is a bargain, not a ballad. It is crowns and borders and treaties, and it will remain so. Whatever the River chose to carve into my chest changes nothing.”

 

Jen flinched, like the sharpness caught her instead. “You do not believe that.”

 

“I must.” Agatha’s throat felt tight; she swallowed it down. “She is a queen. She is Lady Death. She did not come to my mother’s hall seeking a wife, she came seeking an ally. You did not see her face when the bond appeared, Jen. I did.” Her laugh broke in the middle, humourless. “She looked as though the floor had opened under her feet.”

 

“Perhaps she was frightened,” Jen said softly. “Anyone would be.”

 

Agatha shook her head. “She does not want this.”

 

“And you?” Jen asked.

 

It landed like a thrown stone. For an instant, the answer flared up fast and hot and terrifyingly honest. yes. Something in her reached toward that bond like a thirsty thing.

 

She caged it behind her teeth.

 

“Nor do I,” she said, and the lie tasted like River silt. “This… mark, this pull, whatever name you give it—it will not rule my life. I will do my duty, and Queen Vidal will do hers. That is the shape of it.”

 

Jen stared at her, disappointment softening into something more like grief. “Agatha—”

 

“But nothing,” Agatha snapped, the words slicing sharp enough to cut her own tongue. “Do not make songs out of this, Jen. There is nothing beautiful in it. There is a woman who paid five million crowns for a stranger and now likely regrets it, and another  who has never been trusted with her own future, bound together whether they like it or not.”

 

The wind pulled at her loose hair; she pushed it back with an impatient hand.

 

“You know how they speak of soul ties when politics is involved,” she murmured, bitterness dropping low. “How they are watched. Controlled. Turned into weapons or omens. I will not have my life put on a pyre for the court’s amusement. Not anymore than it already is. So you will not speak of this. Not to your parents. Not to your friends in the infirmary. Not to anyone at all.”

 

Jen gave a half smile. “You ask much.”

 

“I ask what I must.” Agatha’s gaze finally came back to hers, raw and fierce. “You are the only soul in this kingdom I trust. Do not make me a fool for it.”

 

Something in Jen’s expression broke at that. She stepped forward and wrapped her arms around Agatha, pulling her in before she could lean away.

 

Agatha went stiff on instinct, then sagged the smallest fraction, breath catching against Jen’s shoulder.

 

“All right,” Jen whispered into her hair. “All right. I will keep your secret. I will not say a word. But Agatha—”

 

Agatha squeezed her eyes shut, bracing.

 

“—no matter what names you give it,” Jen finished, voice rough, “it is still yours. No council, no queen, no Tidekeeper can unmake that.”

 

Agatha said nothing.

 

The River moved on beside them, quiet and relentless, as the bond thrummed low beneath her ribs like a second, unwelcome heartbeat.

 

 

By the time they turned back toward the castle, the sun had climbed higher, and Agatha’s composure felt stitched together with thin, fraying thread rather than raw panic.

 

“So,” Jen said at last, nudging her shoulder lightly. “Aside from being soul branded at your own wedding, how was breakfast?”

 

Agatha groaned. “Do not call it that.”

 

“What, breakfast?”

 

“You know very well which part I mean.”

 

Jen’s mouth twitched. “Did she glare at you the whole time? Did lightning strike the table? Did she turn your cup to ash with a single look?”

 

Agatha’s gaze dropped to the flagstones. “She…complimented my hair.”

 

Jen actually stopped walking for half a step. “Your hair.”

 

“Yes. Apparently it ‘suits’ me.” Agatha’s mouth twisted. “Then she told me it looked tidy.”

 

Jen stared at her, scandalised on principle. “Tidy?”

 

“You see my dilemma.”

 

“Agatha, that is not a disaster, that is just… socially awkward.”

 

“It is unnerving,” Agatha insisted. “Compliments are weapons in these halls. You know that. I do not know what to do with one that sounds almost…” She trailed off, groping for the word and refusing to land on genuine. “Unpractised.”

 

Jen’s expression softened a little. “And then?”

 

“We ate in silence.” Agatha exhaled, a thin breath. “I attempted to speak of her lands. Of the forests, the cities, anything at all that might prove I would not be completely useless as a consort. She answered, but every word felt measured, as if she feared saying too much by accident.”

 

“That sounds more like her being careful than you being unbearable,” Jen pointed out.

 

“Perhaps.” Agatha rubbed at the line of her brow. “And then my mouth betrayed me. Again. I asked if she was always that quiet in the mornings, or if my company was particularly uninspiring.”

 

Jen choked properly this time. “You did not.”

 

“I did,” Agatha said grimly. “I might as well have hurled myself into the River and saved everyone the trouble.”

 

“And what did she say?”

 

“She said she was finished.” The words still sat heavy in her chest. “Pushed her plate aside, stood, said she must contact her advisors.” Agatha’s lips pressed together. “She could not get away fast enough.”

 

Jen frowned, thinking it through. “That could mean she was unsettled. Not that she hated you.”

 

“It means she was finished,” Agatha said, echoing the queen’s tone without meaning to. “Finished with the food. Finished with the conversation. Finished with… me. Take your pick.”

 

Jen shook her head, curls bouncing. “You are very quick to convict yourself.”

 

“Someone has to,” Agatha murmured. “The rest of them will be only too happy to follow.”

 

They passed beneath an archway and into the cool shadow of the inner hall. Stone closed around them again, the light sharper as it filtered through high windows and tapestries. Agatha drew in a slow breath, bracing herself for the familiar press of walls and watching eyes.

 

“Still,” Jen said, quieter now, “for what it’s worth… if she truly couldn’t stand you, I doubt she would waste time complimenting your hair at all. Tidy or otherwise.”

 

Agatha made a noncommittal sound, unwilling to give the thought a shape.

 

She was about to offer some wry deflection when the sound hit her.

 

It met them before they even turned the last corner—a low, busy murmur where there should have been quiet. The dull thud of trunks being dragged over stone. The scrape of wood. The rustle of linen. Voices pitched low in the particular tone of people trying very hard not to be noticed.

 

Agatha’s steps faltered.

 

Jen glanced at her, then forward, brows drawing together. “That’s your wing,” she murmured.

 

They rounded the corner.

 

Servants clogged the corridor outside Agatha’s chambers. Two footmen strained under the weight of a large chest, angling it toward the stair. A maid hurried past with an armful of folded dresses. Cupboard doors gaped open; drawers stood half pulled, their contents stripped. The air smelled of dust and cedar shavings and thin, anxious sweat.

 

For a heartbeat, Agatha’s mind refused to arrange any of it into sense.

 

Then one of the maids stepped out of her doorway carrying a familiar shawl.

 

Blue, worn soft at the edges. Her mother’s old shawl. The only thing from that woman Agatha had ever kept.

 

It was being laid neatly into a trunk alongside the midnight gown she had worn only the other day, its deep fabric already half swallowed by linen.

 

“That is mine,” Agatha heard herself say.

 

She moved without thinking, skirts brushing past a footman as she crossed to the open trunk. The maid holding the gown started and nearly dropped it.

 

“My lady—”

 

“What,” Agatha said, each syllable very clear, “are you doing with my things?”

 

The girl’s eyes flew to the floor. “We were told to—”

 

“By whom?” Agatha demanded. Her pulse beat sharp in her ears. “Who ordered this?”

 

The maid’s mouth opened and closed, trapped between terror and duty. Around them, the corridor stilled; every servant seemed to find an urgent reason to look anywhere but at their new queen consort.

 

“Agatha,” Jen said softly, a warning threaded through her name.

 

“By whom?” Agatha repeated, not looking away from the maid.

 

“Do not terrorise the staff, Agatha.”

 

The familiar voice slid down the hall like cold water.

 

Agatha went rigid. Slowly, she turned.

 

Her mother stood at the far end of the corridor, hands folded, gown immaculate. Two of her friends—wives of minor councillors—lingered a few paces behind, pretending decorous disinterest while their eyes gleamed with it.

 

“Mother,” Agatha said, jaw tight.

 

“Really,” her mother went on, approaching at an unhurried pace, “must you glare at them so? They are only doing as they were told.”

 

Agatha’s fingers curled until her nails bit into her palms. “And what, precisely, were they told?”

 

“To pack,” her mother replied. Her gaze swept over the open door, the half-emptied chamber beyond, and a small, satisfied smile brushed the corner of her mouth. “You will not be needing these rooms much longer.”

 

Agatha’s stomach dropped.

 

Jen shifted closer to her shoulder, silent, solid. Agatha could feel her there without looking.

 

“I do not understand,” Agatha said, her voice thin around the edges. “No one has said—”

 

“Her Majesty has decided to depart sooner than planned,” her mother said, as if remarking on the weather. “Naturally, when a queen expresses a preference, we move quickly to accommodate it.”

 

The words hit like a stone.

 

“Sooner,” Agatha echoed. “How much sooner?”

 

Her mother’s brows rose, delicately amused. “At first light.”

 

The corridor tilted.

 

“As in… tomorrow?” Agatha asked, though she already knew the answer.

 

“Did you imagine she meant a fortnight hence?” her mother replied lightly. “Tradition is charming, but hardly binding. Queens are not required to linger in provincial halls for two weeks simply to satisfy old ladies’ gossip.”

 

Heat flared under Agatha’s skin—humiliation, sharp and blinding. Her throat worked around it.

 

“The arrangement was—”

 

“The arrangement,” her mother cut in, silk hardening to steel, “was that you would wed her and leave with her when she wished. That you would not disgrace us. That, for once, you would not make things difficult.”

 

A faint flush had risen along her mother’s cheekbones now, the only sign of strain.

 

“And yet here we are,” she went on. “Her Majesty can scarcely wait to be gone. You must have truly bored her if she is this eager to flee back to her duties this soon after the wedding.”

 

Her mothers inquisitive friends let out callous snickers at that behind their fans. Agatha felt her cheeks burn.

 

“That is not fair,” Jen said, before Agatha could find her breath. “My lady, you cannot know—”

 

“My conversation with my daughter is no concern of yours,” her mother said, not even granting Jen a glance. “Attend to your own family’s standing before you meddle with mine.”

 

Jen’s jaw snapped shut. Agatha flinched as if struck in her place.

 

Her mother’s attention slid back to her, cold and appraising. “I did warn you, Agatha. All you had to do was present yourself politely, speak when spoken to, and avoid any…unseemly displays. You must not have listened, I can only imagine what you have done while you two were alone to have Her Majesty fleeing for her own court before the ashes have cooled.”

 

Agatha’s fingers dug crescent moons into her palms.

 

“You do not know why she wishes to leave,” she managed. “You were not there.”

 

“I know enough,” her mother replied. “I know custom. I know that no bride in living memory has been taken from her bridal house as quickly as you are about to be. I know that a queen who is pleased with her choice does not bolt the morning after.”

 

Each word found its mark, slicing through the thin, frayed thread of Agatha’s composure.

 

Somewhere beneath her ribs, the bond pulsed once—sharp and unbidden—like a hand reaching for hers. It only made the humiliation worse. That treacherous connection, tugging her toward a woman who apparently could not endure a handful of days in her presence.

 

“If Her Majesty wishes to return to her court, she is free to do so,” Agatha said stiffly. “I am sure she has far more important matters to attend to than lingering here.”

 

Her mother’s smile sharpened. “Such loyalty. You do realise it is yourself you condemn with such words? The halls will say she could not wait to be rid of you. That is what they will remember. Not her duties. You.”

 

Agatha’s breath caught. For an instant, she thought she might be sick.

 

“You speak as though I had any say in this,” she said. “If the queen sent word, why was I not told?”

 

“Oh, but you are being told,” her mother replied. “Now.” She waved a hand, as though dismissing a minor detail. “Her messenger arrived some time ago. Naturally, preparations began at once. There was hardly any point disturbing you at your little river tryst when there was work to be done.”

 

Agatha’s cheeks flamed. Jen bristled beside her, hands balling into fists.

 

“You might have allowed Her Majesty to tell me herself,” Agatha said, voice very low.

 

Her mother’s eyes cooled further. “Do not be childish. Queens do not run about delivering their own tidings like housemaids. She entrusted us to make ready. Consider yourself told.”

 

The final word fell with the weight of a door slamming.

 

For a moment, the corridor blurred at the edges. Agatha was distantly aware of her own pulse roaring in her ears, of the ache in her throat, of Jen’s fingers just brushing the sleeve of her gown as if asking for permission to steady her.

 

“You will wish to see what is being taken,” her mother said briskly. “And perhaps say farewell to these rooms. You will not see them again after tonight.”

 

She inclined her head a fraction, the barest shadow of courtesy. “Try not to weep on the carpets. They have only just been cleaned.”

 

With that, she turned and swept back down the corridor, her little retinue trailing after like a flock of well trained birds.

 

Silence rushed in behind her.

 

The servants resumed their work in muted fashion, eyes skittering away from Agatha’s like minnows. The trunk lids lowered more softly now, as though even wood could feel the strain in the air.

 

Agatha stood very still.

 

“Agatha,” Jen said, quietly.

 

The name sounded as if it came from a long way off. Agatha stared down into the open trunk before her, at the shawl and gown and neatly folded shift—her life reduced to careful layers of cloth and cedar chips.

 

“She could have told you herself,” Jen went on. “It was cruel not to.”

 

“She is a queen,” Agatha said. Her voice held steady, to her faint astonishment. “She does not waste time on such things.”

 

“That is not an excuse,” Jen muttered.

 

“It is an explanation,” Agatha replied. “Besides, it makes sense. Why would she stay? Our…predicament is a problem. I am a problem. Better to cut her losses quickly and flee back to where she can toss me in a corner and forget I exist.”

 

“Do not do that,” Jen said sharply. “Do not speak of yourself as if you were some spoiled piece of fruit she is tossing aside.”

 

Agatha’s lips twisted. “What would you have me say?” She dragged her gaze from the trunk at last, meeting Jen’s eyes. “That this is a grand romance? That she is whisking me away early because she cannot bear to be parted from me?” The bitter taste of the words coated her tongue. “The whole court will see this for what it is.”

 

Jen didn’t flinch. “They will see what they wish to see. You do not have to help them sharpen the knives.”

 

Agatha laughed then, a small, brittle sound.

 

“I am to leave tomorrow, Jen,” she whispered. “Tomorrow, I will walk out of this house and into hers, at the side of a woman I scarcely know and cannot read. I am to leave you. Leave everything I know. And I could not even manage a single day here without driving her away early. What am I to do there.”

 

Her throat closed around the last syllable. The corridor wavered again.

 

Jen stepped forward and caught her hand, gripping it tight. “You did not drive her away.”

 

“You do not know that.”

 

“I know you,” Jen said. “And I know you are very good at deciding you have failed before anyone has had the chance to speak.”

 

Agatha’s mouth pulled into something that might have been a smile in better light. “A useful skill. It spares one the surprise.”

 

Jen’s fingers tightened around hers. “It is a cruel one. And you do not deserve it. Not from them, and not from yourself.”

 

A maid edged past with a smaller chest, keeping her gaze fixed firmly on the floor. Agatha and Jen both fell silent until the woman had gone, the sound of her footsteps fading toward the stair.

 

“This feels like punishment,” Agatha murmured at last, barely moving her lips. “Sent away early like a misbehaving child.”

 

“Then you will just have to make certain they remember you are not a child,” Jen said. “You are a consort. A queen’s chosen. Let them choke on that.”

 

“Chosen,” Agatha echoed, the word strange in her mouth. Her eyes dropped back to the trunk. “It does not feel like choosing.”

 

“Feelings lie,” Jen said quietly. “Coin does not. Nor does a crown.” She drew a breath. “Do you want me to stay? Help you sort what they’re throwing into these boxes?”

 

For a moment, the offer wavered in front of Agatha like a lifeline. She could almost see it: the two of them on the floor, sleeves rolled, Jen swearing at misfolded gowns while they pretended this was any other journey. Any other leaving.

 

She swallowed it back.

 

“If you stay, your mother will come hunting,” Agatha said. “And then we will have to deal with the wrath of two mothers in these halls.”

 

Jen’s mouth twisted. “I suppose the one good thing about this is your mother cannot touch you once you are gone.”

 

“That,” Agatha said, “depends very much on how long her reach proves to be.”

 

Jen huffed, impatient. “You deflect like a councillor already.”

 

“I am adapting to my surroundings.”

 

Jen rolled her eyes. “Fine. But I will be back before nightfall. If they forget to pack your good boots, I shall stage a revolt.”

 

“Spare the servants,” Agatha said. “They are only doing as they were told.”

 

Jen’s gaze softened. “And you?”

 

“I will do as I am told as well,” Agatha replied. “For now.”

 

Something like pride flickered across Jen’s face. She stepped in, squeezed Agatha’s hand once more and, before anyone could think to object, leaned up to press a quick kiss to her cheek.

 

“I will see you later,” she said. “Do not let them strip you down to nothing before then.”

 

“I have nothing they have not already tried to take,” Agatha said, but she said it lightly, as if it were a jest and not a confession.

 

Jen gave her a look that said she heard the truth anyway, then turned and walked away down the corridor, her steps crisp, back straight. A moment later, the corner swallowed her.

 

Agatha stood where she was for a few breaths longer, listening to the low shuffle and murmur all around her, the soft thud of trunks closing over pieces of the life she knew. Then she drew herself up, smoothed her skirts, and stepped into her half emptied chamber.

 

The servants parted for her as if she were a blade.

 


Agatha did not go down to luncheon.

 

By the time the bells marked the midday watch, most of the trunks were closed, their wax seals cooling. Only a few plain dresses and a travel cloak still hung from the open wardrobe. The rest of her life had been tucked away behind polished wood and leather straps.

 

Someone knocked, knuckles soft against the door. One of the castle’s senior maids bobbed a curtsey in the doorway.

 

“My lady? The table is laid. Her Majesty has taken her place.” A carefully neutral pause. “She wishes to know if you will be joining.”

 

The thought of walking back into that room, sitting opposite the queen again after everything that had occurred—with her chest still aching and her mother’s words still ringing—it made her tongue feel like lead in her mouth.

 

“My stomach is unsettled,” Agatha said. “Give Her Majesty my apologies. I would not wish to offend her with poor company.”

 

The maid hesitated, then dipped her head. “I will say you are unwell.”

 

She withdrew. Agatha listened to her footsteps fade and only then let herself breathe.

 

Not long after, a tray appeared on the low table by the window. The tray carried bread, a bowl of broth still faintly steaming, a cup of pale tea that smelled faintly of mint and some sharper herb. There was more on it than she was used to when she hid herself away like this—an extra heel of bread, a little dish of salted butter, a slice of some pale cheese.

 

Perhaps on of the senior stewards had decided a fainting consort would be bad for appearances. Or perhaps the servants were simply overdoing it, unsure what was expected of a queen’s wife.

 

She stared at it for a long moment, jaw tight, then turned back to the trunk she had been pretending to sort.

 

By the time the broth had gone cold and a thin skin had formed across it, she rang for the maid and asked that the tray be taken away.

 

“I am not hungry,” she said, though her head was beginning to ache and her stomach ached with more than emptiness.

 

The afternoon thinned around her. Light crawled across the bare patches of wall where shelves and hooks had once hung. She worked mechanically, checking lists, smoothing folded fabric, pretending she had any say in what stayed and what went.

 

True to her word, Jen slipped back in before the evening bells.

 

She did not stay long—only enough time to scold a footman for trying to pack Agatha’s battered boots, to re-fold a stack of chemises with more care than any servant dared, to sit for a moment on the edge of the bed and rest her head briefly against Agatha’s shoulder.

 

Her mother’s voice floated down the corridor once, sharp as a whipcrack. Jen pulled away with a grimace.

 

“I have to go,” she whispered. “If I am late to table my own mother will flay me. Again.”

 

“Go,” Agatha said. “I will be fine here in my lonesome.”

 

Jen huffed a soft laugh, then sobered. “Send for me if you cannot sleep.”

 

Agatha nodded, though she knew she would not.

 

When Jen left for the second time, the chamber felt emptier than it had all day.

 

 

She did not go to supper either.

 

Another knock. Another maid at her door, this one more hesitant than the last. “My lady… Her Majesty is in the great hall. Your place has been laid.”

 

Agatha sat at the edge of the bed, hair unbound, fingers worrying at the embroidered hem of her sleeve.

 

“Tell them I am still unwell,” she said. “I would only dampen the mood.”

 

The maid hesitated. “Shall I stay? Fetch the healer?”

 

“No,” Agatha said quickly. “No, that will not be necessary.”

 

The maid retreated. Eventually, a second tray appeared—supper this time, lighter but laid out with the same anxious generosity as before. Another bowl of broth, a small cutlet of something cooled under its glaze, more bread than one person needed, the same pale tea whose scent warmed the room.

 

She did not touch this one either.

 

By the time the sounds of supper had faded—the muffled rise and fall of voices, a faint drift of music, the echo of chairs and cutlery—the room no longer looked like hers at all. Only the bed remained dressed as it had been the night before, and even that felt like something borrowed.

 

She washed at the basin, changed into her nightdress, and climbed beneath the covers as if the act itself might hold the day at bay.

 

The canopy above had never seemed so low.

 

On the wall opposite, a pale outline marked the place where a small shelf had hung for years. Her crooked wooden bird, the one she had carved with a smuggled knife under Jen’s supervision, lay now in some padded corner of a trunk, anonymous among folded linen.

 

Tomorrow, all of it would go. Out of this house, out of this city, across the River to a court she had never seen, where strangers would look at her and see only the price paid and the mark on her chest.

 

The bond hummed low and stubborn beneath her ribs, as if aware of the direction her thoughts had taken, as if leaning already toward the woman who had ordered her departure and, apparently, her meals.

 

“I will not go crawling,” she said into the dark. There was no one to hear it. “You may drag me, but I will not beg to be kept.”

 

Fear crept in when she lay still too long. Not the sharp panic of the riverbank, but a heavier thing, slow and cold. Fear of being alone among new mouths and old rumours. Of sitting across from that unreadable gaze day after day, never knowing whether the hand extended toward her would shield or strike.

 

You survived this house, something in her reminded her. You survived your mother. You survived being unwanted where you were meant to belong most.

 

She turned onto her side, drawing her knees up, one hand fisting in the sheet.

 

She had learned, here, how to bend without breaking. How to make herself small when she had to, and sharp when she could not. The queen’s court would be no gentler. But it would be different. New walls. New rules. New cracks to slip through.

 

And she was not entering it empty-handed. She bore a title now, whether she felt fit for it or not. Wife. Consort. Problem, perhaps—but one that now came with a crown’s seal.

 

“They will not make a fool of you,” she told the dark. “Not there. Not again.”

 

Whether she meant her mother, the court, or the queen herself, she did not say.

 

Outside, the wind worried at the shutters. Somewhere in the house, a guard’s step passed, measured and distant. Beneath her palm, the mark on her chest warmed faintly, as if answering some thought she refused to let to the surface.

 

“I know who I am,” she whispered, barely stirring the air. “I am not a mistake to be thrown away.”

 

The saying of it steadied something inside her. The fear did not vanish, but it loosened its grip, leaving room for a thin, stubborn thread of defiance.

 

She held onto that as her eyes finally closed, in a bed marked for leaving, in a chamber stripped for the journey, on the last night she would ever sleep under her mother’s roof.
