Chapter 7: The Price of Appearances

Chapter Notes

Happy new years 🥳 here’s to another year of being obsessed with the same two lesbian witches.
Wrote a good chunk of this more than slightly tipsy so idk how I really feel about it but I’m sick of looking at it and it’s nearly 3am here so I hope you enjoy

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Luncheon had been understandable.

 

Rio had made a mess of breakfast. She had spoken too much where it was uncalled for and had spoken far too little where civility expected it.

 

She had not intended to offend her. In fact, before that meal the thought that she might have offended her had not crossed her mind.

 

During their first meal together Agatha had appeared stiff at times, yes. She had been curious, intrigued, almost teasing in a scarily perceptive way. She had not seemed slighted. But perhaps Rio was simply incapable of reading her in the same way the younger woman appeared proficient in reading Rio.

 

Because as she had sat in the same seat she had that morning, trying her hardest not to stare at the empty chair ahead of her, it was clear she had, in some way, deeply offended her wife. The table had been laid for two again: two settings, two cups, steam curling from dishes that grew steadily cooler. Yet only one had taken their place this time.

 

The servants had drifted at the edges of the hall, careful in their movements, careful in where they let their eyes fall.

 

“Perhaps she’s simply running late,” she had tried to convince herself. Even as the food grew colder and the silence grew thicker.

 

Rio was not sure how long she had sat there before it finally dawned on her that Agatha would not be showing up.

 

Eventually she had beckoned over a servant , attempting to maintain an air of indifference as she asked after her consort’s whereabouts. 

 

“Her Ladyship is unwell, Your Majesty,” She had been told. 

 

She had suspected it was a lie. Deep down. Somewhere traitorous, where a vague awareness had begun to take root—her body tuning itself towards the woman she was now bonded to. What little she could make out did not suggest illness. But something else, something far more complicated than Rio wanted to touch or decipher in that moment.

 

She had tried not to dwell on it. Tried not to pick it apart and examine it with a similar intensity that she had felt turned on herself that morning. Because if she had, she would have had to admit that she cared. 

 

And caring was not something she could afford at present. 

 

Not here. 

Not with her. 

 

She knew she should have simply gotten up at that moment. Taken her leave. Unconcerned herself in the matter. Put it down to the sensitivities that came with young age and too little summers under one’s belt.

 

Despite this, she had not been able to simply let it go. 

 

She had ordered a meal to be sent to her consort’s chambers, detailing exactly what was to be brought, in part because she did not trust this court’s opinion of a “hearty” meal. The maid had waited in uneasy silence as Rio specified the milder broth rather than the rich stews, extra bread, a little cheese, mint for the tea instead of the harsher eastern blends.

 

She had not been entirely sure what constituted a suitable sickbed tray  for a woman she was almost certain was not unwell, but she had tried regardless. And perhaps she had been a little excessive, but was that not better than the alternative?

 

She had not touched her own meal in the end.

 

She had left only after confirming a tray would be sent up as she had requested.

 

And then she had locked herself away in her guest chambers under the false pretence of convening with council from home. Her desk was, in fact, piled with scrolls and parchments detailing aspects of the union she knew she should be turning her mind to. Despite this, her thoughts had circled back, again and again, to the image of azure eyes observing her and truly seeing her in a way she hadn’t felt since—

 

Well. It was safe to say she had not endured that kind of scrutiny, that kind of knowing, in a very long time.

 

She had ran a hand through the roots of her hair and had tried to ignore the faint tremor of her fingers. She knew she would have to redo the braid but that had sat near the bottom of an already long list of concerns at the time.

 

And when a familiar feeling had stirred in her chest, she had tired to pretend she couldn’t feel the thread of guilt bleeding into it.

 

 

Dinner had been worse.

 

It had stripped away any room for pretending.

 

While Agatha’s absence at lunch had been unsettling her absence at dinner was a cause for concern.

 

Rio had taken her place at the head of the table in the grand hall. Her entrance was supposed to be the last. Everyone had taken their place by the time she had taken hers.

 

Everyone bar one.

 

The seat to the left of Rio had remained empty.

 

Once again she had tried not to over examine it. Tried not to let her gaze linger on it, not to let a frown set in her brows. She had kept her back straight and her expression unreadable, had put on a show of pretence that nothing was amiss.

 

The sight had still troubled her.

 

It had gnawed at something within her. Something that made her very nearly get up and abandon her place in a hall full of eyes.

 

Of course she had not done that.

 

It would have ben bad optics. Something they had enough of already. Her consort had refused to dine with her the day after their wedding. Publicly. She knew how it looked. That, she told herself, was what truly bothered her. Not the absence itself, not the image of a young woman sitting alone behind a locked door, but the fact that such a refusal looked…poorly. A sovereign’s wife declining her place at a table so soon after the vows would make or easy whispers if word travelled. It suggested unrest. Weakness. A union already splintering.

 

That was what bothered her, she had insisted to herself. The repercussions. The appearances.

 

It might almost have been convincing.

 

Almost.

 

But the longer she had remained seated there, with the buzz of hushed chatter, scraping utensils and low music floating around her the more she had strained not to glance to her left, not to imagine her consort’s current state, not to care.

 

As the meal had gone on, the lie had frayed into a quieter, unbidden thought.

 

What if she is truly unwell?

 

The question had sat with her at the desk, on top of the neat piles of parchment. It had slid in again over the cold cuts of meat on her plate that she pushed around with her knife. It had sat with her until, in a moment of frustration she would later excuse as simple pragmatism, she had done something she had been avoiding since the moment the mark had burned across her skin.

 

She had reached for the bond.

 

Not merely tolerated its presence. Not merely poked at its outer corners. She had gone looking for it this time. She had shut her eyes briefly and let out a slow breath and turned her awareness inward, toward that faint thread beneath her ribs that was no longer wholly her own.

 

For a moment there had been nothing, and she could have almost laughed at herself for indulging such foolishness.

 

And then something had answered. Not in words. Not in anything so clear as pain. Merely a tightening—like a hand closing somewhere in the hollow of her chest, like a curtain drawn shut over a window. Something there and not there all at once.

 

It had frustrated her. The ambiguity.

 

It had not been like calling upon the other currents she knew. The old ache and heaviness she had long since learned to brace against. This was quieter. As if she stood at the edge of a riverbank in the fog, the sound of the water there but dimmed, its shape hard to make out.

 

She had followed it all the same.

 

The thread had pulled tighter, the fog drawing back, still distorted but a little easier to make out.

 

It did not feel like fever. It did not feel like the dull weight of sickness. It was something else. Closed. Coiled inwards. Wrong, her instincts had supplied. Not in the way a body failed, but in the way a wound bleeds with no blood.

 

She had withdrawn from the thread as if it burned.

 

It is not illness, she had thought, pressing her thumb hard into the edge of the table until the bone protested.

 

Which meant whatever kept her consort from her side was something she had no right to touch. Something she had perhaps caused and could not mend with herbs or orders or treaties.

 

She had told herself, firmly, that she would not reach again.

 

She had turned her attention back to the great hall.

 

The chatter had not ceased but Rio had begun to notice something else between the noise.

 

The looks.

 

She was no stranger to having eyes on her. That was not what caused her skin to prickle.

 

The looks were not directed at her but rather to the side of her.

 

To her left.

 

To the vacant spot that seemed to command more attention than it would have if it had been occupied.

 

It would have been fine if it had stopped at the looks.

 

She could handle stares.

 

What she could not handle was presumption.

 

Presumption and disrespect.

 

Both of which seemed ample in the court she had found herself in.

 

Because between the stares came the whispers.

 

Spoken behind careful hands and napkins but spoken all the same.

 

A particular look between non other than her consort’s mother and one of her ladies at her side, followed by a round of derisive laughter, had irked something inside her.

 

“Would you care to share what is of such amusement at the dinner table Lady Harkness?” Rio spoke for the first time that evening, her voice steady and her words even.

 

The lady beside Evanora had the half decency to appear ashamed, though Evanora Harkness met Rio’s eyes with a knowing smirk.

 

“Oh, we were only discussing how unfortunate it is that Her Ladyship is…indisposed,” her voice pitched with feigned innocence and just enough volume to carry to the nearer tables. “What with your departure looming so close, it is a shame, truly, we will not be able to see the two of you together in the glow of your still green marriage. “

 

Rio did not respond but her eyes narrowed. She had expected whispers of this, of course but to have it come from her mother in law was…grating.

 

“We had all hoped, of course, that the newlyweds would honour us with their presence a little longer. But perhaps our humble court fatigues Her Majesty more easily than we realised.”

 

A ripple of laughter moved along the table like an ugly little wave. Rio watched the way eyes slid unashamedly, as if pulled by a string, toward the empty chair at her left and then away again, sharp with interest.

 

Evanora’s words seemed to speak an invitation as though others had been waiting for the opportunity to voice their own options as well.

 

“Perhaps the princess simply failed to…commend herself,” someone else had added, softer, cruel amusement tucked beneath the words. “Hard to hold a queen like this, I imagine. Harder still to make her stay.”

 

The vague feeling in the bond had suddenly made perfect sense. The tight, closed wrongness she had touched earlier was  made clear. It was humiliation anticipated before it could be delivered. A young woman choosing absence rather than sit here while they carved her apart with their tongues.

 

For a fleeting moment, Rio had been glad that the woman they spoke of was not there to hear their cruelty. And yet she knew, just as surely, that Agatha already understood how her court weighed her. The damage had been done long before Rio’s arrival; this new slight merely gave them sharper knives.

 

Only then had the full shape of her earlier decision revealed itself. In her own court, such a change of plans would have been met with some murmuring, a raised brow here or there, perhaps a sharply worded letter from a councillor too fond of ceremony. In this place, it was different she had discovered too late. Newlyweds were meant to apparently remain as long as custom demanded. To show themselves, to be seen and appraised and blessed and gossiped over. To leave after a handful of days was not merely inconvenient—it was a slight. A judgement.

 

She had been too used to her decisions only affecting her own reputation. 

She had failed, entirely, to consider how swiftly this court would lay the blame elsewhere. On the daughter they had given away. On the unseen bride who had not, in their eyes, held her queen’s attention long enough.

 

The thought sat with her like grit between her teeth.

 

More comments followed, little barbs dressed up in silk. She heard Evanora murmur something about “delicate nerves” and “a sensitive constitution,” the words wrapped in a smile that did not reach the woman’s eyes. She saw the press of lips, the exchanged glances, the way a young lady at the far table hid a smirk behind her hand.

 

It was not herself they were weighing upon their tongues. It was her consort’s. Her worth. Her failure. Her shame.

 

“Perhaps,” the same minor lord as before ventured, emboldened by wine and his fellows’ laughter, “Her Majesty found the goods of the auction less appealing upon closer inspection than they had appeared at first glance. One can hardly fault a queen for wishing to return to her own halls when—”

 

“You speak,” Rio said, before he could finish, “with remarkable confidence on matters far beyond your knowing.”

 

She had not raised her voice, but the hall heard her all the same. Conversation faltered. The lord froze, cup half raised.

 

Rio set her own goblet down with great care.

 

“If my visit has been brief,” she went on, every word measured, “it is because affairs of the crown and River do not bend themselves to your supper tales. There are matters that require my presence elsewhere. Matters you would not begin to comprehend, and which I have no intention of wasting breath explaining.”

 

A murmur, quickly stifled, ran along the nearest benches. The lord’s face had gone the colour of curdled milk.

 

“As for Her Ladyship,” Rio continued, allowing the slightest edge into the title, “her conduct has been without fault. I would advise you—and any who share your talent for idle speculation—not to mistake my obligations for some lack in the woman who now stands as your queen’s consort. Or in my regard for her.”

 

Silence had followed, sudden and sharp. Somewhere down the table a chair had scraped, someone shifting as if to relieve an unseen pressure. Agatha’s mother’s smile had faltered by a fraction, the corners drawn a little too tight.

 

Rio had not looked at her. She kept her gaze on the man who had spoken until he dropped his eyes to his plate and muttered something that might have been an apology.

 

The moment had passed. Conversation, slowly, resumed its former volume, though the laughter was thinner now, and glances in her direction were several shades more wary.

 

She had taken  no pride in it. It had not been for her own sake.

 

She did not, even then, regret her decision to leave. Her queendom did in fact need her. Perhaps she stretched the truth on the severity of the matters that awaited her, but the truth could not be spoken. Not allowed, especially not here. If anything the evening had solidified her decision. She did not want to keep them in this cursed court a day longer than she had to—not after seeing how they regarded the woman they called their princess.

 

But as the meal had worn on, as dishes were cleared and wine was pored and repoured it hit her that it  was the second time in as many days that she had misjudged the weight of this kingdom’s customs upon its princess. The second time she had failed to account for the way her actions would be read through those archaic, ridiculous expectations—and the second time Agatha had borne the cost of that failure.

 

She had decided, somewhere between the main course and the honeyed pastries she barely tasted, that there would not be a third.

 

Whatever this bond between them proved to be—curse, blessing, mistake—she would not go on stumbling through her consort’s world with her eyes half shut, letting every misstep fall on the younger woman’s shoulders.

 


Later that night, when the hall had emptied and the torches in the main corridors had been banked low, Rio had asked to see Lady Harkness.

 

The request could not be refused. Not by someone who valued the benefits of upholding the treaty between them. And Rio suspected that was what Evanora cared for most.

 

They met in a smaller chamber off the great hall, a room meant for private audiences. A fire smouldered in the hearth; tapestries hung close on the walls to keep the draft at bay. Evanora was already there when Rio entered, seated in a high backed chair as if upon a throne she no longer held, a cup of spiced wine resting on the table at her elbow.

 

She stood when Rio crossed the threshold, dipping just low enough to be respectful, just shallow enough to remind everyone present—servants, guards, the walls—that she had once been bowed to instead.

 

“Your Majesty,” she said, with a smile that did not trouble the skin around her eyes. “How may I be of service?”

 

Rio did not sit at once. She came to stand by the table, letting the silence hold for a heartbeat longer than courtesy required. Her hands were clasped behind her back to keep from bracing on the chair, or from curling into fists.

 

“There is one matter I wished to settle before we depart,” she said. “It concerns your daughter’s preparations for travel.”

 

Evanora’s smile sharpened.

 

“Agatha,” she said lightly, as if tasting the name for flaws. “Yes, of course. Her trunks are already prepared, Your Majesty. We laid out her finest gowns for the celebrations. You will find she is suitably attired.”

 

For feasts in these halls, Rio almost said. Instead, she inclined her head a fraction.

 

“For the road,” she replied. “We depart at first light. The journey will not be a short one. I wish to ensure Her Ladyship has what she requires.”

 

Evanora’s hand fluttered, a little dismissive wave.

 

“The carriage will keep her comfortable,” she said. “She will have cushions, blankets. What more could the girl need?”

 

Rio’s jaw ticked. “I speak of more than cushions.”

 

She let the words come out mild. It cost her.

 

“Has she boots fit for riding?” Rio asked. “Not embroidered slippers. Leather, thick enough for long miles. Soles that will hold on wet stone.”

 

There was a pause, slight but telling. Evanora’s fingers tightened on the stem of her cup before relaxing again.

 

“My daughter is not accustomed to… tramping about like a village girl,” she said, smile returning with a brittle edge. “Her place has always been here. At court. We have not had much call to fit her for such…endeavours.”

 

“Not even for visits to the border holdings?” Rio kept her tone even. “To the coastal estates? She has never ridden out with the hunts?”

 

“A princess does not need to go chasing deer to understand her realm,” Evanora said. “Besides, Agatha has always been… delicate. Travel unsettles her. She prefers familiar surroundings. Mirrors and books and such.” She took a sip of wine. “If she is unused to hardship, it is by her own preference.”

 

Rio very much doubted that, but she chose not to voice it as she didn’t trust herself to speak for a moment without saying something regrettable. The ease with which Evanora had spoken stoked something hot and dark in Rio’s chest. As if keeping a child penned in stone and silk were a quirk of temperament. As if a woman could now choose where to stand when every door was barred and every path was watched.

 

“She is no longer simply a princess,” Rio said carefully at last. “She is the queen’s consort now, and with that comes many new endeavours. She will not be hunting on this journey. She will, however, be crossing mountains and open country. Carriages break axles. Roads wash out. In my experience, weather does not always heed a noble’s nerves.”

 

“I am certain your escorts are capable,” Evanora replied. “They will see to it she does not have to set a foot in the mud. Agatha is used to—” she tilted her head, as if searching for a kind word and finding none—“plenty. A little discomfort now and then will not harm her. Might even do her good.”

 

Rio thought of the way the bond had tightened earlier with hurt that had nothing to do with wounds and blood. Of the way those same lips had curled hours earlier while the hall laughed at the absence of the woman they were discussing.

 

Her fingers shifted against her back. She did not let them close.

 

“And for the cold?” she asked. “What cloaks has she?”

 

Evanora brightened, as if they had finally found a topic she could enjoy.

 

“She has a splendid new mantle in deep blue,” she said. “The embroidery took three months. There is another of black velvet, trimmed with sable. Very striking. She cuts a fine figure in both, I assure you.”

 

“Velvet will drink every drop of rain it sees,” Rio said. “And drag at her shoulders until she can hardly stand. I speak of a cloak that keeps the wet out. Wool. Waxed leather at the shoulders. A hood she can pull close against the wind.”

 

Evanora blinked, as if Rio had begun discussing the habits of pigs.

 

“We are not sending her to live in a barn, Your Majesty,” she said, a touch of offence creeping in at last. “You speak as if the roads between here and your capital are nothing but mire. Surely your people maintain them better than that.”

 

“I will remind you there is a long journey between this capital and my own,” Rio said, voice dangerously soft.   “My people maintain what they can, but you should know there are roads that I cannot account for, as they do not fall within the bounds of my territories.”

 

Evanora simply stared at her with an almost bored expression and Rio felt the anger rising within her bones. How could this woman be so blithe about her only daughter embarking on a potentially dangerous journey?

 

“Besides, the River floods when it wishes. Rain falls when it wishes. The wind does not care for embroidery.”

 

The bond tugged again, towards somewhere not too far from them— to its other half behind some locked door, perhaps pacing, perhaps curled small and lying awake restless. Rio could not tell. She only knew that every careless word in this room seemed to scrape against that same raw place where the bond lay.

 

“Has she ever left this city?” Rio asked. “Truly left it. Not to the next estate over for an afternoon. Has she ever slept a night beyond these walls?”

 

Evanora hesitated, then gave a little shrug.

 

“Once or twice, when she was younger,” she said. “Children are tiresome. They grow restless if you keep them in one place. We outgrew that phase. She settled. The court is where she belongs.”

 

Settled. As if her daughter were a spilled drink mopped up, a stain scrubbed into obedience.

 

Rio imagined a girl with eyes like the clear heart of a flame, pressed into brocade and veil and told to still herself until even her laughter seemed an affront. Imagined her watching carriage wheels roll away, over and over, with other people in them.

 

“You did not think she might one day need to know how to bear a journey?” Rio asked. “To endure a change of air without struggle?”

 

“If she has such trouble, I am sure you will manage her,” Evanora said. “You seem very capable, Your Majesty.” Her smile thinned. “In any case, she is your concern now. That was the agreement. We have provided what was asked.”

 

We have provided what was asked. A kingdom and a girl, tied together on an auction block. Gowns for a wedding. No boots for escape.

 

Something old and sharp rose in Rio’s chest at that—an urge to bare her teeth, to flash her eyes, to send everyone in this room scattering like startled birds. She pressed it down. She was not in her own halls. She could not afford to let the wrong thing show.

 

“My concern,” she repeated, and the words tasted truer than she liked.

 

She drew a breath deep enough to steady the line of her shoulders.

 

“You are right,” she had said. “Your daughter is under my protection now. My concern. And since your court has not found cause to see to her needs beyond your own walls, I will have your workrooms attend her before dawn.”

 

Evanora’s eyebrows lifted, faintly affronted.

 

“There is no need to trouble yourself so, Your Majesty,” she said. “We are quite capable of outfitting a princess. If we had been told earlier—”

 

“You have had her entire life to prepare her for the world beyond these stones,” Rio said, not unkindly. “You chose not to. I do not intend to compound that error.”

 

Colour rose, high and sharp, along Evanora’s cheekbones.

 

“She is… sensitive,” she said. “Particular about everything, she will probably complain about the fabric feeling wrong against her skin. There is no use wasting good people’s time on that girl.”

 

Rio thought of the younger woman who had stood before her and spoken her mind with little fear of consequence when Rio had misstepped. The woman who looked at her that morning and spoke what she saw. Sensitive, they called her. Rio saw something else.

 

Someone who had been forced into a certain box, whom people looked at but only saw the outer corners of. Someone who had been clearly denied basic care by those who were meant cherish her the most.

 

Rio could not continue that. She would not.

 

“She will not be your concern much longer,” Rio spoke at last. “Nor will her so called fussing.”

 

Evanora’s smile sharpened. “No. As I said, she is yours now.”

 

“Then we understand one another,” Rio replied. “No one will lay a hand on her tonight. Your tailors have dressed her since childhood; they know her form well enough to cut cloth without pulling her from her bed.

 

She let that sit for a heartbeat, then added, “Have them summoned to my chambers within the quarter hour.”

 

“At this hour?” Evanora’s brows climbed. “You expect my workrooms to magic you a new wardrobe before dawn, Your Majesty?”

 

“I expect them to answer when their queen calls,” Rio said, voice calm. “Which is what I am now is it not?”

 

Evanora’s eyes narrowed at that. Rio kept her own gaze levelled and light. Women like Evanora could not unsettle her.

 

“I will give them the patterns I require,” Rio said. “Something suited to the road, in my colours, bearing my crest. The rest can be settled when I see what your craftsmen can manage.”

 

Evanora stared at her for a moment, a flicker of understanding there. “You mean to dress her like one of your own, then.”

 

Rio met her gaze without blinking. “She is one of my own now.”

 

The colour that had seeped into Evanora’s cheeks deepened at that. “Very well,” she said. “I will send word to the workrooms.”

 

“Do that,” Rio said. “I will speak to them myself. I would not have the details…mislaid in passing.”

 


They arrived quicker than expected, if she were to be honest.

 

Either Evanora truly did value the treaty more than she let on, or her household had simply learned long ago to run when called.

 

A knock sounded at her chamber door—three polite raps, not the brisk rhythm of a guard. Rio set aside the parchment she had not been reading and called for them to enter.

 

Two tailors stepped in, a man and a woman, both middle aged, both in work stained wool with measuring cords looped about their necks. Behind them came a younger leatherworker, his apron smelling faintly of oil and dye, and a maid struggling under the weight of a neatly folded gown over one arm and a ledger tucked against her hip.

 

They bowed—lower than Evanora had—and hovered just inside the door as if uncertain how far they were permitted to come.

 

“Your Majesty,” the woman said. Her voice was steady, but Rio could see the tension in the tight line of her shoulders. “Her Grace said you required our services this night.”

 

“I do,” Rio said. “Come in. Close the door behind you.”

 

The younger maid nudged the leatherworker with her elbow; he turned and pushed the door shut, the latch catching with a quiet click. The chamber felt smaller for the number of bodies in it, firelight casting shadows against walls. Even then, that newly familiar presence tugged faintly along the bond—muted, turned inwards, but there. It sat under Rio’s breastbone like a folded hand.

 

She ignored it, or tried to.

 

“You have been told we depart at first light,” Rio said.

 

“Yes, Your Majesty.” The woman swallowed. “We were informed the princess—that is, Her Ladyship—lacks certain… articles you deem necessary for the road.”

 

“That I deem necessary if the road refuses to behave,” Rio corrected, but without heat. She gestured toward the small table near the hearth. “Lay that gown there.”

 

The maid obeyed at once. The gown spilled across the wood in a careful tumble of fabric. Rio recognised the cut now, high at the throat, tight at the ribs, sleeves that went entirely to waste beneath the heavy ceremonial overgowns. Beautiful. Useless.

 

“The measurements?” she asked.

 

The male tailor stepped forward with the ledger, holding it as if it were a holy text. “We brought her latest,” he said. “Taken for the wedding garments. There has been no time for her to change much since, save perhaps—” He cut himself off, as if aware he had strayed too near a line.

 

Rio took the ledger from him. Lines of ink marched neatly down the page: height, shoulder width, waist, hip, arm. Someone had annotated the notes with the occasional practical comment—prefers looser sleeve; complains of seams—and Rio felt a brief, irrational flicker of gratitude toward whichever seamstress had bothered to note what the girl liked rather than simply forcing cloth upon her.

 

“She has not been easy to dress, I gather,” Rio said mildly, eyes on the page.

 

The woman tailor winced. “She has… opinions, Your Majesty.”

 

Good, Rio thought. Let her keep them.

 

Aloud, she only said, “Her opinions will not be offended by what I ask of you tonight. I require very little, but it must be right. A travelling cloak and an under-layer suited to the road. A pair of boots that will not cripple her by noon. Simple, practical. No trailing hems, no velvet. She will be seated in a carriage if all goes as it should—but if it does not, she may have to ride or walk. She will need to be able to move.”

 

The leatherworker shifted his weight, nervous but alert. “Your lands, Majesty?” he asked. “I have never seen them, but I have heard—”

 

“Temperate,” Rio said. “The worst you will be dressing her against is rain, river mist, and long days. The air cools quickly once the sun drops. Roads can turn slick after a single shower. I do not want her shivering in soaked brocade because someone thought embroidery more important than sense.”

 

He nodded, gaze going distant in the way of a man already sketching patterns in his mind.

 

“And colours, Majesty?” the woman asked. “Her Highness usually wears—”

 

“I appreciate what she usually wears,” Rio said, looking up from the ledger. “But for this, I want my colours worked in. Deep green, river-blue, silver. Not gaudy—this is not a court mantle. A clasp at the throat bearing my crest, and another—small—at the shoulder. Nothing that shouts at every traveller on the road, but enough that anyone who knows how to look will understand whose protection she rides under.”

 

They exchanged glances at that, quick and sharp. They were not fools. They knew what Rio was asking of them.

 

“Your Majesty,” the man said slowly, “her existing gowns bear her family’s sigils. If we alter—”

 

“You will not touch those,” Rio said at once. “This will be new work. If cloth is scarce, speak with the stores; if you are refused, send the keeper to me. I will pay what is needed.” She shut the ledger with a quiet thud. “I am not here to erase her past. I am ensuring she is not soaked to the skin at the first change in weather.”

 

The words had come out sharper than she intended. The tailors flinched, then straightened, recognising what lay beneath the tone if not its source.

 

“You will have your cloak, Your Majesty,” the woman said. “And boots.”

 

“In her correct size of course,” the leatherworker added.

 

Rio inclined her head. “Good.”

 

She hesitated then, glancing once toward the adjoining wall as if she might see through it. “I hear she has never travelled far,” she found herself saying, more to the room than to the people in it. “Not properly. Think of a girl who has spent her life in corridors and courtyards and is now to see her first long road from a jolting seat when you are crafting your pieces.”

 

The woman’s expression softened, just for an instant. “Aye, Majesty,” she murmured. “We understand.”

 

“And…” Rio paused, weighing the line between necessity and indulgence, between what she could admit to herself and what she could not. “Make the cloak light enough not to smother her in the sun, but with a lining that sits soft on the skin. No coarse seams at the neck or under the arms. If she is to be bounced along for the duration of the journey, I would rather she not arrive rubbed raw because no one thought past the first mile.”

 

That was, she told herself, simple pragmatism. A consort distracted by discomfort tired faster, complained sooner, made for slower travel and poorer spirits among the escort.

 

“You will not have time for anything ornate,” she added, letting practicalities settle over the moment. “And I am not asking you for it. A clean cut will do. Sturdy stitching. You have the night. If you require extra hands, take them. Do you believe you can get this done?”

 

The man swallowed. “This is… much, Majesty. But not impossible.”

 

“That is good,” Rio replied. “Because it must be done.”

 

“And payment, Majesty?” the leatherworker asked, almost apologetically. “We will use better materials if you wish her truly dry and steady footed. Her Grace is… careful with such matters.”

 

“I have no doubt,” Rio said dryly. “My steward will see you given what you need. If he hesitates, tell him I ordered it. If your wages are docked for any of this, you will send word to my capital and I will see the shortfall repaid twice over. I will not have you punished for doing what I asked you to do.”

 

It was that, more than the promise of coin, that seemed to ease something in their faces. Work was one thing; the threat of a disgruntled patron afterwards was another.

 

“Thank you, Majesty,” the woman said quietly.

 

Rio nodded once, brisk. “Go. Work. Wake who you must. Remember, you are not making finery for a feast. You are outfitting the queen’s consort for a road that does not always do as it is told. Treat it with the seriousness you would any important order, and all will be well.”

 

They bowed again, even lower this time, and began to gather their things. The maid refolded the gown with reverent hands, as if loath to leave it exposed to smoke and drafts. At the door, the leatherworker hesitated and glanced back.

 

“Your Majesty,” he ventured, “I have always liked her Ladyship. It is good to see she will be going someplace …better than this.”

 

Rio did not respond to that. She did not trust herself to. She gave another curt nod instead which was reciprocated before the door was shut lightly leaving her alone once again in a room that was not truly her own.

 

“This is politics,” she told herself, under her breath, as she moved to snuff two of the candles. “Optics. A consort arriving in tattered pieces reflects poorly on a sovereign. A consort drenched and blistered reflects worse.”

 

But when she lay down later, cloak and boots already halfway to being brought into existence for a woman who did not yet know they were coming, it was not treaties or appearances she thought of as sleep crept up on her.

 

It was the thought that this may be the first time in that woman’s life that she would be presented with something made not to display her, but to carry her safely forward. And that thought caused something to ache within her as she eventually drifted to sleep.

Chapter End Notes

Damn I really just wrote another chapter without them interacting. I swear I don’t even do this on purpose, it pains me to do it but I think it was necessary to get their perspectives. I promise next chapter they will be reunited and hopefully I’ll have made it worth the wait. Not too much on either of them. They’re imperfect and are both just trying their best with the crew life dealt them. Also are we clocking something yet? No? Okay goodnight. 💜💚

Chapter 8: The Journey Pt 1: Never Truly Alone

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Agatha awoke before the sun.

 

She did not rise with haste. She lay very still, in the same bed she had laid in since she had outgrown a cot, and let the knowledge of it sit on her chest like stone.

 

This would be the last morning she woke here.

 

By nightfall she would be far along the road to another land. Another castle. Another court. Another bed.

 

To a place where she knew no one at all, save the woman who would be taking her there.

 

The woman who had bought her hand in marriage in exchange for a throne.

 

The woman who organised their departure without so much as a word of warning.

 

And the same woman who the River had seen fit to tie her soul to in a cruel, cosmic jest.

 

She stared at the empty wall to her side and felt that torturous tug in her chest once again. It seemed to have a nack for making itself known at the least desirable moments. She turned her face into the pillow until it faded into something bearable.

 

You will not swallow me, she thought, and she did not know whether she addressed the bond, the woman, or the life ahead. Not as this place tried to do.

 

Whatever waited for her at the end of this road, she would not let it silence her. Not as they had done here. If she had to live on pure spite alone, then so be it. Spite had sharper teeth than hope.

 

With that promise in mind she breathed in the scent of her sheets one final time before pulling them back away from her body. The cool morning air hit her in an instant. It had that particular chill that belonged to the hour before sunrise, damp and thin, nosing under her nightdress as she stood. She rubbed her arms and looked around her chamber, really looked, the way one might look at a body prepared for burial.

 

It was bare in a way she had never seen it.

 

The dressing table was stripped of its clutter. Only the faint ghost rings remained, pale on the polished wood, marked where pots of kohl and combs and trinkets had once stood. The few books she had gathered over the years, snuck behind her mother’s back, often with the help of Jen, were gone—hopefully all carefully boxed by servants and not thrown out. The rug beside her bed, worn soft in one spot where her bare feet had landed every morning, looked suddenly lonely, already half-forgotten.

 

She crossed to the window and laid her fingers on the stone where she always rested her elbow to look down into the gardens. The stone was cool, its familiar roughness catching against her skin. Soon, no one would stand there again in such a way.

 

“Good riddance,” she murmured to the emptiness, but the words did not quite ring true.

 

Perhaps it was the small part of her that had always hoped this place would become more than a place of residence and less like a prison.

 

Her palm drifted from the sill to the wall, tracing the places where tapestries had hung, where shelves had been, where her life had taken up space. A little inventory of absence. Here the hooks where her cloaks had hung. Here the peg that had always caught the hem of her gown if she turned too sharply. Here the corner where she had sat with a book in her lap and dreamt of anywhere but here—and now “anywhere” had become “there,” and there was little comfort in it.

 

When there was nothing left to touch, nowhere left for her fingers to land that did not feel stripped and cold, she drew a steadying breath and crossed to the inner door.

 

Her chamber opened into a small sitting room, a luxury granted when she had come of age where she had outgrown the confines of her bedchamber but her mother did not want her wandering around—or perhaps she had simply not wanted her around herself. It had always been the warmest part of her rooms with cushions strewn, books hidden in various nooks and crannies and a shawl or two draped over the back of a chair.

 

Now it was as bare as the bedchamber.

 

Well. Almost.

 

On the centre table and couch, a set of items had been carefully placed with almost ceremonial precision.

 

She paused in her step before inching closer in curiosity.

 

On the table lay a pair of leather gloves. They looked costly from sight alone, though not in the way she’d grown used to her mother’s possessions appearing costly. The leather was fine, the cut neat, warm enough to ward off a chill yet stylish in a way that suggested quiet prestige. Beside them lay a folded underdress. Draped over one side of the couch was a deep forest green tunic-dress, simple yet well cut.

 

Her eyes trailed to the other side of the sofa where a darker green cloak hung.

 

And at the foot of the couch, set squarely together on the rug, a pair of boots.

 

Not the dainty, heeled things her mother favoured for court. These appeared built to last with dark leather burnished to a soft sheen, soles thick and ridged for grip, the stitching small and sure along the seams. There was a little extra padding at the ankle, she noticed as she peered closer, where the leather might otherwise rub.

 

For a ridiculous moment, she glanced back towards her bedchamber, half expecting to see her mother lurking in the doorway with a disapproving purse of her lips.

 

But Evanora Harkness would never have commissioned such things. There was too much sense in them. Too little pride.

 

She reached out and let her fingers brush the cloak first. The material was smooth but weighty, the kind that might shrug off rain for a time. On the clasp a small sigil had been engraved, matching a second, larger one on the shoulder stitched in dark thread, subtle enough in colour that it was barely noticeable in the dimness.

 

Her family’s crest was a serpent wound around a burning branch.

 

This was something else entirely.

 

She took the cloak up in both hands and pulled it closer, heart beginning to beat harder despite herself.

 

There, sewn with care, was the queen’s mark. A dark oval meant to be a river stone, its centre stitched with faint, flowing lines like current. Around it, a ring of thorned vines had been worked in finer thread, their tiny barbs forming a jagged crown.

 

Placed strategically where no one with eyes could fail to see it when the clasp lay at her throat.

 

Her eyes drifted to the other items laid out. The boots bore the same emblem pressed into the leather at the outer heel. The tunic had it worked, discreet and careful, at the collar.

 

Her mind supplied the possible answers, sharp and quick, each one cutting at a different angle.

 

A brand. A sign to every eye between here and the border that she belonged now to the Vidal throne. Property marked and owned.

 

A warning, perhaps, to those who liked to touch what did not belong to them that touch this and you answer to me.

 

Or less likely, and therefore most dangerous to consider. A thought spared for a girl who owned nothing fit for the journey she was about to embark.

 

An act of care.

 

Her throat tightened. She set the cloak down as if it might burn her.

 

Whatever it was, whatever intention had guided the queen’s hand when she had given the order for these things to be made, Agatha would not soften herself around it. She would not stand in this stripped room and imagine gentleness where there might be only ownership.

 

Her fingers lingered, unwillingly, on the padding at the ankle of one boot. It would keep the leather from chafing. Someone had thought of that. Of blisters and bruises.

 

She snatched her hand back.

 

Entertaining that line of thought was foolish. She could deal with ownership. She had dealt with it all her life.

 

What she was less versed in was kindness.

 

 

The courtyard below bustled with movement.

 

From the top of the broad front steps, Agatha stood in her new cloak and boots and watched servants dart between carts and horses, their arms full of trunks and bundled cloth, the breath of the animals steaming faintly in the thin morning light. The air was full of the thud of footsteps, the clatter of metals and wood and the low calls of stablehands and guards.

 

At the centre of it all waited the queen’s carriage

 

Well, carriage was an understatement for the beast that stood before them.

 

Agatha had never seen anything like it before. Four dark carriage stood nose to nose to tail, their frames far heavier than of those she’d seen others ride in growing up. The wheels were branded with iron, the wood glossed and well kept. Each door bore the river stone crest on its side, painted in muted colours that still managed to catch the eye. The glass appeared tinted, so dark she could barely make out a thing inside from where she stood. Rows of tall, dark horses stood in front, prepared to pull its full weight.

 

Behind the carriage, a line of covered wagons sagged under the weight of luggage and supplies, and riders were already mounted at the head and tail of the column, the queen’s colours and crest hanging in narrow banners from their spears.

 

It looked less like a royal convoy and. More like a small, moving fortress.

 

She could feel the queen beside her, even without looking at her. The feeling of eyes glancing over at her periodically being a big giveaway.

 

Agatha kept her own gaze fixed forward.

 

They had exchanged pleasantries when they first arrived. A brief, polite good morning. That had been enough. Since then she was trying her hardest to avoid eye contact until absolutely necessary. She’d rather not look at her other woman wearing a suspiciously similar cloak to her own and wonder about what exact message she was trying to put out.

 

Thankfully, there had been no formal breakfast that morning. She would not have been able to avoid another meal and the last thing she needed was to make a fool of herself at another breakfast right before being entrapped with nth other woman for what was to be a long, torturous journey.

 

They had sent up another tray instead while she dressed. It had looked far closer to the sort of meal she was accustomed to than the laden spread from the previous day. Simple. Manageable.

 

She knew she should have tried to eat as much as possible before the journey but she had only managed three small spoonfuls of oats before her stomach had twisted and closed like a fist. She had gone longer without food before she told herself as she tried to ignore the voice in her head that reminded her just how many days it had been since she had last eaten a proper meal.

 

Below, a herald stepped forward and lifted his staff. His voice rang out across the courtyard, announcing Her Majesty of the Riverlands and Her Grace the Consort, naming the journey, blessing the road in the River’s name. People bowed. Guards thumped spear butts against stone. Somewhere, faintly, a bell chimed.

 

Agatha’s spine stayed straight. Her hands were steady at her sides. She did not look at the queen.

 

When the formalities were done, they began their descent.

 

The steps felt longer than they ever had before. Each one took her farther from the rooms she had grew up in, from the life she had known. At the base, the first of the linked carriages awaited, its door open, a guard standing ready to close it as soon as they were inside.

 

They had taken no more than three steps towards it when a voice cried her name.

 

“Agatha!”

 

The sound pierced through her like an arrow she barely had time to urn before something small and solid collided with her front.

 

Arms wrapped tight around her waist. The impact drove a short, startled breath from her lungs. For a heartbeat she stiffened, then her body recognised the shape and weight of the embrace and she folded down without thinking, her own arms coming up to clutch the girl who had thrown herself at her.

 

“Jen,” she breathed, the word cracking on her tongue.

 

She felt, rather than saw, the queen go stiff beside her. The slight shift of weight. The way the air changed briefly, growing colder and heavier. Out of the corner of her eye she almost caught the brief twitch of a hand towards the hilt at the queen’s hip before it stilled.

 

But Jen was crying into the front of her cloak, and that tore her attention back.

 

“I tried to come sooner,” Jen said in a rush, her words muffled. “My mother held me back, she thought it would be better to let you go without a fuss. As if I could ever do such a thing.” She pulled back use enough for Agatha to see her face, eyes bright and wet. “You’ll write to me, wont you? Promise me you’ll write? I’ll die of boredom here if you don’t.” She tried to pass it off as a light joke but Agatha flet the undercurrent of her words. I need to know you are safe and alive.

 

Agatha’s own throat had closed too tight for speech. She managed a nod, fingers digging into the fabric at Jen’s shoulders.

 

She wanted very much to say you are the only thing I shall miss about this place. She wanted to say don’t let me go. Please dont let me go. She wanted, absurdly to grab her hand and run away like they had always whispered about in the quiet of the night.

 

But her legs remained rotted to the ground and when she opened her mouth, nothing came.

 

Jen seemed to read the struggle there, because she gave a watery little laugh and hugged her again, hard.

 

“Good,” she said, as if Agatha had answered. “Good. Then I’ll be fine.“ She leaned back in, her nose brushing against the edge of Agatha’s ear the next part. “Do not let them make you small. Remember you are still Agatha, my best friend no matter what your last name or title is. You are the strongest and bravest person I have ever known. Don’t you dare forget that. And do not let them forget either. Remember you are never truly alone, I will always be there for you, no matter the distance between us.”

 

Agatha made a sound that might have been a laugh and might have been a sob.

 

“Lady Kale.” Her mother’s voice cut across the courtyard like a knife.

 

Jen flinched. Agatha felt her grip tighten, rebellious for a heartbeat longer, before duty—or fear—won out. Evanora stood at the foot of the steps, composed as ever, her hand resting lightly on the rail. Her gaze on Jen was cool enough to freeze a stream.

 

“That is quite enough,” she said. “Her Ladyship must go. You are making a spectacle.”

 

Jen pulled back reluctantly, scrubbing at her cheeks with the heel of her hand. She gave Agatha one last, desperate look.

 

“We will meet again,” she whispered. “I swear it.”

 

Agatha nodded again because she still did not trust her voice.

 

Evanora’s fingers closed around Jen’s arm, apparently tired of the display. She drew the girl back towards the e step, dismissing the scene with a sight turn of her hand. To everyone else it looked like a brief interruption of proceedings.

 

To Agatha the moment, it felt like the last thread being cut.

 

“Your Grace.”

 

The queen’s voice drew her attention back to her surroundings. Agatha realised she was standing half turned towards the steps, fingers still curled in the air as if Jen’s sleeve were there.

 

She smoothed her hand down the front of her cloak and forced her feet to move.

 

The carriage door was still open. The queen stood beside it, a gloved hand raised in invitation.

 

For a moment, pride alone kept her still. She did not need help to climb a single step. She did not need the queen’s hand.

 

Then she felt the eyes on her. The court’s her mother’s. The queen’s. Refusing that hand, here would be as much of a scene as Jen’s embrace had bee. More, perhaps.

 

She placed her fingers in the queen’s.

 

The grip that closed around hers was firm but warm. It steadied her in that brief moment when one foot left the ground and the world tilted. She stepped up into the carriage and released the hand at once, taking her seat beside the window as the queen followed her in.

 

The door shut behind them with a solid, final sound. More of the queen’s people loaded into the carriages behind them. A guard’s voice finally called an order. Outside, wheels creaked, harness jingled, the shout travelled down the line. The carriage lurched as the horses leaned into their traces and the whole train began to move.

 

Agathas gaze turned to the window as if pulled.

 

Through the thick glass she saw the courtyard slide past. Servants thinning away into doorways. Guards peeling off to take their places along the column. Her mother standing at the bottom of the steps, hands folded, expression as smooth and unreadable as ever.

 

Beside her, Jen bounced on her toes, waving wildly  as if she might somehow bridge the growing distance with sheer determination. Agatha lifted her own hand, pressing her palm to the glass rather than raising it, unable to bring herself to wave back and make it real.

 

The castle she had grown up in, with its stone and towers and narrow windows, began to shrink as they rolled towards the gates. The walls she had walked every day of her life slid past, then the gates, then the outer town with its roofs and streets and smoke.

 

Soon they would be beyond even that. Out on the open road, between one life and the next.

 

Agatha watched until the last glimpse of the familiar walls was gone, until all that remained in the window was the pale strip of sky and the line of the road running forward into an uncertain distance.

 

Then, very slowly, she drew her hand back from the glass.

 

 

They sat there in silence as the first stretch of the journey unfolded, neither speaking a word nor paring a glance at the other.

 

The carriage rocked and jolted around them, filling the small compartment with the steady rattle of wheels on and the thud of hooves.

 

Agatha sat with her back to the cushioned seat, hands folded in her lap, gaze fixed stubbornly on the window. The world outside lurched past in narrow strips. The last crooked houses of the outer town, then fields lined with low stone walls and trees with burnt leaves reaching up against a pale sky.

 

Across from her, the queen sat with the same poise Agatha had begun to recognise. She did not fill the space in the way she would have expected someone of her legacy to. Something that still caught Agatha off guard. There was no looming, no theatrical enact. Only a controlled stillness, and the faintest sense of pressure.

 

The bond coiled between them, quiet but stubborn. If Agatha allowed her mind to drift too close to the edges of it, she could feel the queen’s presence there—a steady pulse, distant and infuriatingly unreadable.

 

She kept her attention on the glass until her own reflection blurred with the landscape.

 

They rode like that for some time before the queen spoke.

 

“The girl who embraced you,” she said. Her voice low and unhurried.. “On the steps.”

 

Agatha’s spine tightened.

 

She did not turn fully, but her eyes shifted from the window to the woman in front of her, sharp and wary. “What of her?”

 

“Is she a friend?” The queen asked.

 

The question was simple enough. It should have been harmless. Still, something in Agatha bristled.

 

“Yes,” she said, the word clipped. “She is my friend.”

 

A beat. The queen’s gaze did not move from her face.

 

“My only one,” Agatha added, before she could stop herself. It slipped out sideways, bitterer than she had meant it to sound. “If that satisfies Your Majesty’s curiosity.”

 

One of the queen’s brows twitched, almost a flinch.

 

“I did not mean to pry,” she said. “You seemed… close. I thought you might miss her.”

 

The gentleness of it—if it was gentleness—made Agatha’s shoulders knot rather than ease. She turned fully now, facing the queen instead of the window, studying her as if looking for the catch.

 

“You thought,” she repeated.

 

The queen held her look without flinching. “She cares for you,” she said. “Anyone with eyes could see that. It is no small thing to lose such a person, even for a time.”

 

Heat pricked at the backs of Agatha’s eyes. She looked away quickly, out towards the fields, jaw tight.

 

“I will manage,” she said. “As I am expected.”

 

Silence settled again, thicker this time.

 

The carriage swayed. Somewhere ahead, a rider called to another; the sound came muffled through wood and leather. Agatha could feel the queen watching her still. Not staring, exactly, but present. Attentive.

 

It set her teeth on edge.

 

After a while, she turned back, anger sparking up through the sadness like dry tinder catching.

 

“Why are you asking me these things?” she demanded. “About Jen. Whether I will miss her.”

 

The queen’s expression did not change, but something in her gaze faltered. “Because she seems to matter to you,” she said. “And because you are leaving her behind on my account. It seemed… courteous to acknowledge that.”

 

“You do not have to sit there and pretend to care.” The words came out hotter than she had planned. “You have done what you came for. You have your throne. You need not pass the time by playing at concern.”

 

The queen’s lips parted, then closed again. For a moment, she looked almost at a loss.

 

“I am not—”

 

“I do not understand you,” Agatha pressed on, the floodgates open now. The ache of farewell, the stripped room, the River’s cruel binding, all of it shoved itself into her throat at once. “I do not know what you want from me. One moment you behave as if you are…” she groped for the word, hated it, used it anyway, “…as if you are concerned. The next you make decisions that tear me from my home weeks before acceptable in the eyes of the court, without so much as a word.”

 

Her hands had curled tightly in her lap. She forced them to uncurl, to lie flat, to look graceful and unbothered. Her voice failed to follow suit.

 

“What do you want from me?” Agatha demanded, leaning forward now, unable to keep her seat as straight and still as the queen’s. “Just tell me. Tell me now, and I will do it, and we may both have some semblance of peace.”

 

The queen’s gaze dipped, briefly, to her hands, then back to her face. “I do not—”

 

“Do you want,” Agatha cut across her, “a quiet, obedient wife to stand at your side and look pretty while you rule?”

 

The carriage seemed to grow smaller around them. The air between them thinned.

 

“Do you wish us to pretend at love,” she went on, “so the courts may sigh and say what a fortunate match it is, how romantic, how blessed.”

 

Her voice dropped without her willing it to, the words slipping out softer, edged with something darker. She shifted forward on the seat, closing some of the space between them, eyes never leaving the queen’s.

 

“Do you want me in your bed,” she asked, each word deliberate, “to warm your sheets after a long day? To ease your temper. To satisfy your desires. To make you feel powerful in your own chambers?”

 

The queen went very, very still.

 

For a heartbeat, the only sound was the rattle of the wheels and the faint jangle of harness. The bond surged, a sharp, startled flare beneath Agatha’s ribs that was not hers.

 

“That is enough,” the queen said.

 

Her voice had not risen, but there was a cutting edge to it now that had not been there before. The kind of edge that would have made men twice her size think twice about pushing her further.

 

Agatha’s mouth curved, humourless. If she were about to be murdered  for insolence she may as well clear her mind.

 

“Then what do you want?” she asked, frustration spiking. “Because I can be all of those things, if that is what you require. I have been raised for little else. But I would rather know now than keep guessing in the dark.”

 

The queen stared at her as if she had spoken a language no one had warned her she might ever hear.

 

“I do not want you for any of those things,” she said at last.

 

The words lay between them.

 

Agatha laughed, a sharp bitter sound. “No? Then what did you enter this arrangement for exactly? You have yet to make that clear, at least to me that is.”

 

The queen’s jaw tightened, for a moment it felt like she was no longer looking at Agatha but something else. Then it cleared and a sharpness replaced it.

 

“I did not enter this mariage under the grounds of love,” she said slowly, each word chosen with care, “or other…frivolous feelings.” Her mouth pressed thin over the phrase. “It was an act of duty. To my realm. To yours. Nothing more.”

 

The admission did little to settle the confusion weighing heavier on Agatha’s mind by the day.

 

Agatha tilted her head and folded her arms across her chest before she spoke again.

 

“You paid quite a high price for ‘nothing more’, Your Majesty,” she said. The title came out like a blade.

 

The queen’s jaw worked once, twice. Something dangerous flickered in her eyes then—controlled, but cracking at the edges. For a moment Agatha thought she might snap back, or call for the driver to halt and have her thrown bodily from the carriage for her insolence.

 

Instead, the queen only looked at her. The carriage swayed; the world outside went on sliding past. Inside, the silence stretched taut.

 

The bond pulsed, restless, as if unsettled by the words they had thrown into the narrow space.

 

At last, the queen drew in a slow breath and let it out again.

 

“You are right,” she said, and the simple admission startled Agatha almost more than anything that had come before. “About one thing, at least.”

 

Agatha’s brows lifted, sceptical despite herself.

 

“I did not,” the queen went on, “take into proper account what advancing our departure would cost you. I considered threats. Weather. The temper of my own council. I did not think of…” She paused, the barest crease appearing between her brows. “Of you. Of what it would mean to be dragged away sooner from the only home you have ever known. Of how your people might perceive it.”

 

She glanced down, briefly, at her own hands, then back up.

 

“For that, I apologise,” she said. The words were plain, without flourish. “It was thoughtless. I am unused to my decisions bearing so much personal weight on another’s reputation and feelings. You do not deserve that and I will try to do better.”

 

Agatha had not realised she was holding her breath until it left her in a small, uneven rush.

 

She had expected denial. Justification. A lecture on politics and safety and how little her feelings weighed against a throne.

 

Not this.

 

Suspicion rushed in to fill the space where her anger had been. “Words are easy, Your Majesty,” she said, but the bite was dulled. “Especially on the road.”

 

“Perhaps,” the queen allowed. “Still. You asked what I want of you.” She straightened, shoulders squaring as if bracing for some sort of blow. “The truth is, I expect nothing.”

 

Agatha’s eyes narrowed. “Nothing,” she echoed, flat.

 

“You owe me nothing beyond the vows you have already spoken,” the queen said. “When we reach my court, you will have your own chambers, your own time. You may go where you please within the palace and grounds, keep what company you choose. No one will cage you or demand that you… perform affection for my sake.” Her mouth twisted, self-mocking. “If you wish to ignore me entirely outside of public duty, you will find me… unbothered.”

 

It was said lightly, almost dry, but there was a strain under it that Agatha’s ear, tuned to such things, did not miss.

 

“This is not captivity, Agatha,” she added, more quietly. “However it may feel now.”

 

Her name on the queen’s tongue sent a strange shiver along her spine. She looked away, down at her hands.

 

“And your friend,” the queen continued, as if the admission had opened a door she might as well walk through, “need not be lost to you. If you wish to write, my riders can carry your letters faster than any common post. We can have messages travel regularly between my court and this one.”

 

Agatha’s head snapped up.

 

For a moment she could only stare, the words lodging somewhere behind her ribs.

 

“Your… riders,” she said slowly.

 

“They ride the river roads,” the queen said. “They change horses at crown posts. It is simple enough to add a pouch of letters to their loads. If you wish.”

 

There was no grandness to the offer. No demand for gratitude. It was presented in the same tone she might have used to discuss the state of the road ahead.

 

All the same, it landed with the weight of something far larger.

 

Agatha felt suddenly, acutely aware of the carriage walls. Of the woman opposite her, who could lead armies with a word and had jut offered to lend her couriers to a single girl’s correspondance.

 

Her first instinct was to recoil from it. Kindness had teeth in her experience; it masked expectation, or worse. It was simpler to live with ownership. Cleaner. Easier to understand. It was also becoming harder to grip onto.

 

“Why?” she asked, before she could swallow it. The word came out smaller than she liked.

 

The queen’s gaze held hers.

 

“Because she matters to you,” she repeated, as if it were the most obvious thing in the world. “And because I am the reason you are leaving her behind.”

 

Agatha looked down quickly, unable to bear the weight of that steady look for more than a heartbeat.

 

“Thank you,” she said, the words stiff and unfamiliar on her tongue. They tasted like surrender and salt.

 

She was not foolish enough to lower her guard over a promise of letters or a pair of boots. But as the carriage rolled on and the landscape unfurled before them, some small, treacherous part of her filed the offer away alongside the padded leather and odd compliments of her hair.

 

Ownership she understood. She had been controlled and paraded like a doll her life long before she had been bought and bartered.

 

Whatever this was, this awkward apology and thoughtful promises… she did not yet have a name for it.

 

And that, more than anything, unsettled her.

 

 

The silence returned after that.

 

Agatha sat there digesting the queen’s words. Twisting and turning them around in her head. Attempting to peel back the layers to discover whatever hidden blade must surely be waiting beneath.

 

I will try to do better.

You owe me nothing.

This is not captivity.

Because she matters to you.

 

Eventually she had to concede that perhaps, just perhaps, she had been wrong.

 

Not entirely. Not enough to loosen her grip on caution. But enough to admit that the woman before her was not quite the neat, simple threat Agatha had plainte in her mind. That perhaps her acts of genuineness were just that—genuine.

 

She allowed herself to consider I the possibility.

 

She would not allow herself to accept it.

 

Accepting it meant lowering her guard. She could not afford that. Not yet.

 

So she sat and thought instead. She walked herself back through every encounter, every exchange. Occasionally, she risked a glance at the woman opposite her.

 

The queen had not moved much since their exchange. She still sat straight and composed, her own cloak falling in clean lines  though good posture had been drilled into her bones as brutally as it had been to Agatha’s.

 

Some part of the facade though had slipped.

 

The air of calm around her had receded to the point Agatha began to suspect it had been feigned from the beginning. A slight frown had made itself home between her brows and her fingers thrummed against her thigh periodically as though she were deep in thought.

 

It struck Agatha suddenly that they were to be stuck in this small carriage for the near foreseeable future. Together. It coud prove to be the most excruciating experience of her life.

 

Or—she could make use of it.

 

The queens fortified walls were lowered the smallest hairline crack but it was enough. Cracks would widen after all. Perhaps this was her only chance, trapped as they both were, to learn something of the woman beyond tales and title. It might help her decide if those apologies and offers were anything but smoke. At the very least, it might give her some sense of the place she was being carried towards.

 

She drew a breath, smoothed her face into something curious and almost mild, and broke the silence herself.

 

“Tell me,” she said, “about your queendom.”

 

The queen’s fingers stilled. She looked up, faintly wary, as if expecting another accusation hidden in the words.

 

“You have asked me this before,” she said, a single brow raised in question.

 

“I was not satisfied by your answers.” Agatha replied with a slight shrug.

 

The queen’s gaze narrowed. “You speak…rather bravely at times.”

 

“Should I be afraid, Your Majesty?”

 

Th queen did not answer immediately. She folded her arms and leaned back eyes remaining on Agatha’s the entire time.

 

“No,” she said at last, carefully. “I suppose not.” A small pause. “Ask your questions. I shall try my best to answer…to your satisfaction.”

 

Agatha tilted her head, studying her.

 

“What is your favourite place within it?” She asked. “Where do you go when you are not…ruling.”

 

The corner of the queen’s mouth twitched, almost a grimace. “A queen is seldom not ruling.”

 

“So i have heard,” Agatha said. “Yet I imagine even you must, at some point, draw breath. Where do you prefer to do so?”

 

“There is a bend in the River just outside the castle walls,”she said slowly.  “A little way upstream. The banks are rock there, not mud. The water runs fast enough to keep itself clear. In summer the spray cools the air, and in winter the mist hangs low. It is… quiet.”

 

Agatha watched her as she spoke. The frown between her brows eased, very slightly, as if the memory smoothed something.

 

“And you go there alone?” she pressed.

 

Something flickered in the queens eyes before it was quickly smothered.

 

“You could say so.” She supplied after a beat.

 

“Would it be untrue?” Agatha pressed further.

 

The queen was silent for a while, when she spoke again, her gaze had gone faintly distant, as though she wasn’t truly seeing Agatha at all.

 

“I suppose I mean,” she said at last, “it often feels as though I am never truly alone.”

 

The words hung there. She did not elaborate.

 

Agatha waited, but no explanation followed.

 

Agatha felt the weight of a dozen possibilities—old ghosts, old sins, the weight of a metaphorical crown that never left her brow.

 

She filed it away with the rest.

 

“And what do you like most about your lands?” she asked next.

 

The queen’s shoulders squared a little; she seemed to shift back into safer territory.

 

“The River,” she said, without hesitation. “It gives us our trade, our harvests, our—”

 

“I know what the River gives your people,” Agatha interrupted, gentle but insistent. “What does it give you?”

 

The queen blinked, as if unused to being separated from the lands she ruled.

 

“It is…” She searched for a word, then let out a breath. “Constant,” she said at last. “It has been there longer than any throne. It will be there when I am gone. It does not care for crowns or councils. I find that… grounding.”

 

Grounding. A strange word from the mouth of a woman whose name was more feared than the River’s floods.

 

Agatha nodded slowly, as if satisfied, though her mind was already turning it over, weighing it against other things she had seen.

 

“And the food?” she went on, unrelenting. “What do you think of the cuisine?”

 

The queen’s brows drew together, not in annoyance but in something like confusion.

 

“We have many influences,” she began. “River fish, of course, and the trade from the ports, and the—”

 

“I did not ask what your cooks can prepare,” Agatha said, unable to keep the ghost of a smile from her lips this time. “What do you enjoy?”

 

For the first time, the queen looked genuinely wrong-footed.

 

She opened her mouth, then closed it again, fingers resuming their light tap against her thigh.

 

“I—” She stopped, exhaled through her nose as if she had just faced down an enemy captain. “There is a stew I have whenever I pass through the outer towns of my queendom,” she said at last. “With beans and smoked meats. It is simple, but it…satisfies.”

 

“Beans,” Agatha echoed, amused despite herself. “Lady Death, terror of half the realms , felled by a pot of beans.”

 

Something in the queen stiffened momentarily before she let out a huff. Not quite a laugh, but near enough to count. Agatha briefly wondered what part of her sentence had triggered the reaction.

 

“I did not claim it was impressive,” she said dryly. “You asked what I liked, not what I would serve to impress visiting dignitaries.”

 

“And what would you serve to impress them?” Agatha asked at once.

 

The queen shot her a look, belatedly realising the trap she had walked into.

 

“Roasts,” she said, sounding resigned. “River trout. Spiced wine. Pastries filled with honey and almond that the capital’s bakers are inordinately proud of.”

 

“And do you like those?” Agatha pressed. “You, not your bakers.”

 

The queen hesitated.

 

“Yes.” She said at last, as though it were some grand admittance.

 

Agatha hummed, as if that confirmed something for her.

 

She kept going. The questions came in a steady trickle, each one small and innocuous on its own.

 

What season did the queen favour? (Spring, when the ice broke and the River ran wild.) Did she prefer the bustle of the capital or the quiet of the outlying towns? (She preferred the solitude and quiet that came with outlying towns.) Did she ride for pleasure or only for duty? (Both. She had grown up on horseback. Her body did not know how not to move.)

 

Whenever the queen began to answer in the broad, safe strokes of a ruler—my people, my lands, the court—Agatha would gently, relentlessly steer her back.

 

“I asked about you,” she would say.

 

At first, it seemed to fluster the queen more than she would have thought possible. She shifted minutely against the seat, cleared her throat, glanced away as if searching for rescue in the passing hedgerows. Her answers were halting, spare, as though she were pulling them out of some half-forgotten, unused part of herself.

 

Agatha watched. She listened. She filed everything away.

 

The queen liked dawn better than dusk, it turned out; not for the symbolism, but because the world felt cleaner then, less tangled. She preferred the sound of rain on tile to music played for her at court. She did not admit to it but Agatha suspected she had a secret sweet tooth. She had never cared for falconry, despite her advisers insisting it was fitting for a ruler to be seen with a hawk on her wrist.

 

“I prefer my hands free,” she said, when Agatha raised a brow at that. “It is difficult to reach for a sword with a bird attached to you.”

 

There, at least, was a glimpse of the stories Agatha had heard.

 

As the carriage rocked on, the questions slowed. Not because she had exhausted them—far from it—but because she sensed that pressing further for now might snap whatever fragile thread had allowed the queen to speak this much of herself.

 

Outside, the road had grown rougher. The steady rhythm of the wheels broke every so often into a harder jolt as they hit a rut or a stone hidden in the dirt.

 

One such jolt came without warning.

 

The carriage lurched sharply to one side. Agatha’s hand, resting on the seat beside her, slipped. Her shoulder tipped, balance deserting her with the sudden shift of weight.

 

Before she could catch herself, a hand closed around her forearm.

 

The queen had moved without thinking. One moment she sat opposite, safely out of reach; the next her gloved fingers were braced just below Agatha’s elbow, grip firm and anchoring, keeping her from sliding off the cushioned bench.

 

For a heartbeat, they froze like that.

 

Agatha’s other hand flew out, catching at the edge of the seat. Her breath had caught somewhere between chest and throat. The bond flared, sharp and bright, at the point of contact, as if startled awake.

 

She looked up.

 

The queen was closer than she had seemed a moment before. The frown between her brows had deepened, not with anger but with sudden, focused concern.

 

Their gazes locked, held.

 

For a suspended moment the rattle of the wheels dropped away, the world narrowing to the feel of fingers on her arm and the steady—almost too steady—look in dark eyes.

 

Then the carriage righted itself. The moment broke.

 

The queen released her at once, as if she had only just realised she was still holding on.

 

“My apologies,” she said, retreating a fraction, her voice returning to its usual even timbre. “The road is worse here than I expected.”

 

She rapped her knuckles against the carriage wall, a signal to the driver to mind the ruts. The familiar air of command settled back over her like a cloak.

 

Agatha sat back, smoothing her sleeve where the imprint of that grip still tingled against her skin. Her heartbeat took its time to settle.

 

“Thank you,” she said, because anything else would have sounded like too much.

 

The queen inclined her head, as if she had merely done something as ordinary as passing a cup.

 

Silence came again, but it felt different now. Less like a wall. More like a narrow bridge, rickety and frail, stretching between two points that had not, until this road, ever planned to meet.

The girls are finally reunited, how do we feel? Hope it was worth the wait. Hopefully I don’t have to write two chapters of zero interaction again for a while because that was lowkey painful.

Also wanted to point out yes I in fact do go back and change certain small details or add on stuff whenever I think of something more interesting sue me. Hopefully I havent confused anyone though I really doubt anyone truly notices besides my brain.

In my head it feels like these previous chapters have been setting up the story and we’re only now entering the parts I’m truly excited for, perhaps because I originally planned all theses chapters to take place in the span of five ish but the story ran away from me entirely but here we are anyways and I’m actually quite happy with how it all turned out.

Chapter 9: The Journey Pt 2: The Halt

Chapter Notes

‼️Massive Tw for depictions of restricted eating/disordered eating. It is a big part of the chapter and not just a brief passage so please take care and if that sort of thing may trigger you feel free to skip. Though I must say it will be a recurring theme throughout the story. ‼️

See the end of the chapter for more notes

The silence stretched. 

Neither of them spoke. The only sounds were the steady rattle of the wheels and the soft thud of hooves against the rough country roads. Across from her, the queen sat as she always seemed to sit—too straight, too still, gloved hands resting one atop the other— as if the motion of the road did not touch her at all. 

Agatha could still feel the slight warmth where the queens hand had gripped her elbow.  The driver had taken more care since; no fresh excuse for hands to reach, or for the space between them to vanish. Thankfully.
