Her thoughts circled back over the conversation that had come before. The queen’s answers lay in her mind like cards on a table. She turned them over, one by one, searching for the trick.

The apology that had seemed to catch in her throat.

The offer of letters.

The halting admission that she had places she ran to when the walls of her own court closed in.

Nothing had sounded like the smooth, practised lies Agatha had grown up around. There had been awkwardness instead. A certain roughness at the edges, as if the words were not used to leaving that tongue. Some answers had been oblique, circling the heart of a thing without quite touching it, but when she tested them for falsehood she found…very little to seize.

Which did not mean there was none. It only meant that, if the queen was lying, she was doing it in ways Agatha had yet to learn.

The bond lay quiet between them, a stubborn coil beneath her ribs. If she let her attention drift too close to it, she could feel the queen’s presence there—a steady weight. Both distant and unreadable. She drew back from it the way one might draw a hand from a flame. She had had enough of being burned.

Her body, unfortunately had far less discipline than her mind. 

The hollow ache in her middle had been a quiet thing all morning, something she could push to the edges of her awareness. She had fed it the same phrases she always did when it complained. You have gone longer without. You do not need it right now. You can endure this. The few bites of oats she had that morning were enough for her despite what her body said. Agatha knew her limits. She knew when she had to cut back and when she was allowed to indulge.  She had kept things under control for so long her mind had formed a rhythm of routines and rules. 

Perhaps she had miscalculated though. The break in routine these past few days with the chaos of the wedding and the preparations for their abrupt departure had admittedly pushed her further beyond her boundaries than she liked. 

Now, the gentle rocking of the carriage did not help the hollow ache or the dull throb behind her eyes. She shifted her weight as subtly as she could, willing the feeling away.

Time slipped. It might have been minutes; it might have been longer. The rhythm of the wheels had a way of stretching moments thin until they lost their edges. Agatha fixed her gaze on a lone tree in the distance and watched it draw near, then fall behind.

Her stomach chose that moment to speak.

The sound was not a delicate complaint. It was a long, low growl that seemed to echo in the quiet of the carriage, loud even over the muffled clatter of the road. Heat shot up her neck. Agatha went very still, jaw tightening, as if she could hold the noise inside her by force of will alone.

Do not. she thought at the queen, absurdly, as if thoughts could command a sovereign. Do not say anything. Pretend you did not hear.

The silence after that felt sharp. Different.

Agatha kept her eyes on the window until the blurred outline of her own face stared back at her in the glass. Only when the queen spoke did she allow them to flicker away briefly.

“When did you last eat, Your Grace?”

The question was simple. No censure in it. It cut like a blade all the same.

Agatha’s fingers tightened where they rested in her lap. “This morning,” she said, aiming for careless. “They sent up a tray. I had a few bites.”

Her voice sounded almost normal to her own ears. That had to count for something. She kept her eyes on the glass, watching her reflection’s mouth move instead of the real woman before her.

“I do not usually take much in the early hours,” she added, as if that might make it sound less of a problem and more of a routine.

The queen did not let the silence fold back over them. “And yesterday?” she asked. “The meals sent to your chamber—were they to your liking?”

Yesterday.

Agatha’s breath caught, just a fraction. Images rose unbidden. The trays that strayed so far from what her mother would usually arrange for her. She had thought them odd at the time. She had not thought to question it aloud. 

Her gaze slid from the window at last, drawn across the cramped space until it met the queen’s. “That was you,” she said, before she could talk herself out of it. “You sent those.”

It was not really a question.

Something in the queen’s expression shifted. A flicker, there and gone, as if Agatha had pressed a thumb against a bruise. She did not look away, but the line of her shoulders altered, a touch less sure.

“I asked the kitchen to adjust them,” she replied. “It seemed…wise.”

Her tone held the same evenness as always, yet there was an awkwardness beneath it that Agatha would have sworn she was not imagining. As though the queen were unused to having her small considerations named.

The discovery did something strange in Agatha’s chest. It did not soften. Not quite. But it unsettled the neat little lines she had drawn around the woman in front of her. It was one thing to be bartered to a stranger. It was another to realise that stranger had been offering her far more consideration in the mere days she has known her, than her own mother had in all her years. 

It did not matter, she told herself at once. It changed nothing. She had not eaten those trays either.

“They were fine,” she said, turning her eyes back to the passing fields before her face could betray any of that turmoil. “I simply was not hungry.”

The words came easily; she had been saying them, in one form or another, for most of her life. They tasted no truer now than they ever had.

Across from her, the queen did not call her on it.

She could feel her eyes on her though. Heavy and searching. 

The bond gave a faint, unhappy throb under her ribs, as if it disliked the lie.

Agatha pressed her tongue to the back of her teeth and said nothing more.

The carriage rolled on. For a while, it seemed the queen might let the matter simply die after all. In her silence Agatha let her eyes drift back over to her. She had looked away, gaze shifting towards the shutter that covered her own window. The space between them filled again with the clatter of the road and the muffled call of a rider outside.

Agatha’s shoulders loosened by a fraction. The heat in her cheeks began to ebb. If she could just get through the rest of the day, if she could make it to whatever inn or house they were meant to stop at without collapsing, then perhaps—

The queen lifted one hand and rapped her knuckles twice against the carriage wall.

The signal was sharp, practised. The change outside was immediate. The train began to slow; the rattle eased into a drag, the pitch of the wheels shifting as the horses were reined in. Somewhere ahead, a rider called down the line. Agatha’s head turned despite herself.

The queen raised her voice just enough to carry through the panel. “Driver.”

“Your Majesty?” came the muffled reply from beyond the wood. “Is something amiss?”

“We should be approaching a town,” the queen said. “We will halt there. Take us to the nearest crown inn.”

There was a beat of surprised silence. Agatha could almost see the man’s blink. “We have no halt planned here, Majesty. The first stop is further along the road.”

“I am aware.” Her tone did not sharpen, but it brooked no argument. “We will stop here regardless. Send a rider ahead. Tell them the royal party will require a private room and a simple meal. We will not be long.”

Her hand dropped back to her lap. The decision, apparently, was made.

Agatha stared at her.

The sinking feeling began in her stomach and spread outward, slow and cold, until even her fingers felt hollow. She knew, with a clarity that made her teeth ache, that this was because of her. Because of the rumble her body had betrayed. Because the queen had asked a question and found the answers wanting.

Outside, a shout went up as one of the riders spurred his horse forward, hooves striking sparks as he broke from the line to carry the message ahead. The rest of the convoy shifted with the change in pace, a creature with many legs turning towards whatever small town lay ahead.

Agatha turned her face back to the window, though the view had gone a little blurred. 

The queen had set an entire royal convoy on a different course because Agatha’s stomach had growled. Because she had not eaten. Because she had lied and someone, for once, had refused to pretend not to notice.

It was her worst nightmare.

It was also the first time anyone with power over her had allowed her body to matter more than a schedule. She was unsure which terrified her more.

She kept her eyes on the glass and watched the town grow nearer, each step of the horses carrying her closer to a room she wanted no part of and a table she wished did not exist.

Not long after, the road narrowed. The carriage now pinched between cottages and workshops. Voices drifted in the air—shouts of market ladies, laughter of children, the bark of a dog—all growing louder as the convoy crept closer. 

The horses slowed further as they reached the first houses. Children ran to the verge to stare. A woman with flour on her hands paused in the doorway of a bakery, dusting her palms on her apron as the river queen’s banner passed. Men straightened from their work, caps in hand, the murmurs of the street thinning into a wary hush.

The carriage drew to a halt with a final grinding of wheels. For a moment, nothing moved. Then the door opened from without. Cool air slipped in, edged with the smell of woodsmoke and damp stone and something faintly yeasty from the bakery nearby. One of the queen’s guards stood framed in the gap, bowing his head.

“Majesty,” he said.

Across from her, the queen rose in one smooth motion. The carriage rocked a little under the shift of weight. She stepped forward and, without seeming to think about it, held out her hand.

“Your Grace,” she said, a simple invitation.

For an instant, Agatha considered refusing. Considered crossing her arms and refusing to step out of the carriage at all. She stopped herself though. She knew what they would think of her. A petulant child. Besides something told her the queen would not be one to give in so easily. If she was to be forced into this arrangement she may as well save herself from further humiliation.

So she placed her hand in the queen’s.

The grip was firm, steadying. Glove against bare skin. It was over in a heartbeat; she was guided down, one step, then another, boots meeting solid ground. When she let go, the warmth lingered on her palm.

The rest of the entourage spilled out of the trailing carriages in a clatter of boots and muttered voices. Courtiers and attendants smoothed their cloaks, brushed dust from their sleeves, craned their necks towards the town as if it had personally offended them.

“We are far too early in the road to be stopping,” one of the councillors murmured, not quite low enough. “We will lose light.”

“Indeed,” someone else agreed. “At this rate we shall not reach the first house before nightfall tomorrow.”

Agatha’s shoulders hunched, unbidden. She kept her eyes on the cobbles at her feet, watching a trickle of dirty water thread its way along the gutter.

The queen turned her head, just enough to pin the cluster of them with a look. The street seemed to narrow around it.

“The itinerary,” she said lightly, “is not a law.”

It was a mild enough sentence. It landed like an edict. The complaints stuttered out, unfinished. Men who had faced down nobles at court without flinching suddenly found great fascination in the buckles of their saddles and the state of their gloves.

“The horses are to be watered and walked while we halt,” the queen added. “See to it.”

She did not wait to see if they obeyed. Her attention had shifted already. She lifted two fingers, beckoning a guard from his post at the head of the line.

“Sir Arlen.”

He stepped forward at once, bowing. “Majesty.”

She inclined her head towards the inn. “They have been told to expect us. Go in. Confirm the room is as requested. Doors and windows secured. No strangers loitering where they should not be.” A brief pause. “Station two of our own inside before we enter.”

“Yes, Majesty.”

He went at once, cutting across the street at a brisk stride, disappearing through the inn’s low doorway. 

While they waited, the town gathered.

People who had been pretending not to stare stopped pretending. A boy perched on a rain barrel, legs swinging, eyes round as coins. A young girl stopped int he middle of the rod until her mother pulled her away. The maid from the bakery had come fully into the lane now, wiping her hands on her apron, gaze flicking from the queen’s dark figure to Agatha’s smaller one.

Agatha stood beside the queen, cloak drawn close. The awareness of so many eyes crawled over her skin. She felt as though every seam of her gown, every angle of her face, every breath was being measured and weighed.

She kept her chin level and her gaze fixed somewhere near the middle distance. She would not tuck herself behind the queen like a child. She would not duck her head as if ashamed. Still, she could not shake the sensation that if they stood there much longer, she might shatter.

Beside her, the queen shifted.

It was a small thing—a slight realignment of her weight, the turn of her head—but it changed her whole outline. She was no longer simply a figure in fine dark cloth; she became, quietly, a blade unsheathed.

Agatha followed the movement without meaning to. She watched as the queen’s gaze slipped over the crowd, not lingering on faces but on hands, on shadows, on the dark mouths of alleyways. Her eyes traced rooflines and upper windows, the bend of the street, the way the houses leaned. It was not the curious, hungry stare of courtiers at a spectacle; it was a calculation of angles and exits.

To anyone else, she might have looked merely regal, surveying her people. To Agatha, standing close enough to hear the nearly inaudible breath she took, it felt like watching someone listen for a note only they could hear.

A breeze slipped down the street, tugging at loose hair and loose thatch. Somewhere a dog barked. The queen’s hand curled once at her side, then stilled.

Sir Arlen reappeared in the inn’s doorway and crossed back to them, bowing low.

“Majesty. The back room is as you asked. Our people are at the doors. The keeper has cleared the corner and set out what he has ready.”

“Good.” The queen’s shoulders eased by a fraction. She nodded towards the inn’s sign, still creaking on its chains. “We will not linger.”

The queen turned to Agatha then, her voice lowering. “Come,” she said quietly. “Best we are out of the street.” 

Then she moved, stepping forward into the narrow street as the crowd parted before her, guards falling into place around them like a closing hand.

Agatha gathered her cloak more tightly around her and followed, each step carrying her nearer to certain shame.

The air warmed around them the moment they entered the doorway. It smelled of spiced meat and sweet ale. The warmth did little to loosen the icy fear wrapped around Agatha’s chest. 

A low murmur ran through the common room as they entered. Heads turned, talk faltered and frayed into uneasy silence.

A banner hung above the hearth. Her father’s crest. It would soon be replaced by one belonging to the queen as would all the others across these lands.  It was clear that this was a house that took royal coin. A place that knew how to brace when riders bearing the crest appeared on the road.

It did not make Agatha feel any less out of place within it.

The man who must have been the innkeeper hovered near the threshold, hands brushing nervously along his apron, face flushed from haste. He began bowing the moment the queen stepped over the sill and did not seem able to stop.

“Your Majesty,” he managed. “Your Majesty, Your Grace— for— forgive the crowd, we had no time to clear— the rider said you were near, we’ve made a room ready at the back—”

“It will suffice,” the queen said. “You have done well.”

She did not raise her voice over the common murmur; she did not need to. The room seemed to draw in around her regardless.

She inclined her head towards the rear of the building. “The room?”

“This way, Majesty.” He stepped aside, wiping his palms once more on his apron before leading them along the wall.

They did not cut straight through the centre of the room like a procession. The queen let the innkeeper guide them along the edge, past benches and barrels, guards pacing at their flanks. People shrank back from their passing, pressing themselves against table ends and chair backs. A serving girl stood frozen in the path for a heartbeat, clutching a tray to her chest until one of the guards gently steered her aside.

Agatha kept close to the queen’s shoulder without quite touching her. The closeness of the room pressed in on every side. She could feel stares drag over her as they moved—up and down, weighing, measuring, trying to fit her into stories they had heard and faces they had imagined. The veiled princess, the failed heir, the foreign queen’s new prize.

Her skin crawled.

She fixed her attention on the floorboards beneath her feet. They were worn smooth by years of boots and spilled ale, darkened where countless footsteps had taken the same path further back, to the same back rooms, for better reasons than feeding a consort who did not want to be fed.

The innkeeper shouldered open a narrow door at the end of a short corridor and hurriedly stood aside. “Here, Majesty. It’s our best room for such— for private use. We keep it ready for such events.”

The place beyond was more ornate than the front of the house. The furnishings of higher quality bought with crown money no doubt. A long sturdy table sat in the centre, its polished wood almost out of place for such a quaint establishment.

On one wall, a single window looked out over the yard; its shutters had been drawn most of the way in, leaving only a thin sliver of light along the sill.

Two of the queen’s guards already flanked the doorway from within, boots braced, hands resting near their hilts. Another stood beneath the window, shoulder to the stone, gaze on the yard beyond. Sir Arlen must have been quick.

“It will do,” the queen said.

“Yes, Majesty.” The innkeeper bobbed again. “We’ve set to the stew, and there’s bread from the morning’s baking. The girl is bringing it now. If— if there is aught else your Majesties require—”m

“Tea, if you have it,” the queen said. “Something light. And water brought for the horses out back.”

“Of course. At once.” He backed out, nearly tripping over his own feet in his haste to be gone, and pulled the door to behind him.

The queen took her seat at the head of the table. Agatha heisted before taking her seat at her left, as she had been taught. The rest of the party drifted to the far end of the table. Far enough to give the illusion of privacy though Agatha could not be fooled. She knew her shape was in the corner of every eye. 

It felt less like a meal than an arrangement.

Cloaks settled. Chairs scraped. Murmured conversation at the far end of the table rose and fell in polite little waves, never quite loud enough to drown out the crackle from the hearth or the tightness of her own breathing. The guards at the door and beneath the window stood as if carved there. The queen’s presence at her side pressed close and heavy, even with a full arm’s breadth of space between them.

Her stomach twisted.

It felt as though she had been trapped in a night terror. She had all but two choices. Both brought a sour taste to her mouth.

If she did not eat, they would talk. She could all but hear it already. They would say she has wasted their time. That the precious consort turned up her nose at a common stew. They would see her as the spoiled girl who had dragged a royal convoy off its course for nothing. They would say she was seeking attention. They would call her childish. 

And childish consorts reflected poorly on sovereigns. 

They would not only question her maturity. They would inevitably  question the queens authority. For the moment she had spoken her vows she had stopped existing as only herself and now existed as an extension of the queen as well. She cared little about the queen’s image in that moment. Let her share in the discomfort. But poor optics were not in Agatha’s advantage. 

Queens whose consorts made them look foolish did not stay patient for long. 

How long before the councillors at the far end of the table began to murmur that she was not worth the coin or the disruption? How long before those murmurs reached the queen’s ear and stayed there?

Could she truly trust that the woman who had bought her would still stand at her back once she was an embarrassment instead of a symbol? She doubted it. Even after promises of freedom, and letters, she doubted it. 

She had not asked for this marriage, but she was not foolish enough to ignore what it might buy her. A queen’s favour was a shield sharper than any blade. If the queen chose to stand at her side, who in these lands could safely touch her?

But what queen would keep patience with a consort who could not even bring herself to eat when the convoy halted for her sake? Weakness was tolerable when it stayed hidden. Once seen, it became a reason to discard.

The other option was to eat.

In theory, it was simple enough. Lift the spoon, bring it to her mouth swallow. In practice, it flet like marching under command. 

It was not the idea of the stew itself that turned her stomach. It was the obedience in it. Eating now, after the queen had ordered a halt, while the room watched to see if she would comply. It made it feel as though she was being peeled open in front of all to see. 

She kept her gaze fixed on a knot in the wood and pretended not to feel the weight of the queen’s attention resting on her, or the prickle of half hidden glances from the far end of the table. The back of her neck felt hot, her fingertips cold.

When the bowls came at last, her mind emptied.

Steam rose from the dish in faint, curling threads. The scent of herbs and long-simmered bones struck her first. Her stomach lurched so sharply she thought, for a terrifying instant, that she might disgrace herself then and there.

It was the worst kind of betrayal. Her body surged towards the promise of warmth, of salt and fat and something to fill the gnawing hollow, while her mind pushed back with equal force. The two met in the middle and left her light-headed, throat tight.

Around them, the party fell upon their own portions with a briskness that might have gone unnoticed to anyone else. Here, every clink of spoon against earthenware sounded like accusation. Proof that everyone else could do this simple thing without fuss.

She reached for her spoon because not doing so would have been its own confession. The metal was cool and unforgiving between her fingers. She disturbed the surface of the stew with the lightest of touches, watching the vegetables and slivers of meat sway and settle.

She broke off a piece of bread, tore it into smaller and smaller fragments, nudged them through the broth. Old tricks. Enough, in most halls, to convince the casual eye that she was participating. People saw what they wished to see, especially when distracted by their own plates.

Here, it felt as though every eye in the room had found its way, sooner or later, back to her.

Heat pricked treacherously at the corners of her eyes. She blinked it away with a sharp, angry breath. She would not cry. Not here. Not over this. They already thought her a child; she would not hand them tears as proof.

She took one spoonful in the end. A small, defiant thing, more to prove she still could than out of any desire to taste it. The broth sat heavy on her tongue. She swallowed it down and let the spoon fall back against the side of the bowl with a faint chime that felt, absurdly, like a bell marking her out.

She kept her eyes on the table. She did not look at the queen.

Rio had been watching.

Not staring. She kept her own movements steady, lifted her own spoon now and again to avoid turning the moment into an interrogation. But from the corner of her eye she saw every careful little motion at her left. She watched as the bread reduced to crumbs and then pushed along the rim, the way the girl’s fingers tightened around the spoon without lifting it, the single mouthful finally taken and then nothing more.

“Is it not to your taste?” Rio asked at last, keeping her voice low enough that it would not carry down the full length of the table.

Agatha flinched. Only slightly, but Rio felt it through the bond as much as she saw it—the quick, sharp sting of being addressed, the flare of something that might have been shame or anger. Her eyes stayed fixed on the stew.

“It is fine,” she said. “I am simply not very hungry.”

In the carriage, her stomach had argued otherwise with such force it had rattled over the clatter of the wheels.

“That is difficult to believe,” Rio answered, after a heartbeat. “Given what I heard on the road.”

At that, Agatha’s chin lifted a fraction. Colour rose high along her cheekbones. For a moment Rio thought she might snap at her as she had done in the carriage, sharp and unguarded. Instead, the girl’s throat worked around some unsaid retort. Her fingers tightened on the spoon.

“With respect, Your Majesty,” she said, each word chosen with painful care, “my stomach is my own concern.”

Rio might have let it rest there. Chalked it up to pride and a bad day, to the stubbornness she had already learned lived just under this girl’s skin. She even considered briefly that perhaps the girl simply did not feel comptable eating in the company of strangers. But then she recalled the quick, defensive lie in the carriage when confronted with the truth about the trays.

The lie itself did not truly bother Rio. The possibilities behind it had. A woman who did not eat under public scrutiny was one thing. A woman who could not eat even when left alone and felt driven to hide that fact was something else entirely. It hit hr suddenly that she had truly never witnessed Agatha eat more than a few bites from her plate the entire time she had known her. She had brushed it off before, written it off to stress and nerves. Sitting here now and seeing the girl fight back tears at the sight of a simple bowl of stew left Rio with the creeping suspicion that there was something much deeper amiss.

She recognised that kind of self neglect. She knew it rarely resolved itself simply because others politely pretended not to see. She also knew little good would come bringing attention to it here or demanding her to eat. 

Not everyone shared her opinion apparently. 

From the far end of the table, a voice slid along the murmur of conversation. Not as hushed as the speaker intended.

“She is hardly eating,” it said. “We might as well have kept riding.”

The bond flared hot with Agatha’s answering spike of humiliation and fury. Before her Rio watched as her shoulders tightened. Her head dipped, the fall of her hair concealing her expression. But Rio saw the tremor in her fingers  as she set the spoon down.

Rio did not turn fully toward the sound. She did not need to. She let her gaze travel down the table, slow and cold, until it found the little knot of men huddled near the bread-plate. One of them swallowed, hard. Another suddenly discovered a reason to attend very closely to the pattern of the table grain.

The whisper died. No one rushed to replace it.

Rio let the silence hold for another breath, then looked back to the bowl in front of Agatha. The contents had hardly shifted.

“Half,” she said quietly. “Eat half, and then we will return to the road.”

Agatha stared at her, eyes wide, as if the word itself had struck.

“Half?” she echoed, barely more than a breath.

“Half,” Rio repeated. “No more is required.”

Agatha’s gaze dropped back to the stew. For a heartbeat, rebellion flickered in the set of her jaw, in the way her hand hovered over the spoon as if she were tempted to push the whole thing aside and flee from the table entirely. 

Then she reached for the spoon again.

She ate in small mouthfuls, each one swallowed as if it had to fight its way past a wall. The colour in her face shifted from furious flush to something more steady as the warmth took hold in her belly, but every line of her body stayed tense. The men at the far end of the table did an excellent job of pretending not to count with her.

By Rio’s reckoning, it took far too long to reach what might politely be called half. When Agatha finally let the spoon rest against the side of the bowl and did not lift it again, the girl’s hands had stopped their slight trembling and the dull, constant ache of emptiness pulsing through the bond had eased by the smallest degree.

It was not enough. Nowhere near enough to what Rio would consider filling. 

But it was more than

“May we leave now?” Agatha asked, voice thin but steady. She did not look up.

Rio studied the bowl another moment, then the set of the girl’s shoulders. This was, she suspected, as much as she would get from her in this room, on this day, with these eyes.

“Yes,” she said. “We may.”

She rose, drawing her gloves back on with unhurried precision. Chairs scraped as those at the far end of the table scrambled to their feet. Rio did not look their way.

“Open the door,” she told one of the guards.

He obeyed at once. Cool air slipped in from the corridor as the latch turned. Rio stepped out first, trusting Agatha would follow. The soft whisper of skirts behind her told her she had.

The innkeeper was waiting in the passage, wringing his hands, unsure whether to bow or flee. Rio slipped a small, weighty pouch from her belt and set it into his palm before he could decide.

“For the disruption,” she said.

He glanced down, eyes widening at the heft of it, then dropped into a bow so deep she thought his forehead might touch the floor. Words tumbled up and died there. She had no need of them.

“See to your house,” she added, already moving past. “We will trouble you no further.”

They crossed the common room in a ripple of silence, the crowd parting as before. The noise of the convoy outside—snorting horses, creak of leather, shouted orders—rose to meet them as they stepped back into the light.

Agatha climbed into the carriage without waiting for a hand this time. 

Rio took her place opposite as the door shut and the carriage lurched into motion once more. The silence that settled between them was not the one they had brought in with them.

But it would do, for now.

The carriage felt smaller on the way out of town.

The same benches, the same fine leather, the same steady sway of the wheels, yet the air seemed thicker, as if the walls remembered what had passed within them and were waiting for it to be named. Agatha sat with her hands folded tight in her lap, eyes fixed on the blur of fields beyond the glass. The stew sat heavy and unfamiliar in her middle. Her body felt steadier for it. Her mind did not.

Across from her, the queen had taken her usual place. Back straight, boots braced, gaze turned to her own window. To anyone glancing in, she would have looked as untouched by the halt as by the rattle of the road. Only the bond under Agatha’s ribs betrayed a faint undertow of something else.

Neither of them spoke.

The silence pressed. It was not the wary quiet from that morning, when their barbs had still had room to breathe. This one sat low and heavy, full of the inn, of a bowl, of a singular word—half—that still scraped at the inside of Agatha’s skull.

She could feel it there between them, thick as smoke. If she let it, it would drag her thoughts back over the scene piece by piece until she choked on it.

She cleared her throat instead.

“Does Your Majesty,” she began, then stopped herself, forced her voice into something lighter, “still hold to her promise about questions?”

The queen looked away from the window. One dark brow rose, slow and eloquent. It was not unkind, merely…considering. As if to say: This is how you wish to play it, then?

“Yes,” she said after a moment. “If you still wish to make use of it.”

Agatha lifted one shoulder, striving for nonchalance she did not feel. “You will weary of answering long before I weary of asking.”

A faint sound escaped the queen at that—something that might almost have been a huff of amusement if it had come from anyone else. The corner of her mouth shifted, not quite a smile.

“I begin to suspect you may be right,” she said. “I had thought the road might exhaust your curiosity. It seems I was mistaken.”

The words were light, almost teasing. They hung there as an offering: something safer to hold between them than the memory of a table and a watching room.

“Not in this, at least,” Agatha said. “There is very little that can exhaust my curiosity. Though I admit…” She let her fingers smooth an invisible crease from her skirt, eyes on the motion rather than the woman across from her. “This question is not entirely about you.”

“Oh?” The queen’s gaze sharpened. Not cold; interested. “That would be a first.”

“It concerns your lands.” Agatha flicked her eyes up, gauging the reaction.

The queen’s posture did not change, but Agatha felt the shift all the same. A slight straightening. A glint of something in her eyes that might have been pride, or wariness, or both.

“I will indulge that as well,” she said. “For a time.”

The carriage rocked gently on a rut; the rhythm of the wheels settled back into its steady chant. Outside, the road had opened again into rough country, fields and hedgerows blurring into a dull green-grey under the lowering sky.

Agatha drew a breath.

“I have heard people speak of you,” she said, “as the Queen of the Riverlands. They use it as if it were your realm’s true name. I wondered if that were so.”

The queen regarded her for a heartbeat, as though weighing what lay behind the question. “You doubt it,” she said, not unkindly.

Agatha shifted. “The Great River  flows through every kingdom and queendom under the sun,” she said. “It belongs to no one. It seemed…odd that your lands alone should be named for it.”

Her gaze slipped past Agatha’s shoulder to the narrow slice of countryside visible through the window. For a moment her expression went distant, as if she were looking at something the girl could not see.

“You are correct,” she said at last. “It is not our true name. ‘Riverlands’ is a convenience. A word other courts find easy to fix in their mouths. My own people do not use it amongst themselves.”

Agatha’s fingers tightened, just a little. “Then what do you call it?”

“In our tongue,” the queen said, and the cadence of her words shifted, a different music threading under them, “it is called Nacerrio.”

The sound sat in the air between them for a moment. Agatha tasted it in her mind, turning the syllables over. It flowed like water over stone. Softer than the clipped titles she had heard bandied about in court.

“Nacerrio,” she repeated under her breath, careful with it.

Something in the queen’s face eased, almost imperceptibly, at the way she shaped the word. A muscle along her jaw unwound. 

“So the name for your lands is not Riverlands at all,” Agatha said. “It is only what others have chosen to call them.”

“Yes.” The queen’s mouth tightened into something that might have been a smile and might have been a baring of teeth. “When the courts beyond our borders speak of us, they think first of the River. Of the trade it brings, the power it carries. They see the crest and forget we were a people before we were a passage for their barges.”

She looked back at Agatha. “It is easier for them to speak as though the River itself were our only feature. As if we are nothing without it.”

Agatha studied her. “And you disagree.”

“I know otherwise,” the queen said simply.

Agatha hesitated, then ventured, “Yet you do not deny that the River belongs to all. It does not end at your borders.”

“No.” The queen’s gaze dropped briefly to her gloved hands, folded now atop one another. “It does not end with us. It begins with us.”

Agatha blinked.

“The Source,” the queen said. “The place where the River breaks from the earth. It lies in Nacerrio. The first spring. The first stone. From there it runs outward, to all other lands.”

Agatha had thought her mind too crowded and sore for surprise. She had been wrong. The words struck through the fog like a thrown stone.

“All this time,” she said slowly, “I wondered where the River’s home might be. No one could say. No book I was allowed to touch could say.” She huffed something that was not quite a laugh. “I begin to see why.”

The queen’s head tilted. “You did not know.”

“No.” Agatha’s lips twisted. “My mother preferred I keep to softer subjects. Needlework. Music. The proper tilt of a lady’s head when she is being told what she already knows. Geography was not considered…essential. But still in all the books I snuck into my chambers, I have never found the answer.”

She let her gaze slip past the queen to the faint gleam of water in the distance, a silver thread cutting through the fields. 

“Many courts would rather pretend ignorance,” the queen spoke. “To say the River has no home, so they do not have to think on the fact that its heart lies beyond their reach. They can protest a thing without naming it. It is safer that way.”

The faintest thread of steel ran under the last words. Agatha caught it.

“They do not like that you hold the Source,” she said quietly.

“They do not like,” the queen replied, “that the Source will not be moved, no matter how they grumble at their maps.”

A beat of silence. The road rolled on beneath them, the carriage swaying in its old familiar rhythm.

Agatha turned the word over again in her mind. Nacerrio. The way it had sat on the queen’s tongue. The shape of it, when she pulled it apart.

Nacer. Rio.

Her thoughts caught.

She looked more closely at the woman opposite her. At the way the light from the window traced the line of her cheek, the shadowed hollows beneath her eyes.

“Nacerrio,” she said slowly. “Your lands share their name with you. Or you with them.”

The queen’s shoulders went very still. Her hands did not move. Only a small shift of her eyes betrayed that the words had struck home.

“It is not a coincidence,” she said.

Agatha’s curiosity, sore and weary as it was, pricked up its head. “No,” she agreed. “I did not think so.”

She watched the queen a moment longer, the intensity of her gaze disguised, she hoped, by the half-light of the carriage. “There is a meaning to it, then.”

“A meaning,” the queen echoed. Her jaw worked once, as if around words she did not care to taste. “Yes.”

Agatha waited.

After a moment, the queen drew a slow breath. “The child born to the crown does not carry the River’s name,” she said. “Not at first. That name is…given, when she stands before the Source and swears herself to it. Each queen of my line takes it in turn. It is a vow. A way of binding oneself to the River and the realm.”

She paused, then added, almost under her breath, “And of letting the River unmake what came before, if it chooses.”

Agatha’s pulse stumbled.

“So you were not born with this name,” she said.

It was not truly a question. The bond thrummed faintly, uneasy.

“No.” The answer came clipped, without ornament.

For a heartbeat, Agatha thought that was all she would offer. That the subject would be closed with that single syllable, as cleanly as a door shut against a draught. Then the queen’s gaze flicked past her shoulder to the window again, to the distant line of the River, and something in her face tightened.

“I bore another name once,” she said. “It belonged to a different life. To a girl who did not sit where I sit now.”

Her gloved thumb dragged once, almost unconsciously, along the edge of her other hand. “There is no use in it any longer. The River has no need of that name, and neither do I.”

The words were flat. Not careless, exactly, but stripped of anything that might have been softness. They landed in Agatha’s chest like a stone.

She had the sudden, absurd urge to ask what the name had been. To haul it back into the light from whatever deep place the queen had buried it, to prove it still existed somewhere. Her tongue pushed against the back of her teeth with the effort of not speaking.

She remembered, rather vividly, the way the queen had looked at her in the inn when she had said half and then said nothing more. The line she had chosen to draw, and the way she had not forced her past it.

The subject of the queen’s name lay there between them now, raw in its own way. She could feel it in the way the bond had gone tighter, thinner. Another line. Another place it would be unwise to press.

“I see,” she said instead, quietly.

The queen’s gaze returned to her. For the first time, it felt odd to think of her as only the queen and nothing else. Nacerrio. Rio. A girl with a buried name, swallowed by a river and a vow.

“You may call me Rio,” she said, after a moment. “If you wish.”

Agatha’s fingers curled against her skirts. The offer sat there, simple and impossible.

“Your Majesty will suffice,” she said, because the thought of letting that name pass her lips felt too intimate, too much like stepping across a threshold she was not ready to admit existed.

Something flickered in Rio’s eyes at that—an unreadable shadow, there and gone. She inclined her head a fraction, as if accepting a truth she had expected all along.

“As you prefer,” she said.

The carriage fell quiet again. Not quite the same quiet as before. The air still held the taste of the inn, of stew and shame. But now there was something else layered over it. The echo of a true name, and the ghost of another, never spoken.

Agatha turned her face back to the window. Fields slid by, and beyond them the faint glimmer of the River, curling away towards parts of her kingdom she had never seen. In her mind, unbidden, a singular word rose again.

Rio.

It sat strangely in her thoughts—lighter than “Her Majesty,” heavier than she would have liked. She did not speak it aloud. Not yet.

But as the carriage rolled on and the light began to thin towards evening, she found that when her mind reached for the shape of the woman across from her, it no longer reached first for the queen.

It reached for Rio instead.

 

Chapter End Notes

I have had this chapter mostly written for a while time now but have been reluctant to post it. Considering the subject matter I wanted to ensure it was handled with care and patience. I hope that comes across while reading.

Also Nacerrio is just a made up word by me using google translate so please bear with me. I was going for something that represents the birthplace of the River. So really it’s just the Spanish verb to be born Nacer combined with Rio’s name which means river. Symbolically I hope you get what I’m trying to get at.

I really hope you enjoyed and I look forward to hearing your thoughts in the comments.

Chapter 10: The Journey Pt 3: Things Not Spoken Of

Chapter Notes

Cw for depictions of food anxiety and disordered/restrictive eating
anxiety / panic symptoms
references to past violence/trauma/ptsd (non-graphic)
And this chapter I feel really touches on power imbalance and coerced circumstances (nothing sexual).

I hope you enjoy and read safely.

See the end of the chapter for more notes

The day continued to unfold around them, hour after slow hour, as the horses pulled them farther and farther from everything Agatha once knew.

 

They sat in silence for the most part.

 

Agatha kept her gaze turned to the window, watching as the world outside shifted shape. Fields rose and fell on either side of the road, littered with crop and livestock. Cows lifted heavy heads as the carriage rattled past. A cluster of sheep turned as one, little faces blinking, then went back to the serious business of grazing.

 

She tried to let the changing scenery fill her. It did not quite manage it. There was too much else already taking up room.

 

The stew sat in her middle. It felt wrong. Not quite pain, not quite comfort. Just weight. Proof.

 

A reminder that she had obeyed. That she had lifted spoon after spoon because a queen had asked of it and then watched her. Because there had been no choice in that room that would not have ended in humiliation.

 

Her nails dug into the flesh of her thighs.

 

Across from her, the queen continued to sit with the same perfect posture and precise stillness as before. She was reading through scrolls and letters without word of commentary though something told Agatha her mind was not solely on the words before her.

 

She thought about what the queen had said. The story of her lands, the admission of a name bestowed and another long buried. “You may call me Rio, if you wish.” The queen had said. Agatha did not particularly wish to do that. Names were powerful things. They transformed a concept into something real. Agatha was not sure if she was ready to accept the reality surrounding the queen. She was no longer simply an idea built of whisperings and legends. She was someone who sat before Agatha and defied all preconceptions again and again.

 

She had no idea what to make of that. She had no idea what she could trust. When she could trust. Every moment she anticipated the moment when the queen would tear off the mask. When the cruelty would come spewing out and Agatha would be left with nothing but shattered hope.

 

So she told herself she would not say it. Would not speak it aloud. It would hurt less that way when the queen revealed herself to be like every other person who held power over Agatha’s life.

 

Perhaps though she could think it. Perhaps she could allow herself to hope in the safety of her mind. No one could hear her be foolish there.

 

Rio.

 

She turned the word around in her mind with purpose and hated how it felt right. Too right. She pushed it out of her mind. The queen would do for now.

 

Her attention was snagged suddenly by the prickling sense of being watched. She glanced across and met the gaze head on. The queen did not immediately look away. She regarded Agatha with a kind of quiet intensity, as if weighing something, or as if searching her face for an answer to a question she had never voiced.

 

Agatha forced herself not to shift under it. She held the stare until, at last, the queen blinked and turned back to her papers without a word, as though nothing at all had passed between them.

 

She herself could not say what had just passed between them, but when they eventually stopped again in the thin light of mid-afternoon, she could not deny that she welcomed the brief respite.

 

The horn sounded, a low, carrying note that shivered through the wood and leather of the carriage.

 

Across from her, the queen did not startle. She simply gathered her scrolls into a neat stack, set them aside, and smoothed an invisible crease from her cloak. The motion was unhurried, precise. As though nothing in this world had ever truly knocked her off balance.

 

It was… unnerving.  How largely unreadable she was. Agatha prided herself in her ability to read a room. To assess the smallest expressions of those before her. It is what kept her safest. It gifted her a sense of control where there was otherwise none. With the queen there was nothing.  Nothing she could understand at least.

 

The carriage jolted as it rolled to a halt. The door swung open a beat later. Cool air pushed in, edged with the smell of damp earth and horse. The queen rose, ducked her head, and stepped down without hesitation.

 

A hand appeared in the doorway, gloved, steady.

 

“Your Grace,” she said, as she had before.

 

Agatha hesitated for a fraction. She wondered to herself if the woman in front of her was truly so staunchly well mannered or if she were more motivated by optics and the audience around her.

 

She placed her hand in the queen’s regardless.

 

The grip was firm but not crushing. Agatha barely had to jump; the strength in the arm that guided her down did most of the work.

 

The stop, this time, was not at a bustling inn but at a smaller crown house. It stood as little more than a fortified holding on the side of the road. It had the air of a place meant more for function than comfort.

 

The horses were unhitched, led away to water and nose into feed. Men stretched stiff limbs, rolled shoulders, rubbed at the backs of their necks. Someone laughed at something; someone else cursed softly at a stone in his boot. The air filled with small, human noises of a journey broken.

 

Agatha stepped carefully along the packed earth and felt her own bones sing with relief at being freed from the carriage’s constant sway. Her legs ached with the dull, deep tiredness that came from hours of sitting rigid. She was grateful—fiercely so—that there was no table laid here, no smell of stewed meat drifting out through the door.

 

At least for the moment, there was nothing to swallow except air.

 

“Here,” came the queen’s voice.

 

Agatha turned. A leather water-skin was held out to her. Dark, worn, its sides beaded faintly with condensation.

 

“I am quite—” she began, the refusal on instinct, but her tongue stuck slightly to the roof of her mouth. Her throat felt tight and sore from disuse. The earlier stew still sat stubbornly in her belly, but the rest of her was parched.

 

She closed her mouth on the lie.

 

“Thank you,” she said instead. The words came a little stiff.

 

Their fingers brushed as she took the skin. The contact was brief, nothing more than the necessary transfer of an object.  Still something moved under her skin at the point of touch, the same nameless awareness that had prickled when she caught the queen’s gaze in the carriage.

 

Her hand stilled for a heartbeat on the leather. The queen’s did as well. Neither of them looked at the other.

 

Agatha lowered her eyes, adjusting her grip. By the time she glanced up again, the queen had already turned away to speak to one of the men from her entourage.

 

Agatha lifted the skin and drank. The water was cool, earthy from the vessel. It hit her stomach and spread out like a thin shield. Not quite the fullness food brought, but something. Enough, perhaps, to keep her traitorous body from announcing itself again with any more sounds.

 

She could live with water. Water had always been safer.

 

Around them, the bustle continued. Men unlatched harness, checked straps, conferred in low tones about wheels and axles and the stretch of road ahead. A stable boy led one of the horses past at a trot, the beast’s flanks darkened with sweat.

 

From the open doorway of the crown house, a girl emerged. She was younger than Agatha by several years at least, certainly not yet past girlhood. Her apron was too big, tied twice around a narrow waist. She carried a covered basket in both hands, knuckles pale with the effort of its weight.

 

She paused on the threshold as she saw the queen, eyes going wide. For a moment she hovered there, as if unsure whether to advance, then seemed to force her feet forward.

 

“Your Majesty,” she said, voice wobbling just enough to betray her nerves. She dropped into an awkward curtsy, the basket dipping with her. “From the house. For your journey.”

 

The queen turned from her conversation with the guard. The severity that had been there when speaking of routes and timings softened by a degree so slight Agatha might have missed it if she had not been watching as closely as she always did.

 

“Thank you,” the queen said, and there was a gentler edge to the words than Agatha had ever seen her spare for the grown men around her. “You have my gratitude.”

 

Colour bloomed high on the girl’s cheeks. She straightened, almost tripping over her own feet in her haste to offer the basket properly.

 

The queen took it from her with care, as though it were something fragile rather than merely a parcel of food. She did not simply pass it off at once, did not bark orders or ignore the giver. She inclined her head a fraction, as if the girl were some minor noblewoman rather than a servant’s child.

 

The girl fled back toward the house the moment she was released, pressing her hands over her mouth the way one did when trying not to grin inappropriately. Agatha’s chest gave a small, unwilling ache at the sight. It was merely unsettling to see anyone look pleased because of the queen.

 

The lid of the basket had shifted just enough to let a hint of its contents escape: the smell of fresh bread, the faint sweetness of dried fruit, perhaps a wedge of cheese wrapped in cloth. Her stomach curled in on itself at once, at the thought of having to endure another shared meal with eyes surrounding her.

 

The queen glanced down into it, reading its weight the way she read everything else, then lifted her head and called one of the men over with a flick of her fingers.

 

“See this stowed in the carriage,” she said. “We will attend to it later.”

 

There was no suggestion they should sit here in the yard and eat now. No summons to the men to gather, no invitation to Agatha to take something.

 

Agatha watched the basket carried away and felt the knot in her throat loosen by the smallest measure. Relief slid through her like a sigh. She had been waiting for the command, the expectant looks, the way every eye would turn to see if she would play her part without faltering this time.

 

It had not come.

 

She tilted the water-skin again, letting another mouthful slip past her lips. She kept her gaze fixed on the line of trees bordering the road, on the sky above them already beginning its slow drift toward afternoon gold. Anything but the retreating back of the man with the basket, anything but the memory of that stew and the queen’s eyes on her spoon.

 

After a little while, the horn sounded again. Men began to move with purpose, climbing back to their posts, settling reins between their fingers. The horses were hitched anew, snorting, stamping, ready to pull on.

 

The queen appeared at Agatha’s side as quietly as if she had stepped out of the air.

 

She wordlessly extended her hand once more, an unspoken expectation in the simple, steady line of it.

Agatha’s gaze flicked from the gloved fingers to the woman’s face. “I am capable of ascending and descending without aid, you know.”

 

“I am aware,” the queen said. There was no mockery in it, no indulgence either. Just that calm, even tone. Her hand did not lower.

 

For a heartbeat neither moved. It became a small, ridiculous standoff in the dust of the yard: Agatha refusing to be coddled, the queen refusing to withdraw what she had offered.

 

Agatha’s jaw tightened. she reminded herself to refuse outright would be to make a spectacle of herself.

 

She narrowed her eyes, chose her loss, and set her fingers in the queen’s palm without further comment.

 

As she settled back onto her seat, the queen followed, taking up her place opposite, the scrolls and letters returning to her lap as though the small interlude outside had never happened.

 

The door closed. The world outside narrowed to a frame of glass and wood again. The carriage lurched forward, wheels finding their rhythm on the rutted road.

 

Agatha turned her face back to the window, to the fields and trees and houses she had never bore witness to before. The light had shifted while they were stopped; the gold of late afternoon had begun its slow lean toward evening, stretching shadows long over the furrows.

 

She could feel the woman across from her without looking. The same straight spine. The same deliberate stillness. The air between them prickled faintly, as if the small, ridiculous standoff in the yard had left a trace.

 

Her gaze flickered then between the queen and the window.

 

The queen spoke suddenly without looking up.

“You wish to ask another question.” She spoke smoothly as she thumbed through pages of parchment in her hands. “You may.”

 

Agatha’s fingers tightened on the folds of her skirt. Was she truly that transparent? Why was it that the queen could seemingly see straight through her while the same could not be said the other way around?

 

“How can you possibly know that,” she muttered, more to herself than anything.

 

The queen’s mouth tilted, a bare suggestion of amusement. “You get a certain look in your eye and then grow suddenly restless each time you wish to ask something.”

 

Agatha bristled. “You have known me scarcely two days.”

 

“I learn quickly.” The queen raised her eyes at last, meeting Agatha’s stare head on. “Ask.”

 

It rankled, being read so easily. It rankled more that Agatha did, in fact, want to ask. The carriage swayed; the trees outside blurred together in a soft smear of green and brown.

 

“I have heard… stories,” she said at last. “Of your queendom.”

 

“Most people have,” the queen said. “They are not usually kind.”

 

“I would not view these as unkind,” Agatha answered. “These stories…they spoke of women in armour. Of ships captained by witches. Of councils where men were made to listen rather than command. Your land was held up as some manner of wonder.” She let her gaze flick, pointedly, to the memory of the men outside. “What I have seen so far seems… rather ordinary.”

 

The queen’s brows lifted by a breath. “You are disappointed,” she said.

 

“I am curious,” Agatha said, which was close enough to the truth. “I begin to suspect that the tales were little more than that. Tales.”

 

The queen regarded her for a moment. She did not speak. Did not frown or glower. She simply watched. And then, at last, she spoke.

 

“Our armies are as they say. Our river guard as well. If you stood on my border, you would find far more braids than beards in armour.”

 

“And yet you chose not to bring them,” Agatha said. “Not one of these women I am meant to be so impressed by. Only grey heads and ordinary swords.”

 

“Ordinary swords can still cut,” the queen said. “But no, I did not bring them.”

 

She set the parcel of letters aside entirely now, resting her forearms lightly on her knees. The motion brought her a fraction forward, enough that Agatha felt the need to draw back ever so slightly.

 

“Certain courts do not enjoy the sight of magic,” she went on. Your father’s court was known to be one, as I’m sure you are aware. I thought it a needless cruelty to my women to march them in and have them endure your court’s scrutiny. They already looked at me as though I had come to devour you all.”

 

Agatha thought of the court she had grown up in. The queen was not wrong in her assessment. She knew all too well how they felt towards things they could not control.

 

“And,” the queen added, voice flattening slightly, “I simply have more use for my best captains guarding my borders and my people than standing at my elbow for ceremony.  Our enemies do not rest simply because I have gone away.”

“You speak as though you yourself are in no danger at all,” Agatha said. “As though you could ride into any court in the realms with a few powerless  men and expect to leave again unscathed.”

 

She knew she was treading close to the edge. That the line between curiosity and insult had grown thin. Some part of her almost welcomed it. Let this be it, then. Let this be the moment the queen’s composure cracked and she showed something other than that infuriating, restrained calm.

 

The queen did not answer at once.

 

The air in the carriage seemed to tighten, as if the space had drawn one sharp breath and held it. The road hummed under the wheels; leather creaked. Across from her, the queen’s shoulders settled back against the seat in a way that might have passed for relaxation, until Agatha realised it was the stillness of something coiling in on itself rather than unwinding. For a heartbeat, the queen’s gaze was not quite on her but on some point beyond.

 

When that gaze focused on her again, it was like being caught in the draw of deep water. Something there had darkened, not with heat or temper, but with an old, cold certainty. It made Agatha shift slightly in her seat, and yet she could not make herself look away.

 

Her head tilted ever so slightly as she regarded Agatha. The faintest prickle ran over Agatha’s skin, the sense that the carriage had grown a little smaller, the air a little thicker, though nothing at all had changed.

 

“For better or worse,” the queen said at last, her voice quieter than before, “there are very few courts left that I must fear.”

 

The words were simple. There was no boast in them, no sharpness. That, somehow, made them worse.

 

“If my life hung on the strength of those men alone,” she went on, “I would indeed be a fool.”

 

Her gaze did not leave Agatha’s.

 

“It does not.”

 

There was a sense of danger now, though it did not feel aimed at her. It simply hung in the space between them, a reminder of something larger than the cramped walls of the carriage. It struck Agatha, with sudden clarity, that the stories surrounding the queen were perhaps not simply tales made to frighten children. Looking into those eyes now, she had the uneasy feeling that if anything, they had been told kindly.

 

A shiver loosened at the base of her spine, her pulse quickening under the weight of that steady look.

 

“And you ride into these courts you do not fear,” Agatha heard herself say, striving for dryness and almost managing it, “trusting in… what, if not them?”

 

The queen’s mouth tipped, not quite into a smile. There was a dry edge to it, something that brushed close to amusement without ever fully softening her expression, as if Agatha’s challenge impressed her more than it troubled her.

 

“In myself,” she said. “In the River. In things the world does not need to understand in order to feel.”

 

The words lingered in the air—thick and heavy.

 

And then it snapped. The queen’s gaze smoothed, the shadow in it folding back into the depths. Her shoulders remained very still. Her voice, when she spoke again, still had that soft even tone.

 

“Do not mistake courtesy for helplessness, Agatha,” she said. The use of her name landed like a stone dropped into still water. “It is always…unfortunate for those who do.”

 

It was not a threat. It did not need to be. It settled in the air all the same, a quiet reminder of what sat opposite her choosing, for now, to be gentle.

 

Agatha drew a breath she had not realised she had been holding. Her skin prickled, but it was not entirely from unease. Something in her chest had tightened and pulled, a strange, treacherous thrill threaded through the chill.

 

“I will endeavour to remember that,” she said, keeping her tone as cool as she could manage.

 

“See that you do,” the queen replied.

 

There was the faintest ghost of something at the corner of her mouth again—whether it was the echo of that earlier darkness or some sliver of wry approval, Agatha could not say. Whatever it was, it was gone almost as soon as she noticed it.

 

Outside, the road rolled on beneath them, the light thinning more and more toward evening. Inside, the carriage felt smaller than it had before, the space between them filled with the lingering sense of yet another thing she did not quite understand—and the low, persistent thrum of something she was not yet ready to name.

 

———-

 

The road unravelled beneath them, mile after mile, until the light outside thinned to that particular purple-blue that sat between day and night. The fields grew shadowed, the trees along the road standing like dark strokes against a paling sky. Lamps were lit along the carriage line one by one, little halos of yellow bobbing with each jolt of wheel and hoof.

 

Agatha’s eyes had begun to sting from staring too long at the blur beyond the glass when the driver’s shout cut through the clatter.

 

“Crown house ahead, Your Majesty,” he called back. “As arranged.”

 

The queen answered with a brief knock on the carriage roof rather than words. The pace eased. The sway changed—the subtle shift in motion that said they had left the open road for a more rutted, trodden path.

 

Agatha peered out.

 

This crown house was larger than the last, built of dark stone and creeping vines that clung to its outer walls. Iron gates ringed the yard, torches already burning in iron brackets by the entrance. The sky above it all was streaked with the last of the evening’s colour, a thin wash of rose and gold surrendering to indigo.

 

The carriage rolled under the arch and drew to a halt with a final shudder. The horn sounded the all clear, shorter and sharper this time. Men’s voices rose at once outside, the scrape of boots and the dull thump of trunks being shifted down from the wagons.

 

The door swung open. Cool air swept in, edged now with smoke from the torches and the faint promise of something cooking somewhere deeper within the house.

 

The queen descended first, as always. Her hand appeared a breath later, steady as it had been at every stop.

 

Agatha took it this time without comment. Her legs protested when she hit the ground; the ache from hours of sitting flared sharp before ebbing to a manageable throb. She took in her surroundings and it hit her, with a small jolt, that this would be the first bed she slept in beyond her father’s walls.

 

Around them, the rest of the party began to spill into the yard. Horses were led away to water. Packs and chests were unloaded in an orderly clatter. The house’s servants moved amongst them, nervous and brisk, taking orders from the queen’s officers and trying not to stare too openly.

 

“To be stopping already,” one of the men near the horses muttered, low but not quite low enough. “Barely past dusk. We have ridden half the night many times before now.”

 

“Aye,” another answered, loosening his saddle’s girth with practised fingers. “Road is clear. Weather good. Could have made another hour at least.”

 

“Her Majesty has grown soft all of a sudden,” a third said under his breath. “Or these eastern roads have made her timid.”

 

A fourth shushed him, casting a glance toward the queen’s back. “Mind your tongue. You know why we keep to daylight with royalty on the road.”

 

“Royalty we had last time,” the mutterer grumbled. “We still rode later than this.”

 

The words pricked at Agatha’s ears. She kept her face turned toward the main door of the crown house, but her shoulders tightened. She had not asked to stop. She had not asked for any of this. The idea that the whole great machine of the journey might be shifting its rhythms for her sake made her skin crawl.

 

The queen, a few paces ahead, paused just long enough that Agatha knew she had heard the grumbling as well. Her head turned a fraction toward the sound, though she did not deign to fully face them.

 

“If any man is dissatisfied with the pace,” she said, voice carrying easily across the yard without being raised, “he is free to remain on the road. Just know that my horses will not.”

 

A few of the younger men had the grace to look down at their boots. Someone swallowed audibly.

 

One of the older guards  stepped forward a fraction, his tone carefully measured. “Majesty, we meant no disrespect. We only wondered at the change. On previous campaigns we rode far into the night. We did not think—”

 

“On previous campaigns,” the queen said, “I rode alone.”

 

The words were not loud, but they cut through the yard all the same. The guard fell silent at once.

 

She turned her head a little more now, enough that there could be no mistake whom she was addressing, even if her eyes did not seek out any one face.

 

“Circumstances have changed,” she said. “Adjust accordingly.”

 

That was all. No raised voice. No threat. No room left for discussion.

 

She turned away without waiting for a reply, the matter evidently closed in her mind, and began to walk toward the steps leading up into the crown house.

 

The weight of the silence that followed was almost worse than the muttering had been. Agatha could feel it settle on her shoulders, heavy and unmistakable. No one said her name. No one needed to. They had all understood well enough what those “changed circumstances” meant.

 

She felt their gazes brush against her like rough cloth as she moved. The urge to hunch in on herself, to make herself smaller, rose sharp and familiar.

 

She did not. She lifted her chin instead, ignored the burn in her cheeks, and followed after the queen.

 

Better, she decided, to walk in the wake of the woman who had just offered to let hardened men march the cold night on their own two feet than to stand in the yard and wilt under their stares.

 

It should not have mattered, whether the early stop was for her or not. Yet as she crossed the threshold into the dim, torchlit hall, she could not help the flicker of thought that lodged under her ribs: that had the queen been riding alone, or with men who had made such journeys with her a dozen times over, they would likely still be on the road, and the dark would not trouble her.

 

Something about that sat uncomfortably in Agatha’s chest. It was not quite guilt. Not quite gratitude. Something thornier, tangled up with the memory of the queen’s earlier words and the echo of danger that had run beneath them.

 

They were shown into a smaller receiving room to await whatever arrangements had been prepared. The sounds of the yard faded to a muffled chorus through stone and wood. Lamps were lit. Shadows climbed the walls.

 

“Rooms have been made ready, Your Majesty,” one of the house’s stewards said, bowing low. “Supper can be brought when you wish.”

 

“Later,” the queen replied. “We would like to be shown to our chambers first.”

 

The steward bobbed again and backed out of the room.

 

Agatha stood just inside the door, hands clasped around themselves, unsure whether to move further in. The queen’s gaze brushed over her, brief and assessing, before she turned her attention to removing her gloves, finger by finger, as if shedding a second skin.

 

“You will have your own chamber,” she said, as though this were merely another practical detail to be noted. “You should rest. It will be an early start on the morrow.”

 

Agatha inclined her head. “As you say.”

 

Another servant appeared in the doorway then, bowing to the queen and gesturing for Agatha to follow. They led her up a narrow flight of stairs and along a short passage whose plastered walls were hung with faded tapestries. At the end of it, the steward opened a door onto a modest chamber: a bed with clean linens, a small hearth already kindled to chase off the creeping chill, a washing-stand, a chair, a narrow chest.

 

When the door closed behind her, the quiet was almost startling.

 

No men’s voices. No clatter of wheels. No sense of being under constant, careful scrutiny. Just the soft crackle of the fire and the hushed pull of her own breath.

 

She stood in the middle of the room for a moment, as though her body was still waiting for the weight between her shoulder blades and the feeling of eyes to return. It did not. Her spine uncurled by slow degrees.

 

There was a jug of water set by the basin, cool to the touch. She poured some into the bowl and splashed it over her face, over the back of her neck, letting the chill of it bring her properly back into her own skin. Her reflection in the small, warped mirror above the stand looked pale, eyes shadowed, mouth set too tightly.

 

She untied the fastening of her cloak and shrugged it off, then worked free of the heavier outer layers she had worn for the journey. A chest of her own clothes had been brought up to the room. Agatha sifted through it, selecting something lighter than the travelling attire she had donned all day.

 

Once she had changed, she sat on the edge of the bed and let herself breathe, slow and deep. The day’s conversations pressed in around her—the carriage, the various crown houses, the way the queen’s eyes had gone dark for that one, long heartbeat. The murmur of men in the yard. The way their complaints had folded in on themselves with a single, quiet sentence.

 

She lay back for a moment, just long enough to feel the mattress under her, strange and not hers, and closed her eyes.

 

A knock at the door pulled her up again.

 

“Come,” she called, smoothing her hair with instinctive hands before she remembered there was no one here to see the disarray but the servant on the other side.

 

The same steward as before opened the door a fraction and bowed from the threshold. “Your Grace. Her Majesty is seated for supper and enquires whether you will join her.”

 

Agatha’s chest tightened at once, as if a hand had pressed flat against it. The memory of their last meal together. The feeling of all those eyes on her, the sound of the mens whispers. It all rose sharp and unwelcome.

 

She swallowed. “Yes,” she said. “Of course.”

 

The steward dipped his head and withdrew.

 

Agatha remained where she was for a breath or two longer, her fingers curling around the coverlet. Then she rose, smoothed her gown again though it did not need it, and did her best to gather what remained of her composure around herself like another cloak.

 

The corridor seemed narrower on the way back down. Each step towards the lower halls made her pulse climb a little higher, her mind already bracing for the sight of a long board crowded with men, of herself at one end under the full weight of their regard.

 

The steward opened a door and stood aside for her to enter.

 

She stepped through—and stopped.

 

It was not the bustling hall she had imagined. No crowded table. No press of shoulders and voices. Instead, the room was smaller, more intimate: a side dining chamber, by the look of it, with a modest hearth and a single, long window now gone dark. A table had been set near the fire.

 

Set for two.

 

Candles burned in plain brass holders. Agatha’s gaze stayed locked on the two places set before her.

 

The queen was already seated at one of them.

 

Agatha lingered a moment on the threshold, uncertain. Her hands found each other at her middle, fingers lacing and unlacing.

 

“You are welcome to sit,” the queen said lightly, gesturing to the seat opposite her.

 

Agatha moved forward, the soft soles of her slippers whispering against the floor. She took the chair opposite, every muscle in her back aware of its own stiffness.

 

“I hope you do not mind,” the queen said, once she had settled. “I requested a smaller company this evening.”

 

Agatha blinked at her. “Mind?” The word came out thinner than she intended. She cleared her throat. “No. It is… quite all right.”

 

More than all right. Relief moved through her like a sudden, silent tide. For once, her tongue did not immediately supply some sly remark. There was only the dull astonishment that there would be no rows of councillors watching her raise a spoon to her mouth.

 

“Good,” the queen said, and there was a ghost of a real smile at the edge of her mouth. “My men have discovered a fondness for their own voices today. I would prefer to enjoy one meal in peace.”

 

Agatha hesitated. “That is all?” she asked, before she could stop herself.

 

The queen tilted her head, as if considering whether to be offended. “Must there be more?”

 

“I—no,” Agatha said quickly. “Of course not.”

 

Heat licked at Agatha’s cheeks for some strange reason. Before she could decide whether to continue sitting across from the woman before her or flee the room entirely,  the door opened again and servants entered bearing trays.

 

They set bowls and dishes between them with swift, practised movements: a simple soup, a loaf of crusty bread, a dish of stewed root vegetables, slices of cold meat, a small plate of cheese. Nothing overly rich, nothing extravagant. Just ordinary food.

 

When the servants withdrew, the room felt quieter still.

 

The queen did not immediately look at Agatha. She reached for the bread, tore off a piece, and dipped it into her soup with an ease that suggested she did not concern herself with who might be counting her mouthfuls.

 

Agatha stared at her own place for a heartbeat, at the steam curling from the surface of her bowl. Her stomach clenched in warning.

 

The queen did not say her name. Did not watch her. Her gaze stayed on her own plate, steady and unbothered.

 

Agatha picked up her spoon. Her hand trembled only a little. She took a small mouthful, more to test the air than the food. No one gasped. No one leaned forward to see whether she would swallow.

 

The queen sipped her soup as though she were alone.

 

Slowly, Agatha took another spoonful. Then another. The stew already sitting in her middle protested at the intrusion, but it was quieter than usual. She did not force more than a few bites down, but it was more than she had expected to manage.

 

For a time, they ate without speaking. The fire popped. The candles burned low and steady.

 

“You have asked me many questions since we met,” the queen said eventually, in the same tone one might use to observe that it had begun to rain. She reached for her cup, turning it briefly between her fingers before lifting it to her lips. “I have answered them as best I can.”

 

Agatha’s shoulders tensed, just a fraction. “Have I exhausted you already?”

 

“Not yet,” the queen said. “But I confess I was wondering whether you might grant me a turn.”

 

“A turn,” Agatha repeated.

 

“To ask,” the queen clarified. “A few questions of my own.”

 

Agatha blinked at her, thrown off balance. She covered it by tearing a small piece of bread and bringing it to her mouth, even if she only held it there for a moment before biting.

 

“You may,” she said at last, when it became clear the queen was waiting for an answer. “Within reason.”

 

“Within reason,” the queen echoed, with that faint thread of amusement. “Very well.”

 

She did not pounce. Instead, she took another measured mouthful of food, as if to demonstrate that nothing urgent hung upon the reply.

 

After a moment, she asked, lightly,  “In your father’s house, what was usually served to break your fast? On ordinary days.”

 

Agatha blinked again. Of all the questions she might have expected—the politics of the eastern court, her mother’s disposition, what she thought of the treaty—this was not among them.

 

“Ordinary days?” she repeated.

 

“Yes.” The queen’s gaze flicked up to meet hers for a second, then dipped back to her plate. “Not celebrations. Not feast-days. The mornings between.”

 

Agatha hesitated. “Bread,” she said slowly. “Cheese. Sometimes cured meat, if there was any left from the night before. Porridge in the colder months.”

 

“No sweet cakes? No fruits?”

 

“On feast-days,” Agatha said. “Or if my mother wished to make a show of generosity before guests.”

 

“And when given the choice?” The queen broke off another small piece of bread, almost idly. “Do you favour this sort of lay?” She nodded very slightly toward the table—plain, solid things. “Or the finer dishes your cooks used to set before you when they wished to impress?”

 

Agatha’s suspicion roused itself at last, curling around the edges of her uncertainty. “Do you wish to make a study of my tastes now, Your Majesty?”

 

“I make a study of much,” the queen said. “You need not answer, if you would rather not.”

 

Agatha toyed with her spoon, tracing the edge of the bowl. She thought of thick sauces and rich meats that lay like stones in her stomach, of the quiet relief of being left with a crust of bread and a sliver of cheese when no one was looking.
