“I prefer simpler things,” she said, more brusquely than she intended. “Heavier food sits ill with me.”

 

“Mm.” The queen nodded, as though filing the information away. “And fruit? Do you eat much of it?”

 

“When it is ripe,” Agatha said. “Not when my father insists upon displaying half-spoiled exotics at his table because they arrived out of season and he will not admit they were a poor bargain.”

 

The corner of the queen’s mouth twitched. “A crime, to abuse fruit in such a way.”

 

Agatha found herself huffing a quiet, reluctant breath that might almost have been a laugh.

 

The questions continued in that vein. What did she drink in the mornings—tea, watered wine, small beer? Had she ever taken her meals in the gardens rather than the hall? Did she prefer her bread soft or with a thick crust? Had she a fondness for any particular herb or spice?

 

They were harmless, on the surface. Domestic. The sort of thing one might ask to fill a lull in conversation. And yet, underneath, Agatha could feel the pattern of them, like fingers feeling out the edge of a wound without pressing directly on it.

 

The crown house was not mentioned. Nor the way she had sat frozen over her bowl there, or the sounds her stomach had made, or the queen’s sharp command to stop the carriage. The word hungry did not pass either of their lips.

 

They talked around it.

 

Just as they did not speak of the bands on their fingers, or the bond that hummed under their skin, or the fact that they were married now for reasons that had little to do with choice. Just as they did not speak of the queen’s names, or the way the air seemed to tighten when she grew truly still.

 

So many things sat between them, unsaid, filling the spaces their words merely skimmed.

 

By the time the plates were cleared, Agatha had eaten more than she would have believed possible at the start of the evening—small bites, scattered over the course of talk, never forced.


The queen did not comment on how much Agatha ate or did not eat.

 

She only wiped her fingers on her napkin, as though this had been any other meal.

 

“You should sleep,” she said, when the last of the dishes had gone and the fire had burned lower. “We will ride with first light.”

 

Agatha rose when she did. For a moment, under the wavering candlelight, the room felt very small, holding too much between its four walls.

 

“Good night, Your Majesty,” she said.

 

“Good night, Agatha,” the queen replied.

 

The use of her name again did something odd to her chest.

 

She let the steward lead her back through the corridors to her own little chamber, and when the door closed behind her this time, the quiet did not feel quite so empty as it had before.

 

———-

 

She had gone through her usual routine: She undressed in slow, mechanical motions. The gown slipped from her shoulders; she folded it with more care than required and set it aside. She let down her hair, fingers working pins free until the weight of it fell in a dark curtain down her back.

 

At home, the sounds of the castle would have been all around her even at this hour: distant laughter from the lower halls, the muffled clatter of a servant clearing evidence of the day, the wind rattling at familiar shutters. Here there was only the soft, uncertain crackle of the fire and the faint murmur of voices far below, too distant to make out any words.

 

She washed as best she could. Cool water against her face, her throat, her wrists. As if she could scrub off the day—the staring men, the stew, the queen’s eyes on her across too-small spaces.

 

When there was nothing left to busy her hands with, she blew out the candle and climbed into the bed.

 

The mattress dipped beneath her weight. The sheets smelled faintly of soap and smoke, not the familiar scent of the pillows back home. The ceiling above her was a different pattern of beams and plaster than the one she had stared at on sleepless nights for years.

 

She lay on her back and stared into the dark until her eyes began to pick out shapes—the line of the rafters, the curved outline of the chair, the faint glow from the fire painting the wall.

 

Jen would have sat on that chair, at home, if Agatha had asked her to stay. She would have swung her legs, chattered about something foolish until Agatha’s mind had eased. She would have made vile jokes about queens and treaties and councils until Agatha had snorted despite herself.

 

Jen was not here. Jen was back in a castle Agatha had left behind with a handful of hurried words and Agatha had no idea when she would ever see her again. If she would see her again. It was one thing to agree to write to one another. Even with the queens offer to deliver those letters through her personal couriers. It was another thing entirely to wonder if you will ever see the one person in your life that made you feel safe ever again.

 

Her throat tightened. She swallowed against it, hard.

 

She rolled onto her side, curling in on herself, knees drawing up toward her chest. The sheets rustled softly.

 

There was nowhere to hide here. At home, she had known the paths, the alcoves, the little corridors where she could disappear when everything grew too bright and loud. Here, there were only crown houses and carriages and rooms someone else had chosen for her. Only strangers. Only the queen, with her unreadable face and her too-seeing eyes.

 

She felt flayed open, somehow. As if the quiet, shameful parts of her she had kept pressed down and out of sight were now lying raw on the table between them. Embarrassment burned through her, hot and sour. Of the way her stomach had announced itself. Of the way the queen had stopped the entire convoy because of it. Of the way the men had muttered in the yard. Of the way every bite since then had felt like a test she could not afford to fail.

 

Tears prickled, sharp and insistent. She pressed her face into the pillow, as if she could press them back where they had come from. Her shoulders shook once in protest.

 

It had all felt… manageable, before. As long as she had been in her own halls, she could tell herself she knew the game. That she was shrewd and careful enough to survive it. That she could read a room, tiptoe around tempers, wield a clever tongue like a blade. That she was, in some small corner, in control of herself.

 

Out here, on the road, under someone else’s command, that lie frayed.

 

She did not feel shrewd. She did not feel clever. She did not feel like the girl who could charm a councillor into forgetting what he had meant to say, or slip a remark under her mother’s guard and make it draw blood.

 

She felt young. And lost. And very, very far from anything that felt safe.

 

A small, broken sound escaped her before she could swallow it. She bit down on the edge of the pillow, clutching it hard enough that her fingers ached, and tried to choke the next noise back.

 

They came anyway.

 

Tears spilled over, hot and fast, soaking into the linen. Her breath hitched, too loud in her own ears. She turned her face further into the bedding to muffle it, terrified at the thought of anyone hearing—of a servant passing outside, of the queen in some room across the hall, all steel and composure, catching the sound of her new bride sobbing like a child in a crown house bed.

 

The more she tried to quiet herself, the harder it grew to draw breath properly. The old, familiar tightness closed around her lungs, that iron band she had known since she was a girl. She forced herself to loosen her grip on the pillow, to drag air in through her nose in small, shuddering pulls.

 

She thought of Jen’s hand on her shoulder, steadying. Not there. She thought of her father, whose concern began and ended at the borders of his own advantage. She thought of her mother, whose likely response to all of this would have been a curled lip and a cutting remark about how no one liked a woman who made a spectacle of her own weakness.

 

The tears came harder for that.

 

She cried for the bed that was not hers, and the castle she had left, and the friend she had lost hold of, and the future she could not see the shape of. For the new queen who was both more frightening and more careful than she had expected. For the feeling of being seen where she least wanted to be seen.

 

She cried because she had no idea what she was doing, really. Because all the sharp edges and clever barbs in the world could not change the fact that she was a girl in a strange house, wrapped in strange sheets, trying very hard not to drown.

 

She cried until she had no tears left to cry. Her sobs softened to small, uneven breaths. Her eyes burned; her head ached with the force of it. The pillow was damp beneath her cheek.

 

She uncurled by degrees, easing her grip on the linen, letting her hands fall open on the mattress. Exhaustion dragged at her, heavy as wet wool. Somewhere beyond her closed door, the noises of the household dimmed further as the night deepened.

 

Her thoughts slipped, scattered, tried to form themselves into worries again and failed. They grew slow and thick, like silt settling at the bottom of a river.

 

With her face still turned into the cooling hollow of the pillow, the last of her tears drying on her skin, Agatha drifted at last into a restless, troubled sleep.

 

———

 

Across the corridor, sleep did not come so easily.

 

Rio lay on her back in the dark, the ceiling above her just another unfamiliar pattern of beams and shadow. The fire in her hearth had burned low; what light remained was a faint, uneven glow along the stones, not enough to catch on much of anything.

 

Her eyes were open.

 

They had been open for some time.

 

She dragged a hand over her face, fingers pressing into her brow as though she might smooth the thoughts there flat if she just pushed hard enough. It did nothing. They kept circling, relentless and restless, like wolves circling prey.

 

She could feel the bond—faint, but insistent—tugging from across the hall, a tightness that did not belong wholly to her. It pricked at the edge of her awareness, asking, urging.

 

She did not answer it.

 

She knew well enough what that sharp ache meant. She knew why the girl’s chest would be heaving, why her throat would be tight.

 

It was not Rio’s place to cross the corridor and stand in that doorway, to question her, to offer some feeble, clumsy comfort.

 

She had placed her in this pain. Every choice Rio had made had led them here.

 

So she lay still. Allowed her to at least fall apart in dignity. She stayed there until she felt ache ebb into something that told her the girl had, at last, surrendered to sleep.

 

Only then did she allow herself a proper breath. It did not ease much.

 

She let her arm fall and stared up at the dark.

 

She had taken Agatha from everything she knew. From her halls. From a life of her own choosing. From her seemingly only friend who she had clung to until the last possible moment. She had watched that parting, the way their fingers had refused to let go until ceremony demanded it.

 

There was a sour knot low in her chest when she thought of it.

 

They had seemed close—closer than most noble daughters were permitted to be with anyone. There had been something in the way Agatha looked at the other woman, in the way the other woman looked back. Not merely familiarity. Not merely comfort. Something sharper, quieter.

 

Were they only friends? The thought slipped in unwelcome. Or did I take more than a kingdom from her when I placed that bid?

 

She had no right to the answer. She knew that. She could not stop the thought from repeating itself though, adding to the storm of thoughts that made her ill with guilt.

 

She shut her eyes against the ceiling. That brought no relief either.

 

Behind her eyelids, other things waited: flashes of red and black, the sick shine of blood under torchlight, hands reaching for her and falling short, mouths forming her name in curses and in pleas. The echo of a battlefield’s last breath. The way a temple sounded when prayers turned to screams.

 

Sleep, for her, had long since stopped being a refuge.

 

She could hunt exhaustion until her limbs shook and her eyes burned, but the moment she let herself slip toward that deeper place, the past stirred. Doors she had nailed shut rattled on their hinges. Faces she did not wish to see again lurked just beyond the next breath.

 

Though perhaps the deepest reason was the one she had buried. The one she could not face. The eyes she could not bear to see again.

 

Not tonight, she told herself, as she always did. She kept herself stubbornly at the surface, clinging to wakefulness the way a drowning woman might cling to a rock.

 

It was a habit now, as familiar as any ritual: lie down, close her eyes, feel the first slide toward that inner place, and pull herself back with a jolt. Repeat. Lie there and let the hours pass.

 

She exhaled slowly, forcing her lungs to obey. The breath scraped on the way out.

 

The girl’s face rose again in her mind—the way she had sat at the first crown house table, stiff and pale, spoon held like a weapon, eyes fixed on some point beyond everyone and everything. The way she had frozen rather than reach for the bread when it was set within reach. The way she had blinked back tears at the sight of a mere bowl of stew.

 

Rio had seen fear in people over food before. She had seen hunger. Had seen greed. This had not been any of those. It had been something else. Something quieter though no less cruel.

 

She had recognised the shape of it. Too well.

 

Rio pressed the heels of her hands into her eyes, hard enough to paint bursts of colour there.

 

She had not meant to embarrass her further by halting the convoy. But neither had she been able to sit there and pretend she had not heard it.

 

The private meal tonight had been a small correction. A quiet experiment. A room with only two chairs, no council, no commentary. Food that would not weigh like lead. Her gaze kept deliberately on her own plate.

 

It had helped. She had seen it—the slight easing of her shoulders, the way her hand shook less with each mouthful, the moment the tension around her eyes loosened by a degree.  Yet she had still only consumed a concerningly little amount.

 

It confirmed what she had already suspected. That this was not a passing quirk. That the crown house had not been simple one bad meal.

 

It was a pattern. An old one, by the look of it. Old enough that she had woven it into the bones of herself without even noticing.

 

Left untended, patterns like that could be as dangerous as any wound left to fester.

 

“I have taken a girl whose body fights her,” Rio murmured, hardly aware she had spoken aloud. “And have placed her in a new bloody arena.”

 

Because the bond between them had opened to a new battlefield. A thread tying them together in a way that would be deemed criminal in some courts and blasphemous in others. Not just woman to woman. Not just witch to queen. But Seen to Unseen. Spirit-leaning at that. Marked by things most high tables would rather pretend did not exist.

 

If that ever came to light—

 

If anyone learned that she, of all people, was bound to a girl like that—

 

It would not be her they came for first.

 

Agatha had not asked for any of it. She had stood there in that hall with surprise written plain on her face when the marks on their skin formed for one another. It was Rio who had understood, in that breath, the full extent of what had just happened. What it meant for her rule. For her realm. For the River.

 

For the girl whose life her hand had purchased.

 

She let her hand fall away from her eyes and stared up into the dark again, jaw clenched so tight it ached.

 

If the bond was discovered, if the wrong eyes learned the truth of what ran under their skin, Agatha would become a target from all sides. A symbol. An excuse. An instrument, to be used to break Rio with. Or a monster, if the  right people had their way.

 

Guilt gnawed along the edges of her thoughts, steady as tide against stone.

 

She had saved the girl from one sort of ruin only to set her down in the middle of another.

 

And still, when she thought of the way her own mother had spoken of her, revealing the darker shadows that haunted those halls; when she remembered that every road they passed was foreign to this girl despite them still being within the bounds of the kingdom she had grown up in but denied the right to see, she knew she would make the same choice again. She did not know what to do with that knowledge.

 

Rio made a low sound in her throat and flung an arm over her eyes, blocking out the faint glow of the hearth.

 

“Fool,” she told herself, not for the first time. “Soft-headed fool.”

 

It did not help. The thoughts did not quiet. They never did, not really.

 

Sleep stayed where it always was for her: just out of reach, on the far side of a door she no longer trusted herself to open.

The hours stretched. The fire sank lower. The night wore on, and still Rio lay there, eyes open to the dark, hand over her face, held together more by duty and spite than by any real belief that rest would come.
